O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by completing it, its end is reached, and by the explanations and declarations contained therein, it seems to me that I fulfil this for you with this treatise, which is a part of the surgical operation with my hands.
For the surgical operation is widespread in our region and in our time, deprived of everything, so that the knowledge of it, and its practice, is lost; and there remain only a few small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been misinterpreted and have led to error and hesitation, such that the understanding of it is clouded and its power and art are distant.
And it seems to me that I will revive that [the surgical operation] through the organization of this treatise, following the path of exposition, declaration, and summarization; and so that it will come with the forms of cautery tools and other instruments of the craft, either by adding to the explanation, or by preparation for it, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skilled craftsman is not found with his hands in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must previously be exercised in the science of anatomy, which encompasses the knowledge of the body's limbs and their forms, their connections and separations; and possess knowledge of the bones, nerves and tendons, their number, and their paths; and of the pulsing veins and quiet veins, and the locations of their origin.
And for that reason Hippocrates says that by name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have discussed regarding anatomy is not free from falling into error, by which men will die, as I have seen many such, for some are trained in this science and yet proceed without knowledge and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled physician attempt to lance a scrofulous abscess on a woman's neck, and cut some of the neck's arteries, so that much blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician attempt to remove a stone from a man who had reached old age, and the stone was large, and he went to the man and extracted the stone along with a piece of the bladder, so that the man died on the third day.
And I already, having fled to escape it, saw that because of the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient, I knew beforehand that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke in our land for surgery, and went to a dark-skinned man whose thigh was fractured; and through the physician's ignorance, he attempted to fix the fracture, over the wound, with splints and bandages with great force, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he unbound it according to his desires, and then he reapplied it, and then left it for several days and ordered that we unbind the ligature; and it remained like that until the thigh and the leg became abscessed, and he ended up perishing.
And I was called to him, and I proceeded to loosen the ligature, and tranquility was achieved, and thus he was relieved of his pains.
However, already corruption had taken hold in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption continued to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who opened a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated over several days, until the severity of it was magnified by the patient's condition.
Because the cancerous wound is purely of melancholic humor, therefore it should not be treated with complete cauterization, but rather the limb in which it occurs should be amputated entirely.
And therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the surgical procedure be divided into two parts: one is the procedure associated with healing, and the other is the work which poses risk in the most critical cases.
And I have already shown this throughout this book where the operation involves mistakes and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to avoid this and abandon it, so that bad words find no way to speak ill of you and disgrace you.
Therefore, take your tools with diligence and God's protection, and treat your patients with kindness and firmness.
And use the best methods, by God's grace, seeking health and praiseworthy success, and leave the terrible illnesses of difficult cure, and remove from your tools that which causes you fear, so that the reputation of your skill and your name is not lost: for greater is remaining glory, and more exalted in the world, and behind your sanctity.
Guilia already said in some of his teachings/instructions: Do not treat a serious illness, so that you are not called bad doctors.
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
The division of the book begins, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered from head to foot, regarding the forms of instruments and the tools of cauteries, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on cutting and piercing, and on cupping, and on poultices, and on the extraction of arrows and similar things, is entirely categorized and ordered by the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and on the treatment of twisting and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and regarding the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary for me to describe the benefits of this cautery and its harms, and in which constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the benefits and harms of cautery is long, verbose, scientifically subtle, and secretly hidden; and many learned people have spoken about it, and they have disagreed, and it suffices for me to take a little from their discourse, fearing to be too lengthy.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits every constitution universally, whether with or without matter, except for two constitutions, which are hot constitutions without matter.
In the hot and dry constitution with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is helpful in that case, and others say the opposite, that cautery is not appropriate in an illness caused by heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry illness be cured with something that is also hot and dry.
And he who says the opposite argues that cautery is beneficial in all hot and dry illnesses affecting the body, because when you compare the body and its humours to the constitution of fire, you find the human body to be cold.
And I say, based on this argument, that experience has shown me this to be true on several occasions.
However, this should not be taken by someone who has not already been experienced and practiced in the chapter on cautery, and who has not learned about the diversity of human constitutions and the nature of illnesses, their causes, their symptoms, and the time frame in which they occur.
But in other constitutions, there is no such concern, and especially in cold and moist illnesses, for all doctors have agreed upon this, and are not divided on the use of cautery in these cases.
And know, children, that the secret of curing with cautery by fire, and its efficacy, rests in contrast to cauterizing with a burning medicine, for fire is simple, its action only reaches the part cauterized, and does not harm other parts associated with it, except for minor damage.
But the action of burning medicine extends to those parts of the body located further away, and it easily causes difficult-to-heal illnesses and severe pain in the affected area.
However, fire, due to its inherent nature and goodness, does not cause this, but only superfluity.  And we already know this from experiment and from the long study of the art, regarding the truth of causes; therefore, I am excused from a lengthy explanation.  And if it were not that my book, on this topic,  conceals the secret of treating with fire, and its effect on the body, and the manner in which it expels diseases, with a philosophical, demonstrable discussion – that would be a matter suitable to your wisdom.
And know, children, that they disagree on the time when it is best to apply cautery, and they suggest the best time is spring.
And I say that cautery is appropriate at any time, because the harm from accidental timing is outweighed by the benefit derived from the cautery, especially if the cautery is for painful or necessary conditions, intense and urgent, which cannot tolerate delay because the feared harm is greater than the minor harm resulting from the timing.
And this does not apply to your wisdom, or children, this is only considered by the ignorant populace and foolish physicians, that is, that not every illness treated with cautery is a cure for all time, and they fail to consider this consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they suppose, for cautery is only a method of medicine that alters the complexion and dries the humidity which is the cause of pain.
But nevertheless, cautery is superior to other medical treatments because of its power, its effectiveness, and the greatness of its master.
And it is possible that the illness may return at some time, depending on the patient's constitution, the location of the illness, the patient's strength, and the accumulation of superfluities within the body, and what they have consumed in food and similar matters;  however, if the illness for which cautery is administered is a subtle ailment in a limb with few superfluities and humors, like toothache and similar conditions, it is possible that the pain will not return, but this is usually a short-term effect.
And it is a common saying that cautery is the last resort in medicine, and it is a true statement, not in the way they understand it, for they believe there is no cure that is as effective as cautery.
The truth is the opposite.  The idea that cautery is the last resort in medicine is only true when we have tried other methods of treatment for some illnesses, and those treatments are ineffective, and only then is cautery used as a last resort, and it can be effective in that situation. Therefore, the idea that cautery is the last resort in medicine is not as the common people and foolish doctors understand it.
The ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this without reason, due to the equal and superior nature of gold's substance.
And they said that the location for cautery does not matter, but this is not entirely true, for I have proven this and found that it only works on certain bodies, not others, and cautery with gold is indeed better than with iron as they said; however, when you use gold to cauterize with fire, it's not as straightforward as they describe, because it burns away in proportion to how much you want it to through the gold's hardness, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you add a flame to it, the gold melts with the heat, and some gold slips into the fire, so the craftsman loses it through this procedure.
Therefore, cautery with iron is done more easily and more accurately with respect to the correct execution of the procedure.
And I have organized this chapter on cautery according to sections, and have ordered them from head to feet, so that the inquirer can find what they want in it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization treats the moisture and cold affecting the brain, the causes of which are twofold: one is the cause of a cold, copious rheumatic flux, called a cold, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and copious sleep, and toothache and sore throat, and generally any illness arising from cold, such as paralysis, apoplexy, and similar ailments.
The method for this cauterization is this: first, you instruct the patient to take a laxative to cleanse the head for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and usual constitution.
And then instruct him to shave his head, and then place him on a smooth bench with your hands on his feet.
And then place the blade of your palm near the bridge of his nose between his eyes. However, when the middle finger has reached that spot, mark the spot with a probe, and then apply the olive cautery, the form of which is as follows:
Then place that [cautery] on the spot marked by the probe, so that it descends, while your hand is gradually tightened, and you rotate it.
Then gently remove your hand and observe the area.  If you see any significant portion of bone exposed, roughly the size of a grain of millet, then raise your hand; if not, return your hand with that iron, and with another of the same kind, if it is chilled, until you can see the bone as I have described to you.
Then take a small amount of salt and dissolve it over the area, and leave it for three days.
Then apply a cloth soaked in water and leave it there until the scab from the fire has been destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmacon unguent until it is healed.
And if the wound remains open, it is better to raise the arm, as this is more beneficial and helpful.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some part of the bone's tissue is burned.
And then whatever is burned to the bone should be scraped off, and then treated.
And others say that with the cautery they should reach the back [of the affected area], applying strong pressure until the bone breaks, and this is the method of minor cauterization: And they believe that from that place, the vapors of the head are escaping.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then treated until it is healed.
However, I have not seen these two types of cauterization in all cases, but only in some men, according to a fearful method;  and, in my view, better is healing, when possible; for the head is weakened by a rupture of its continuity, just as we have witnessed in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of frail nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and with that method and work, there is healing.
Second Chapter.
Of the cauterization of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain affects the entire head of a prolonged illness, and remedies such as leeches, cupping, head purges, plasters, compresses, poultices, and similar treatments have been used, and especially if the head has already been cauterized with a cautery that doesn't benefit from any of those remedies, then consider this: if the patient's head is strong in its nature, and isn't frail, but shows a significant amount of cold, then cauterize below the first cautery a little. And then cauterize that area above the patient's temple with a cautery until the skin is broken. Cut through the skin until the bone is exposed to the extent we have described, and cauterize that with a cautery on the back of the head in the area called the "vas" – which is the location where a cupping vessel is placed. And ease your hand in doing this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience very great pain, different from the pain of other cauterizations of the head.
And I will say this about the cautery in its place, for the cautery used to cauterize the temples and the back of the head should be a finer cautery than the one used for the middle of the head; and this is the way to do it.
Third Chapter.
Concerning the cauterization of non-chronic migraines.
When there is persistent pain in the head, or pain that extends to the eye, the head should be purged with medicaments, and the other treatments mentioned in my descriptions of illnesses should be administered.  If all else fails, cauterization in such cases can be done in two ways: either with a sharp, burning medicament, or with iron.
However, cauterization with a burning medicine is done by taking a clove of garlic, squeezing it, and scraping the ends of it from both ends. Then, you should cut the painful area on the temple with a sharp lancet, until a small pit is formed under the skin, so that it is concealed.
Then, apply pressure to it with a compress to constrict it, and leave it in place for three hours.  Then remove it, and take away the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days.  Then, apply a cotton pad soaked in vinegar, until the area is healed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines, which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron, according to this method: heat the cautery iron, for which this is the procedure:
and it is called "clavus clavillis," because the head of it is shaped like a nail, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small protrusion.
Then place it on the painful area, and hold your hand (so that the underlying artery is not harmed) and turn the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount adhered by the sharpness of the cautery is about half of it, then lift your hand so that the artery underneath isn't harmed, so that there isn't a flow of blood.
Then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; and then place cotton soaked in butter, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of the scalpel, according to cautery, and be careful not to cut an artery in this particular, not-ancient, procedure.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the cauterization of ancient migraines.
When you treat a migraine with what we have said about the previous treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes because the illness does not respond to what we have said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire, then you should heat the scalpel cautery until it is white-hot, then apply it to the painful area in a long line, about half the length of a finger, or a little less; and hold your hand steady until you cut the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful about the continuity of the jaw, which is strained by chewing, and that the ligaments and nerves that control it are not inflamed or cause spasms.
And if it is about guarding and being careful of the flow of blood from the severed artery, because in severing it there is fear, especially for someone who doesn't understand what they are doing and is not skilled and experienced; and leaves the operation incomplete at the beginning.
And I will come back to the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery according to its method, in its place in this book.
And if you see that this treatment is not effective for this illness and see the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize the affected area in the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it heals.
And if you wish, apply the cautery we mentioned in the chapter on artery constriction with a cautery having two blades; this is a better and more effective cauterization.
Chapter 5.
Of cauterization for ear pains.
When pain in the ear comes from cold and is treated with laxative remedies and other treatments as previously described, and the pain does not subside, then apply the cautery, specifically the one called 'puncta', of which this is the form:
Then, apply the heated cautery point near the ignition, and move it around the entire ear, in a circular motion, or on each affected spot if the pain is localized. Move the cautery along the ear's base, slowly, after marking the location with a probe.
And if the cautery makes ten points on the whole ear, then treat those areas until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
Of the cauterization of tumors.
Tumors which are cured by cauterization are only of the types that come from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that comes from dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of tumor with poultices, emetics, and other remedies, and your treatment is unsuccessful, then you must cauterize the patient with three cauteries: the first at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the diseased area, for softening only occurs in the healthy-appearing area.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize at the right side of the ear's extremity, a little above the temple bone; and another at the temple, the length of which should be equal to the length of the finger (or thumb), and hold the cautery until about half of the affected area is burned.
And this is the method of cauterization, and it is a type of surgery, the method of which is described beforehand. However, it is more subtle than what you have seen; and the knife should remove a little of the excess fat.
And then treat the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old, and you have treated it with what we have described, and your treatment is not effective, and the patient has no fever, then cauterize the patient with four cauteries: on each temple, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said, and with a cautery on the throat. And the method of cauterization is done according to what has been stated above.
And it has already been cauterized on the mouth of the stomach; and it is further back; and soon after it should be treated with what you proceed with.
Chapter 8.
Of cauterization for forgetfulness, done by fire.
Large jars and pills must be given to the patient at the beginning, to purify the brain.
And then shave the entire head of the patient and place a plaster of the kind described in the treatise on plasters on their forehead.
And apply it several times, for that is a way of cauterization. And do this according to the same order which I have already described.
And so, if this treatment is successful; and if not, then apply three cauteries to the back of their head, descending from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place the width of a finger between each cautery, and then treat the cautery with what has been previously described.
But if you desire more and the patient tolerates it, then apply a cautery to the middle part; and if you desire even more, then cauterize it above the two horns.
Then treat it until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped, according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the entire body.
You should proceed with the cleansing of the head with herbs and what we have said; and then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with cauteries on all the horns of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if in the disease of softening of the body, the patient is weak and the illness is severe, then cauterize the patient with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and continuously with a cautery until it is softened considerably, and remove your hand. And then treat him until he is healed, and this should be an olive oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary to treat his brain at the beginning with herbs and the other treatment we have described in the division when the illness is severe and can tolerate the exception of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate the medicines, then administer balms and masticatories that will treat the brain, beforehand for many days, with good dietary adjustments.
Then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the back of his head, and cauterize on each of his temples. And if the illness is severe and can tolerate it, then cauterize it with the cauteries I have mentioned, having simultaneously paralysis and softening of the body upon the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
Let the cautery be olive-oil based, as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a delicate cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholia.
When the causes of melancholia are corrupted humidity and thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries we have mentioned in the section on paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholia is a superfluity inclining towards melancholia, and the patient's body is moist, then give him to drink what purifies his brain, according to what has been stated previously in the division (or section).
And then shave the patient's head, and then apply a properly made cupping glass to the area of the scalp, and place it in the middle of the head.
And the patient should be seated upright, and held steady on all sides.
And then apply a liniment; and a good old sheep's wool is most suitable. And warm that over the fire with a moderate heat, which the patient can tolerate when it is placed on him. And then place that in the middle of the patient's head, within the cupping glass, and leave it until it cools.
And do this with the patient once a week, with the other treatment for him until he is healed; or if you want, cauterize that by making small punctures according to the patient's tolerance, but keep your hand with a cautery prepared in this way: this type of cautery moistens the brain evenly.
And then place that cloth soaked in broth or hen's grease.
Chapter 12.
On the cauterization of descending water in the ear.
When you have observed the beginning of water in the ear, based on the signs I described in the division, then begin and give the patient to drink something that will dry his head and protect it from all moisture.
And have him sweat in a bath for a few days.
Then command him to shave his head and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if it is the beginning of the spreading of water in both ears, or on one side if it is the beginning of water in one ear.
Cut with a cautery all the veins and arteries that are under the skin; and let the cauteries be at that length, in the width of the temples.
And beware of blood flows when it is cauterized; and if you see blood flows from him, stop them with any medicine that is possible for you.
And I will come with the knowledge that is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of those and caution about blood flows.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck, under two bones, with two cauteries placed directly upon them.
Chapter 13.
Of the cauterization of old tears.
When tears of the eyes are old, persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are apparent in the area of the head's exterior, and it is verified that this is excess of cold phlegmatic material, then cauterize that area with the cautery that we described in the chapter of heating water in the eyes,  and use a cautery in the middle of the head and two cauteries in the neck below the temples, and two cauteries in the neck below the bones. And if more treatment is needed, then cauterize that area along the entire side of the eye socket on the browbone/eyebrow area with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Of the cauterization of nasal foul odor.
If your treatment with the methods we've discussed in the previous section is ineffective, then prepare and give the patient decoctions to drink for three nights; then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium-sized cautery and an olive-oil-based cautery. And then cauterize the area with a clove-shaped cautery, using two cauteries, one small one above each eyebrow under the hair.
And be careful of the flame, so that you do not burn it.
And this is the shape of the clove-shaped cautery.
Chapter 15.
Of cauterization for softening the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery using this lunar cautery; and this is its shape:
And if you, cauterize that area above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries on every side, and let them extend away from the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the amount of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too hard with the cautery, but just enough that the third part of the eyelid is adhered.
Chapter 16.
Concerning cauterization of the eyelid when the hairs are turned inward and cause the eye to become inflamed.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward causing inflammation, cauterization in those cases is done in two ways: either with a burning iron or with a medicinal burn.
If with a cautery of fire, first instruct the patient to remove the hairs as one would shave them, until they are pulled out and cut; and if the inflammation is near the eyelashes, then you should bandage the eye to prevent movement until the hairs have grown back.
Then, once the hairs have grown back and are trimmed, place the patient's head on your lap, and carefully make a mark on the eyelid with a probe, marking it with a shape similar to a myrtle leaf, starting near the hairs.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or in a poultice of ptilli on the eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this form.
And then cauterize over the marked area, little by little, multiple times, until the surface of the inflamed area, which resembles a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
A sign of successful treatment is that you see the eyelid contracted and the hairs raised towards the eye's puncture point.
And then lift your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with a bandage over it until the scab has healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if any cause returns to the hairs' origin, and the eyelid is softened, then repeat the cauterization in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are beneath the eyelid, then cauterize them until they are raised to their natural position, and are smoothed so the hairs no longer irritate the eye.
But the cautery with a burning medicine, which is what you order the patient to do, is to let the hairs grow out and smooth them, and then a leaf-shaped piece of cloth is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And then take known herbs and living vinegar.  And mix those things well, and do this quickly so it doesn't get cold; and then spread this mixture onto the cloth shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place this over the affected eye or both eyes; and put a cloth soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head should be supported by you.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it slightly, but do not remove it until the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, for the burning sensation should be like that of fire.
And when the patient perceives the burning sensation, then remove the medicine and wash it away with your finger; then, when the burning subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.  Then consider if the eyelid has been raised as intended through the burning or incision, because if it is, then the procedure is complete; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the area not previously treated, and don't stop until the procedure is successful and the eye is healed.
And then apply a cloth over it until the burn's pain is gone, and then treat it with a healing ointment or another treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, in the course of your treatment, to carefully watch that nothing of the medicine gets into the eye.  And if, after a few days, the eyelid is softened and any hairs have fallen into the eye, then reapply your medicine to the softened part of the eyelid, just as you did at first, and continue until it is healed.
And this is the method using myrtle leaves.
And know that the eyes of men vary in size and smallness, and therefore your treatment is tailored accordingly.
And the path to recovery will not be hidden from the one who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Of the cauterization of fistulas that are in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula using the methods described in the divisions of diseases, and your treatment isn't successful, you must cauterize it in this way: instruct the patient to place their head on your lap, and have another attendant hold the patient's head between your hands, so it doesn't move or shake.
And then place a piece of cotton soaked in egg white or in a ptilli mucilage (plant based adhesive) on the patient's eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this form:
which is concave, like the shape of a reed or a hawk's feather, on the end where the cautery is made.
And if desired, it can be pointed at the other end. And if you want, it can be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and drain and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and remove the pus from it.
Then place the cautery, which is well-heated, upon it, and hold your hand a short distance from the cautery near the edge of the nostril, so that neither your hand nor the patient is disturbed, and then place the cautery on the edge of the nostril and cauterize it.
And so if at the beginning your cautery penetrates the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if needed; and leave it thus for three days, and then apply a bandage with a cloth and surround it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if more than ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier practitioners said:  Cut into the place of the fistula and make it open, then place into that fissure a thin strip of material of which its form is [described] (or: shape). And pour into this fissure a quantity of melted lead, hold your hand to steady it (and be sure it is applied correctly) so that the melted lead does not injure the opening; and you should place on the opening of the patient a bandage soaked in albumen (egg white) or water, because the lead adheres to the place of the fistula and heals it with a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed by what we have mentioned about the cautery and its treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a treatment through the nose and reduce the fistula to the nasal passage according to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
On the cauterization of the fissure (or split) of the pot (or possibly:  of the abscess).
Often fissures occur in the lips, especially in the lips of children.

And its (the cautery's) concavity should be according to the sharpness of the knife.
Then carefully place that heated cautery on those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
And then treat it with a plaster until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
Of cauterization of fistulas in the mouth.
When an abscess develops at the root of the uvula, or on the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, and it then forms a fistula, and it breaks open, and becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and you treat it, and it does not respond to treatment, then you must apply a cautery according to the size of the opening in the fistula, and then place that heated cautery into the opening of the fistula, and hold it until the hot iron reaches the bottom of it, and do this one or two times; and then treat it with whatever we have said about treatment until it is healed.
And therefore, if the pus is removed and the wound is healed, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to expose the area and remove the corrupted bone, as described in its corresponding chapter.
Chapter 20.
Of cauterization of the teeth and softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are decayed, and you treat them with medicines, but it does not help, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be appropriate, so that you position the cannula over the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, holding it a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as you wish.
And then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water and keep it in for an hour, and then spit it out, for the decayed tooth is sealed, the softened gums are strengthened, and the corrupted moisture is dried out.
Chapter 21.
Of the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is caused by cold, or there is cold or a worm inside, or you do not consider medical treatment appropriate, then cauterization in that case is done in two ways: either cauterization with cow dung or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with dung is done by taking cow dung, boiling it in an iron pot, and then taking a piece of cloth and wrapping it around the tip of a probe.  Then, dip that cloth in the boiled dung and quickly place it on the aching tooth until it cools down.
And then repeat this several times until the heat's effect reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, you can soak wool or cloth in the aforementioned dung and place it over the aching tooth, then place a heated iron on top of that until the iron reaches the point of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire involves taking a heated needle or iron, ensuring it has enough thickness so that the heat doesn't reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be appropriate, and place it on the tooth while holding it until the cautery cools.  Do this several times, for the pain will cease either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, through the pressure of the cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good dung and hold it for an hour, then spit it out; and this is the method of cauterization.
And cauterize any extremity you can, as is possible for you.
And this is the method of the cannula:

And then treat it with ointment, bandages, and dressings until it is healed.

Chapter 26.
Of cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and excessive moisture enter the stomach, disrupting its constitution and multiplying there, and if you continue treatment methods and do not succeed, then the patient should lie on their back, extend their arms and legs, and then cauterize it with three cauteries: one below the foot for the space of a finger with a wedge-shaped cautery, and two more below the sides of the first, until a triangular cautery shape is formed; and separate those that are not joined, to create a space/separation.

Chapter 27.
Of the cauterization of a cold/chilled liver.
When pain in the liver arises from cold, humidity, or excessive windiness, until its normal state is restored by the expulsion of excess, and the illness is treated as we have said and not otherwise, then the patient should be placed on their back, and mark three cauteries according to this shape and this same size, over the liver below the ribs, in the area where a person lies on their back.
And the space between each cautery should be the width of a finger, and the cauteries should be aligned with the length of the patient's right body.

Chapter 28.
Of the puncturing of a liver abscess with a cautery.
When there is an inflammation or abscess in the liver and you wish to know if that abscess is in the liver tissue itself or in its covering, then if it is in the liver tissue itself, the patient experiences considerable weight and sharp pain.
And if it is in the liver's covering, the pain is sharp and often exhausting for doctors to treat.
Therefore, the patient should be turned on their side; then, locate the abscess with a probe; and then apply the cautery, which is shaped like a pointed rod, the shape of which is shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the pus is consumed, until it reaches the cyst, until it bleeds out/scabs over.
And then treat that area as part of the treatment for the other abscesses until it is healed.
And only use this type of cautery if its use in the art of medicine is well-established, and many illnesses have been cured by it; however, sometimes, some physicians might be bold in applying it to this operation, but nevertheless, we leave that up to them, and it's better [to proceed more cautiously].
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancient authorities on the roots of astrology said that cauterization of cold pleurisy using this method is effective.  You should take a long, dry root from the roots of astrology—a long, pointed root, the thickness of a finger.
Then immerse it in oil and heat it in the fire, and then cauterize with a cautery at the place between the continuation of the collarbone (furcula) and the neck; and with two small cauteries, guiding them slightly downward towards the area under the chin; and with two cauteries on the area sloping downward between the 5th and 6th ribs, angling them slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the foot; and another on the stomach; and with three cauteries on the back areas; and one cautery in the place between the two shoulder blades; and with two cauteries on the two sides of the foot, positioned below the cautery between the shoulder blades.
And it is not necessary to hold your hand with the cautery, but rather it should be held (or operated) clearly with the cord/handle (or instrument).
And some ancient authorities say that some people use an iron cautery shaped like a ray, and heat it up, and place it on the area between the ribs, until they reach the abscess and it is drained of pus, as we have described in the case of a liver abscess.
But this cautery perforation is not without risk, for it quickly causes an accident at the fistula site where healing is not possible.
Chapter 30.
Of the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat spleen disease using the methods previously described for treatment of the spleen (or its division) and your treatment isn't effective, you can cauterize the area in three ways, all of which are appropriate:  one is to make three or four cauterizations in a line along the length of the spleen, following the shape of the cauterizations used for the liver, which have been described previously.
And let the distance between each cauterization be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of the cauterization be the same as the one we described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand onto the cauterizing instrument; and let the patient lie flat on their back.
And there is another way to cauterize, which is to ignite the cautery using two fluids, a method discussed in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and lift the rib cage located on the side of the spleen where the patient's left side rests.
And your lifting should be along the length of the body, so that the cauterizations are made across the width of the body.
Then, insert the two fluids, ignited strongly, into that space, so that with these fluids, you penetrate the ribs on the other side.
And then remove the cautery; and thus four cauteries have been made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery using three fluids.
And then you treat the cautery, once it has stopped emitting sparks for too many days, for it is more important than the other previous things in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
Of the cauterization of dropsy.
Cautery is not used in dropsy, but rather in a specific type of swelling.
When you treat the dropsy using the methods of treatment we have discussed in the division, and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary to cauterize with four cauteries around the navel, and with one cautery on the stomach, and another cautery on the liver, and another cautery on the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and another cautery on the stomach. And the depth of the cautery should be proportionate to the severity of the condition.
And then leave the cautery open so that it drains for a long time.
And do not evacuate the patient of the treatment near the cautery with what is needed, for by doing so, two intentions are added to it, and the healing will be hastened by the aid of sanation.
The shape of the cautery used to cauterize the belly should be wedge-shaped, similar to the previous one described, and the one used for the back should be olive-shaped, similar in form to the previous one.

Chapter 36.
Of the cauterization of fistulas that are made by nature in the diseased parts.
When nature does not obey the treatment we have described for the affected area, and the patient is alarmed by this, it should be treated with cauterization.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in a natural cavity, and it is old, with running sores and corrupted moisture, then the best course of action is to probe it with a thin probe, and then determine the depth of the fistula with the aforementioned probe.
Then heat the cautery iron, which should be similar to a ray. And then place that heated cautery iron into this fistula, according to the depth indicated by the probe.
And repeat the application of the cautery until those corrupted tissues are burned, once, or twice, or thrice, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn the nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or towards the intestine, then be careful of all those areas; and do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy area and you think it is not penetrating.
And then treat the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the matter has been removed from it, and it remains like this for a long time, then know that it is completely healed.
And if the matter has not been removed from it, then know that it is penetrating or in the vicinity of rotten flesh, or that it is caused by conditions that will return to that area.
