And the cautery for children should be subtle, according to their size and proportion.
And then treat the cauterized area for three days with a dressing until the scar from the fire is gone.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for 40 days until the area and the wound are sealed.
And you must regulate their diet according to the duration of their treatment, which should avoid straining the abdomen and prevent the intestines from protruding further during recovery.
And then, when approximately 40 days have passed, administer a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another 40 days.
And he should abstain from strenuous work, overeating, and excessive drinking, and loud noises, for if he follows this regimen of treatment, he will be fully healed.
And I will now describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the abdomen.  If, at the outset, you want to prevent them from protruding, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery, in proportion to its size, and make it as dry as a third part of itself.  Then treat it with what we have already said, and that is sufficient.
And some earlier practitioners might say that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, according to this shape, since it is identified with a probe.
And a line is drawn across it, on the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and you press down with a wedge-shaped cautery, but the first, simpler cautery is better.
Chapter 46.
Of the cauterization of twisting (or torsion).
When, in some limb, pain arises due to a blow or impact, and that pain persists for a long time, and you find no cure with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery should be applied to each limb according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, the location of the twisting, and the pain in it.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, good.  But if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is the custom of those pains to be punctured from one limb to another limb near it, so it is necessary to apply cautery repeatedly until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of cauterization for leprosy.
Some forms of leprosy are greatly helped by cautery, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize this, consider if the leprosy is superficial and can be cured with what we have said in the division, and not this one.  If you fear the corruption might spread throughout the patient's body and constitution, then cauterize on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, another below that, between the forehead and the hairline; and two on the temples/ear regions; and one at the back of the neck or nape of the neck.
Finally, press the cautery slightly into the bone until a small indentation is made, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is alleviated; and cauterize over the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and is clearly visible, then you must cauterize with the cauteries described for the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and two cauteries on each cheekbone, and two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the vertebrae near the hip, and one on the hip, and two on the shoulders, and one on each shoulder, and two on the elbows and one on each elbow, and two on the knees, and two on the thighs, and two on the lower leg muscles, and two on the joints of the toes of each foot; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of the feet, and on the armpits of the arms.
Be careful of the nerves that are on the heel so you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pelvic bone, and another on the opening of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient feels no pain from the cautery, just as they don't from incisions, because the body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as is prescribed by the types of tools.
And then treat the cautery with flour of barley cooked with honey and with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Concerning the cauterization of numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery enter the flesh slightly. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness, which affects the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back and the nerves that serve that limb, is cauterized, and the numbness is removed.
However, do not presume upon this unless you are knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the distribution of the nerves throughout the entire moving body.
Chapter 49.
Of the cauterization of albaras, which is morphea.
When albaras is old and you cannot cure it with any medicinal ointments, then cauterize over it with a cautery, applying a little heat according to the severity of the disease until the whiteness and color of it are removed, and its color is changed.
And then treat it with flour of barley and lentils, mixed with roasted olive oil and arnica leaves, and with a portion of pigeon blood or swallow blood; all mixed together.  A cloth should be soaked and placed over the affected area until healed.
Chapter 50.
Of cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is starting and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, in the same way as it is rolled out (or in the same way it is spread out).
And the learned ones say it should be cauterized with the last cautery in the middle; but I have not seen that done, because I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have seen that happen several times.
And it is better to cauterize it in a circle around it as we said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of malignant growths.
When a malignant growth is slow to mature, and this is due to excess of the [malignant] matter, or the age of the patient, because they are old and frail, with little blood, or due to time, and you want to hasten the maturation of the growth; then cauterize in a circle around it with many small, puncturing cauteries; and then leave it, for it will speed up its maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the growth until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in the lower location, so that the arm's condition is relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Concerning the cauterization of corrosive sores/ulcers.
A corrosive ulcer is a wandering corruption in the limb, which corrodes it just as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if this corrosive ulcer is in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, then you use cauterizing irons, small ones in small areas and larger ones as appropriate to the location of the ulcer.
And then cauterize it from all sides until you eradicate the entire ulcer and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply dressings soaked in mixed oil to the cauterized areas until the scabs are all gone and all corruption is eradicated.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
But if after three days, healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas.
And sometimes a corrosive medicine is used, as it serves in place of cautery. But however, cautery with fire is a more speedy remedy.
And I have already described the treatment with a sharp medicine in the division, so take it when you need it.
Chapter 53.
Of cauterization of ulcers and other similar afflictions.
Many times there are ailments called ulcers beneath the sick foot, which are large, jagged sores, hurting the foot.  Cauterization for them is done in two ways: with fire and with hot water.
But cauterization with fire involves heating a concave cautery, similar to a small crucible, made of iron, fitting the size of the ulcer in all directions, and with fine edges.
Then place that heated cautery on the ulcer, and rotate your hand with the cautery around the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer. And leave it for three days until it begins to discharge pus. Then apply a poultice of crushed wild mallows with salt, and leave the poultice on it for one night, for that will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment until it is healed.
And if this doesn't work, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you must administer cauterization with water. Take a cannula of bronze, iron, or a hawk's feather, and place it on the ulcer.  Pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand over it.  Rotate the cannula with pressure from your hand all around, ensuring the cannula's edge is pressed into the root of the ulcer.  The hot water will reach the base of the ulcer.  Allow the patient to endure the effect of the hot water for about an hour, and then remove it.  The ulcer and its roots will be eradicated.
And do this with those and then the other until all those that are of their kind are on the body.
And then treat those pus-filled sores since the roots of the ulcers are eradicated, with ointments that generate new flesh.
Chapter 44.
Of the cauterization of tremor.
When someone has a tremor caused by cold in the nerves, or by a cold-induced fever, or by another similar cause, then you should cauterize it with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one cautery between each vertebra; and with a cautery on their foot, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And the tremor of the patient subsides, hastening the recovery from the cold illness.
Chapter 45.
Of cauterization of white blemishes on the body.
When white spots and pustules, which are caused by thick, corrupted matter, appear on the body, it is necessary, at the beginning, when they are evident, that the head of each fistula be cauterized with a fine cautery made from myrtle wood, the end of which is heated; or with long root of erestology; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes, at the beginning of their expulsion, a cautery is applied in this manner; and if they do not yield, the excess is broken up, and the afflicted body heals.
However, this must be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
Chapter LVI.
Concerning the cauterization of accidental blood flow by arrow-shaped incisions.
Many times, an arrow wound is incised towards the wound itself, which then protrudes, or towards the incision of an abscess or cauterization of the limb, and similar cases, and it is difficult to heal that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand against the opening of the arrow wound, and place your index finger over it, pressing repeatedly until the flow of blood under your finger is stopped, and it does not come out.
And then place the small and large olive-shaped cauteries, in some quantity, in the fire, and blow on them until they are very hot.
And then, take one of those, small or large depending on the location and wound where the artery is broken, onto that vein quickly, after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery until the blood is cauterized and tightened.
And if the blood is expelled from the artery opening when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from the cauteries that are prepared in the fire, and thus continue to do this one after another until the blood is constricted.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and that another harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to constrict the blood, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods: either with a cautery as we have said, or by extraction if it is not already extracted, for when it is extracted, the extremities of the patient are often burned and the blood is constricted, or that it is tied with thread or a bandage by a strong constriction, or that medicines whose virtue is to constrict the blood are placed upon it, and are strengthened by a cushion with suitable compression.
However, the one who administers a proper constriction by means of a bandage and constriction with cloths or by applying burning substances and similar, does not achieve it quickly.
But if this happens to someone and the physician is not present, nor is medicine available, then, using gentle pressure of the index finger on the opening of the vein as we have said, proceed to strongly compress it until the blood is stopped from its outward flow, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and above the artery, it should be sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened;  and further action, whether with cautery or medicine, is necessary.
Of incisions and perforations, leeches, phlebotomy, sores, and similar afflictions.
We have already discussed in the first chapter all illnesses for which cauterization with fire and comburent medicines are appropriate, along with the occasions and causes for them, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have organized the chapters from the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the seeker of this knowledge will be relieved, concerning whatever they have inquired about.
And before I begin this, children, it is necessary to understand that in this chapter, the content is related to the first chapter on cauterization.
And for this reason, great care is required, because in the operations of this chapter, many times, the evacuation necessary for life is achieved through bloodletting, be it by incision of a vein, or an incision on an abscess or an opening for discharge, or the removal of a foreign object, or extracting a dart, or the incision of a stone, and similar procedures. And all of those are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in the hands of those who do them lies death.
And I warn you that this should not happen to you, for sometimes you will encounter men with many kinds of illnesses. And some of them will become enraged by their illness, and death is near for them, due to the severity of their illness, and the length of their suffering, and with the illness comes fear, which signifies death.
And of those is the one who gives you their belongings and says, "You are hoping for health," and their illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not agree to the one who comes to you in this manner; let your caution be greater than your desire and your recklessness.  Do not presume any cause upon this, but only with certain knowledge which verifies this to you, and with which comes a praiseworthy outcome.
Practice in the treatment of all your patients with prognosis and indication to that which is converted to health, for in that is help for you in gaining fame and memory, praise and glory.
Oh, children, may God inspire your understanding and do not deprive yourselves of his guidance and protection, for this is in his power.
And I have organized this chapter, distinguishing it by sections, as was previously done in the chapter on cauteries, concerning the treatment from the head to the feet, so that this will alleviate what you seek and of which you have need.
First Chapter.
Of the treatment of water contained in the heads of children.
Know that this ailment often affects children around the time of birth, and when the nurse tightens the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it occurs due to a hidden cause that is unknown.
However, I have only seen this illness in children; and all those I saw it in died, and thus I am convinced it is a hopeless case in such patients.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was constantly swelling until he could no longer support himself because of the size of his head, and that the moisture continued to increase until he died.
And this moisture is either accumulated between the skull and the bone, or accumulated beneath the bone on the spinal column.
When the treatment for this condition, where the moisture is between the skull and the bone and the abscess is small, you should make an incision in the middle of the head,  a slit whose length is about two inches, until the moisture flows out.  This is the method of treatment for this condition (or, this is the form of the operation).
And therefore, if the moisture is more extensive and the abscess larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, a sign of this is that you see the seams of the skull open on all sides, and the water is squeezed out when you press on it with your hand in various places, and this is not hidden from you. You should make an incision in the middle of the head using three slits in this manner:
Then remove all the moisture from the incision; then tighten the slits with cloths and dressings; then apply wine and oil to it until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with dressings and ointments, and support the head evenly; and the patient should eat dry foods with low moisture content until the affected area is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, where you consider the size of the abscess and the collection of water, for sometimes it is more in the back of the head, or in the front, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore, make the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it in the way that seems best to you.
And be careful not to cut the artery, lest blood flow out and the patient die from that blood loss and the evacuation of moisture.
Second chapter.
When there is persistent catarrh or inflammation in the eyes or foot, and the usual medicines are not effective, then the final treatment in this case is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you want to incise those, if the patient is holding their head, rub the area with a rough cloth to expose the arteries, then tie the patient's head with a cloth; then consider where the vein pulses; and mark the spots on each side near the ears, because they are hidden in some people.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and then cut each one with a sharp incision, penetrating the bone, and do so at the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the probe under the artery and sever it at the points above.
And the length of the incision should be about two fingers wide, for when any vein is cut, the blood flows out with a pulsating exit, which jumps out frequently.
But if the artery is not visible, then you should measure from the ear the distance of three fingers, and then mark it with a probe and cut to the bone.
And the amount of blood that needs to be drawn is about one ounce, and the amount may vary more or less, and all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of inflammation remains on the bone, then cut that out so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And then bind the wound with bandages and linen strips, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When, in some cases, you encounter chronic migraines or severe headaches, caused by irritating moisture and by the quality of inflammation in the temples, or a sudden severe pain resembling these, and treatment with the established methods of medicine is not effective, then we have proven effective in such ailments the extraction of arteries in the temples, or cauterization of those as described previously.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those arteries is that the patient shaves the hair which is on the temples; and notice the artery appearing in the temple as it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden except to a few people because of the violence of the cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's neck should be tightened with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or apply hot water to the area until the artery appears clearly to you. And then you take the instrument, which has this shape: And then you carefully slice the skin with that instrument until you reach the artery; and then you apply cautery to the area and extract that artery in a systematic way until you pull it free from the surrounding tissues and expose those beneath it, from all sides.
But if the artery is thin, then wrap it with the tip of the cautery, and then cut off a portion according to the extent to which its ends are lengthened and drawn together, so that there will be no blood flow; for if it is not severed, the blood will not fully recede.
And then drain the blood from a vein one to three times.
And if the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with strong double-stitched silk or long cords, and do not cut it until the wound has healed and blood flow ceases.
And then you cut away the excess tissue that lies between two ligaments; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize that with a cautery, even to the bone, with a scalpel cautery, until its ends are cut, and the same action will take place there.
And it is a better course of action if the patient does not have a fever or is not of a hot temperament; for cauterization is one of those things that helps to remove moisture, and thus it is better for curing.
And it is necessary, after removing the arteries, that the area be filled with old lint and be sprinkled with properly prepared powders, and then be treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth, and with poultices until it heals.
And if, while working, there is a further flow of blood from the artery, then you must try to stop that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the area with lint, and pressing your hand until the blood is stopped. And if none of those things is at hand, then place your finger on the area until the blood is congealed, and apply water of great coldness to the area until the pain subsides, and it is constricted as needed.
And the easiest way to remove an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery, having two blades, after marking the two spots with a probe, and making each one extremely hot, until they reach the bone and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between the ends of it is stretched, because they are not completely consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two blades is important. However, if the sharpness is less than that of two blades, it is better, for if it is sharp like two blades close to each other, it will not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in those [blades], the heat of the fire is maintained in them, and the flesh is cut easily.
And this method is better than any other method and is more convenient.
And the space between the two blades should be the width of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the treatment of bodies afflicted with acute and acrid humors in the eyes.
When the eyes are acrid, and the vessels beneath the scalp become inflamed, and you find no medicinal cause for their treatment, and sometimes the patient's face begins to redden, and they feel a crawling sensation on their forehead as if ants were crawling, and the eyes are weakened, moist, to the point that their skin is blurred, and the eyelids are burning due to the sharpness of the tears,  ancient healers faced difficulties in treating these cases and often called upon a variety of procedures.
And you instruct the patient to shave the hair on their forehead, and then make three equally spaced incisions across the forehead, following its length. And if the incision is two fingers long: one incision should be centered on the forehead, directly above the bridge of the nose; the second should be positioned slightly along the length of the space between the eyes; the third at the other end of the forehead.
And be careful not to cut the arteries which are located on both sides, and keep your hand away from continuing the incision through both jaws. And if the space between each incision is the width of three fingers.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to soak up the blood.
And then insert the spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the shape of the incision: which is from the temple area up to the second incision. Scrape with that the entire membrane which is between the two incisions with the chisel that is over the bone, and then do this from the middle incision up to the second incision. And then remove this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a knife, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the blade of that sharp part against the flesh adjacent to the membrane, and its handle against the bone, and push it until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut with that all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision continue in this way on the membrane, and then do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.
And since a quantity of congealed blood flows, express the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old cloth, and put on those powders mixed with wine, oil, and sour wine, and make sure that no hot abscess develops.
And on the third day, loosen the ligament and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basil ointment with rubbed oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the treatment of sores and descending ulcers in the parts within the head.
When a person experiences sharp and persistent sores, and the patient's ulcers are small and spreading, and the healing of them is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and the skin around them is affected, and there is a sharp and debilitating pain in the scalp area, then know from these symptoms that the sores and ulcers come from a multitude of veins in the parts of the head itself.
And the best treatment in these cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make a small incision in the middle of the forehead (starting with an incision from the left temple to the right temple; and the incision should go down to the bone; and carefully move your hand away from the temporal lacerations, which are likely to cause further problems during chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth, and then fill the gap between the two edges of the incision with old cloth or linen. And then apply a poultice made of powders mixed with wine and oil, to prevent a painful abscess from forming.
And when you remove this and see that the abscess has begun to diminish, then you must clean the bone until flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen that encourages flesh growth, just as if you take two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony and mix them, making a plaster from this, which should be applied to these wounds to promote the growth of new flesh.
Chapter 6.
Of the treatment of that which affects the ears.
Every ailment affecting the ears belongs to one of four categories: either a mineral, or something resembling a mineral, such as iron or glass; or a plant, such as a thorn or bean; or a fluid substance, such as water, sour wine, and similar liquids; or an animal.
And therefore, when a stone or a similar substance that doesn't adhere or grow in the ear is lodged there, and you expose the ear to the sun, and if you can dislodge the stone, then instill into it some kind of violet or cedar oil, and then remove it, and make the person sneeze with some remedy, and block the nostrils to support the sneezing action. And then place some cloths or wool around the ear, and stretch the ear outward, for often it can be forced out by these methods.
And if it isn't forced out, then administer extraction with a tool called a *gesti epicicarios subtil*, of which this is the form:
And if it is forced out with the instrument, good; and if not, then administer extraction with a thin, slightly curved implement.
And therefore, if it is not forced out by that, then make a copper cannula and insert the end of the cannula into the ear canal, and seal around the cannula with softened wax and oil, so that only the cannula is open. Then extract with your hand with strong traction, for often with this method we have had success.
But if it is not forced out in this way, then take a lit candle or a similar tool used to hold small birds, and place it on the wound's edge, and then gently wrap cloth around it. And then gently insert that into the ear canal, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not forced out with all the methods we have described, then you should consider cutting it open before it becomes a painful abscess or spasm.
And the method of cutting it is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein, and draw out blood according to its nature, and then make a cut between your hands and turn the ear to its upper parts, and make a small incision at the root of the ear towards its thickest part, at a place that is well pressed by you. And let the incision be in the shape of a crescent until it reaches the stone, and, once the stone is found, remove it if possible with appropriate instruments; then close the incision quickly at that time, and heal it until it is cured.
And if the cause of the inflammation in the ear is one of the growths that swells and inflames, then administer its cure and extraction; but if you do not obey this method, then you should take a thin, subtle spatum according to this method: And administer with that the excision of that growth which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the ear's vapor, and put it in small pieces, and remove them with tweezers or with a subtle gesture, or with an incision as we have said, because the extraction is then easier.
But for water that has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing, with caution at the beginning, and fill the ear with old cotton, and lie on the ear that has water in it. If the water is thus expelled, it is good; if not, then take smooth small stones, the length of a finger, heat them slightly in the fire, and the patient puts one into the ear canal and steps on that foot of that side, and strikes with another stone on the stone that is within the ear. Therefore, do not cease with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease with the stone until all the water is expelled. And when the water is expelled in this way, you take paper or linen, place one end in the ear, and heat the other end with fire until the majority of it is burned.
And then put in more linen, and do this as many times as necessary until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula as described above for the stone extraction.
But in the extraction of a beast entering the ear, if it is small, like a tick or similar, treat it as we said in the division; but if it is a large creature that appears within the ear itself, then perform its extraction with forceps and hooks, and its removal is easier than all the other things that happen in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment doesn't work, then you must expose the ear to the sun.
And thus, if any creature of the worms appears there, then extract it with fine forceps or hooks; but if no creature appears, then take a cannula that is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, of which this is the form: And introduce the narrow end into the ear according to how much the patient can tolerate; and then, with a strong suction, suck with it, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not yield to this treatment, then you should seal and close the area around the cannula with wax, as we have said; then administer distillations, which the ancients have already tested in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your application be with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom, with a small hole, and broad at the top.
And then it should be like a pump, so that what is within the cavity of the cannula is continuously pushed where it is needed. And if you wish, take the reed and wrap its end properly with cotton, then pour oil into the cavity of the instrument, or the juice of some herb, or whatever other medicine you desire, into the cannula—and that is in the ear—then insert the reed with the cotton on top, and squeeze with your hand gently until the oil is expelled into the cavity of the instrument and the patient feels the oil within.
And whatever is put into the ear should first be slightly warmed over a fire. And be careful that the substance you are distilling into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Of the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes, children get this from their mothers' wombs; it also happens in some men, an obstruction in the ear canal due to a plaque or fleshy growth; and this obstruction sometimes forms at the opening of the ear, preventing the passage of sound, and sometimes it's in the external parts.
And that which occurs at the opening, preventing sound from passing, is in the most difficult conditions for treatment.
And in those cases where sound passes through, you should place the patient's ear towards the sun and observe it. And therefore, if obstruction appears, open it with a subtle instrument, of which this is its form:
If its extremity has a small width, one part sharp and the other not; and the instrument has two loops to avoid harming the ear.
And therefore, if the obstruction is due to flesh growing in the ear or its passage, then remove it with fine tweezers and carefully cut away all the flesh until it is gone.
And if the obstruction is in the [ear] canal, then take a fine, thin [stick/rod] and slightly heat it with fire, and then place it into the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have felt obstruction making hearing difficult for the patient, gently relieve the pressure; and be careful not to injure the ear's nerves.
Then place a cloth, sized to fit the hole you have made, soaked in Egyptian ointment, inside the ear.  Do this for several days until you're sure the wound is healed.  Or, take a herb, grind it into powder, and apply it to the area. If the ear is already suppurating, you must remove the herb and replace it with another one wrapped in a bandage made from warmed oil until the suppuration subsides; then treat it further until healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use another treatment to staunch the bleeding, as described in the treatise on [blood]letting.
Chapter 8.
Of the treatment of [a condition] called "athezil" that affects the eyelids.
Athezil, which sometimes affects the eyelids of the eyes, is sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with forceps or tweezers, and cut them off with a knife from their roots, and then apply a compress to the areas.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize those areas with a small, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return if any cause remains from their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that occurs on the eyelids.
Often a cause similar to a swelling in its strength and hardness is found in the eyelids, and thus it is called swelling.
And it is a buildup of thick moisture on the eyelid, both above and below.
Therefore, the treatment is to consider if the swelling originates from something external to the eyelid and spreads outward; and the cause of it is light/minor.
Therefore, make a slit over it along its width, and then gently scrape it from all sides until it subsides, then support it with a bandage and excise it.
And if the swelling is large, then you treat it with sutures; and heal it until it is cured.
And if it is small, you will not have any trouble because the ointment will restore it and cause it to heal.
And if the swelling is receding towards the inner part of the eyelid, away from the surface, then elevate the eyelid, support the swelling with a bandage, other than if you intend to make an incision, and press it from every side.  If the eyelid is incised, this will not help in any way.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water, and treat the area with something that promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the treatment of a boil on the eyelid above the eye.
A boil is a sore that develops within the eyelid tissues above the eye, and it is more common in children, worsening the eyes of those afflicted and causing difficulty in the morning; and they cannot see the sunlight clearly because tears accumulate there.
And therefore, you often see them lying on their faces all the time, and they sleep on their faces; and the eyelids below their eyebrows are moist, which are already covered by inflammation, and the presence of a boil is apparent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel a boil there.
And a method of operation is this: have the patient rest their head on your lap, then place a cloth over them and heat it, and make a circle of it according to the size that contains the boil on all sides.  Then place this over it and press with your fingers everywhere so the boil is contained within the circle.  Then make a small incision in the middle of the moist area, using a lancet, with a slit not wider than the incision made during a bloodletting procedure.
However, in the beginning, you must break open the boil completely until you reach the boil, for in most cases the boil progresses through an incision, following the form of a white pus.
Then remove that pus with cloths that you wrapped around your fingers on the outside and rub it back and forth between your right and left hand until it has disappeared.
And be careful not to press too hard on the incision, so that with the lancet you do not pierce the vessel and damage it.
However, if no boil appears at the beginning of the incision, but you feel a slight pressure on the incision that produces pus; then remove the pus as we described earlier;  then soak cloths in warm water and vinegar and place them over the area and compress them with powders.
And some put salt and bread in the cavity of the incision so that the remaining moisture is absorbed.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if there is a hot abscess in the area, then treat it with cooling plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Of methods of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, there are pimples/inflammation  that arise beneath the natural skin and become hardened, then these attack the eye and cause ailments such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and tissues, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called "tactinir of the eye."  It is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with a sharp medicine, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or by cutting and stitching the tactinir; or by means of canes, as I will explain.
And therefore, you should place the patient's head on your lap; then gently turn the eyelid with your left hand, and if it turns, that's good; and if not, insert a needle into that part that is furthest below the eyelid, and push the needle with the thread through it, and this should be — near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow — close to the hairs, and pull the thread to the top through the eyelid, and turn the eyelid with the instrument.
And then, in the part inside the eyelid beneath the hardened hairs, make small incisions with the major lacrimal instrument down to the minor lacrimal instrument; then pull out the thread and place small powder of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then press on the eyelid with a tenaculum, and shape it like a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the degree of elevation of the eyelid, for this varies greatly in people, for some require a large incision in the eyelid according to the degree to which the eyelid is softened.
And then, using the instrument, make incisions along the two lines you have marked, beginning at the major lacrimal and continuing to the minor lacrimal. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, it should be as wide as the king's finger.
And then, place the forceps at one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape the entire area; then, using the suture, join the two edges of the wound together with a thin wool thread or thread, and clean the blood; and if you wish, affix the excess thread to the two eyebrows with certain adhesive substances, otherwise do not bother.
And then leave the stitches of thread in place for three or four days, and then treat the wound.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitches, and treat it with things that dry and constrict, for the eyelid is lifted by the sealing of the wound and its adherence; but stitching is better.  Therefore, the ancients say this.
However, be diligent about the patient, and it is a good operation, and there is no fear for the patient.
And there is another method in treating this, and it is that you should shape the eyelid like a myrtle leaf, and then lift the eyelid with three separated and joined forceps in this manner:
But if that is not possible, holding the forceps, and that is not suitable, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the shape, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads equally between your fingers, and then with those, lift your hand in a suitable lift, raise the entire eyelid on which it is marked as we have described.
And then bring together the two edges of the wound with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess occurs near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with plasters and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes the eyelashes on the eyelid need to be removed, in which case you should use what we have said about incisions; but stitching in such cases is more artificial.
To treat with canes is according to this form: And you must turn the eyelid inside out and cut the figure inside it as I have described; and then, by art, make two thin canes or splints whose length corresponds to the length of the eyelid and whose width is less than the width of the gap. And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully attach each one to the excess of the eyelid of the eye, and tightly bind the canes in each part, and leave it for a few days, because the flesh will die from the tightness and will be rejected and decompose until it falls off by itself.
But if it has been delayed, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Therefore, once it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised, and the skin is not irritated.
And this is the method for the two canes, which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of lifting irritated skin from a wound with a needle when the skin is hardened.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and irritating hairs in the wounds, then the operation for them is that you take a fine needle and put it on a fine linen or silk, and then carefully fit its ends together, each in a very subtle manner. And the length of the threads should be about the length of one palm.
And then you carefully attach another fine thread underneath, and use the ends of that if you want.
And then position the patient's head on your lap, and let it be near the light so that your work is clear, for it is a delicate and minute operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the attached hair, which needs dexterity, and swiftly penetrate the eyelid until you remove that hair from above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair out above it and place it in the forceps, and let there be one, two, or three hairs, and no more.
And then pull your hand with it until the hair is pulled out, along with the forceps and the natural hair from the eyelid.
But if the forceps is pulled and the hair is not removed, you will pull the forceps in different directions with the ointment that you have prepared, until the hair is pulled out of the wound.
And close it with the vision of the eye, because then you will remove the forceps and the ointment, and everyone will wash and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and healthy flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and lift it up with that, or leave it until it extends approximately some distance, and then lift it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of an abrasion which comes on the upper eyelid.
When an abrasion occurs on the eye, and the eyelid is contracted upward, either on the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound, incision, or cauterization, and is called a harelip, this abrasion or is natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of a wound, incision, or cauterization, or by similar poorly healed injuries.
The method of treatment in such a case is to break up the scar tissue and separate the lip edges.  Place a piece of linen cloth between them and tie them together until they heal.
And you should not use treatments that dry or constrict; for if you do, the abrasion will return worse than it ever was before.
Use remedies that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice made from water steeped with mallow and flax seeds, or diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with an oil.  Dip the cloth into this and apply it as a treatment.
The key to your treatment is to break apart any unhealed scar tissue, returning it to its original form.
And this operation, which we have described, is simply a way of correcting the abrasion so that the area returns to its former shape.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of an abrasion that occurs on the lower eyelid.
This abrasion on the lower eyelid, properly called an abrasion, may be either a natural or an accidental affliction.
But accidental abrasions are caused by blows, cuts, cauterization, or similar injuries.
And the method of treating it is that you take a needle with a double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and pass it from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches both extremities of the flesh.
Then spread the flesh apart with the needle and stitch it with the needle, and cut with a wide scalpel. And if the eyelid returns to its proper shape, so much the better; and if not, take a thread and place it in the slit, and with it, close the eyelid, and bind the two fissures together at the lower eyelid.
And let the extremities of the two fissures be at two angles of the incision you have made, meeting, and forming an angle where they come together.
And this figure is the Greek letter: lambda.
And now remove the flesh, in the amount that is the lower sharp edge of the eyelid.
Then bring together the separated parts and sew them with the wool thread; and then treat them with the softening medicines and poultices we mentioned until it is healed.
And if it needs to be repaired by incision, suture, or cautery, then you should make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid, as previously described. Then separate with a poultice between the two lips, as we have said.
In summary, when treating an eye injury (alxacrati), whether above or below, the procedure should follow the preparation of the eye injury, which can present in various forms.
And the skilled artisan always prepares in every possible way until the form returns to its natural, or nearly natural, state, and the patient according to what is appropriate for the operation and the instruments in the best conditions.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
When you encounter this adhesion in many people, so that the eyelid is firmly glued to the white of the eye, and the natural movement and function are hindered, then you must insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid, and lift or extend it with forceps.
And then you carefully dissect the adhesion with a thin, blunt-edged instrument, so that the incision is not sharp and the patient is not disturbed by the operation.  Leave the instrument in place to avoid injuring the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should carefully sever the adhesion until the eyelid returns to its natural form and all adhesion is removed.
And then you apply salt water to the eye, washing it; or use a solution of lead dissolved in water.
Then carefully separate the eyelid from the eye with a gentle hand, and apply wool soaked in egg white to the eye.  After three days, use healing remedies until healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the incision of a toenail and the protruding flesh of the tear ducts.
Know that a toenail is of two types: one is nervous, and resembles a hard, fine splinter; or it is non-nervous, resembling frozen white moisture, which when the iron touches it, or is forced to be picked up with forceps, is detached and the forceps do not get stuck in it.
And both types originate from the larger tear duct, gradually extending to cover the eye and obstructing the light and movement of the eye.
But the method of operating on it is that the patient places their head on your lap, then opens their eyes, and you raise the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with forceps of little bending and pull it away from the surrounding area. Then take a needle and insert a piece of horsehair, or oxhair, or cowhair, or similar, or strong thread, or gradually twist the tip of the needle and place it in the middle of the toenail, and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread; and pull it away from the surrounding area, and scrape away the piece of nail that is obscuring the pupil. And thus you remove it with the hair until the end of it.
And then you cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or small scissors, and leave the natural tissue of the tear duct intact so that cutting it does not cause a continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail, with forceps or thread, as we have said, is pulled out and then scraped away with a lion's spatula (a scraping tool).  This is the form/shape of it:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and cause a rupture, and that the pupil is not pierced.
After the incision, put a little powdered salt or a prepared powder into the wound, and leave it there until the next day; then treat it with whatever is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not painful and you cannot insert a needle, nor is it embedded in the flesh by the forceps, then in many cases this type of nail is treated with medicines, and is healed as I have described and recommended in the division.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the wound of the patient and take a sharp, slender lion's spatula shaped like this: with subtlety. And if it is often thin and inflamed and has spread to the surrounding area, then apply a distillation from your apothecary's preparation or rose-colored ointment or powdered salt to the wound and cover it until the next day.
And then repeat the procedure until all that is of it is removed, otherwise a hot abscess will be in the wound, for then you leave it and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your procedure on that with care until it is healed.
If the tear duct protrudes in the wound causing great discomfort, it should be suspended with a thread; and cut it from that one part, but do not continue the incision so as not to cause a flow of tears.
Then apply rose-colored ointment and the apothecary's preparation to the tear duct until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the incision of the ulcer and what arises from flesh adhering to the wound.
Sometimes in the wounds of some, red flesh grows and is overlain until it covers the pupil, or approaches or tightens around the eyelids, and strongly compresses the eyelids at the outer edges, resembling the surface of a balustrade.
But the operation on him is that the patient rests his head on your lap; and then open the wounds of that and gather all that flesh with suitable forceps, and hold it with a pair of forceps or a spatula; and then cut the flesh from the beginning until the entire operation with the scalpel is finished, and watch that you do not injure the wound during your operation.
And your incision shall be with the same scalpel that we described in the incision of the nail, or with small forceps, with which the scab is removed, according to the form that will appear in this chapter.
Therefore, when your incision is complete and all that flesh is removed, then fill the wound with coarse salt, or pour into it a red powder similar to corrosive medicines, and place over the wound externally a cloth with egg white until it is assured of a cautious suppuration.
And if any cause of inflammation remains and a cautious bleeding occurs, and you fear a cautious abscess, then leave the wound and treat that abscess with what will cause that abscess to subside, and then repeat the operation until it is healed, and similarly do with the flesh that forms that comes out of the wound of this sort.
However, you should avoid any treatment of the wound by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that that member is enduring the operation.
Chapter 18.
Of the collection of pus in the wound.
Pus comes from red veins that are crusted over the wound and prevents its healing, and the wound should be treated for many days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the wound in which there is pus is strong and has no other illness than pus, then you collect that pus.
And you should tell the patient to place their head on your lap, and then restrain those veins with an ounce or two, according to your judgment; and that ounce should be of a subtle, twofold application, in this way:
or two ounces gradually in one vessel, according to this form:
And then those veins should be collected with subtle forces, subtly, and the blood should be wiped away hour by hour, until you see those veins pulled away from the wound and are rolled up.
And be careful not to damage the wound with the extremities of the spatula; and if your operation is in the middle of the day before the sun, and be very firm in your operation so as not to cut anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then you distill into the wound either green or red juice, so that with its sharpness it corrodes what has been pulled away from the vein.
But if that is not possible for you to collect it all at that time, then you plaster it with whatever eases the pain, and leave it for several days until the pain is calmed and the possibility of a hot abscess is ensured.
And then repeat according to the same method until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
Of the reduction of a nasal injury.
When you treat a nasal injury with cautery or a sharp burning medicine, and, as is stated in the description, it is not healed, then there is no remedy in it, but rather you must open the abscess above its maturity, and drain all the moisture and pus until the bone is exposed.
And thus, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the shape:
And it is called a "sharp head," and it is made of hardened iron, and the head is round, like a pestle, which is made pointed by filing or by fine carving (as is in the following form).
And place that on the corrupted area of the bone, and then turn it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And do this several times, and then restore the area with desiccant and astringent substances.
Therefore, if the area is consolidated and healthy flesh has grown, and the body is free from pus and remains for 40 days, and is not renewed or inflamed, and no accident occurs there, then you know it is healed.
And if not, then it is not a cure, but rather to reduce the fracture to the shape of the nose, and according to this method: and it is that you expose the second bone with iron or with a sharp medicine, and once the bone is exposed, then you take a strip according to this form:
Let the tip of the iron be triangular, and the shaft be thin and tapered to the tip, like this (or, as is often done).
And then place that on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and move your hand a little away from the eye, and then move your hand with the strip until it penetrates the entire bone, and the patient will feel this because they will find air escaping when you move your hand.
Therefore, then restore the area with astringent drying medicines, as you have been taught.
Then, when the flesh and the area are hardened, the matter that spread outwards returns to the nose, thus it is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the eminence of the eye.
When the eye protrudes completely and you do not encounter damage or diminution in the vision, then it is necessary to leave the patient alone and then to follow it, and then to apply leeches to the neck without incisions, and to apply them with good suction; and then apply a liniment made of catnip, aloe, henbane, and sarcocolla to the eye, and then press the plaster over the eye with many powders.
And the method for the powders is to take very fine linen cloths, and each cloth should be doubled into four folds; and make many of those.
And then place those, one upon another, according to the amount the eye needs. And then press upon those with a hard cloth with strong pressure; and repeat the leeches with suction without incisions; and then remove the leeches and leave the eye pressed tight day and night.
But if meanwhile the swelling is softened, then it is necessary that it be very tight.
And then remove the bandage day and night, just as we have said.
And if you see the eye already reduced, good; and if not, then repeat the poultice with powders and the pressure and the leeches until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.
When there is a rupture in the uvea tunic, and it is prominent and protruding from the eyelids like a cluster of grapes, and the person's form is marred by it, and you wish to incise it, then the operation there is as we have recounted, that you insert a needle into the roots of the uvea from the lower to the upper part, and then put another needle on which there is a double thread from the tear duct side, and make that penetrate and let the first needle follow its path; and then you incise the place with the double thread and remove the part of the uvea in the circuit of the needle, and tighten that greatly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool infused in egg white, and leave until the thread with the grape falls out.
And then treat the eye with this, which comforts it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes, a collection from every side with a cloth circle around the eye adjusts a large protruding mass, and it is then pierced with a fine needle that comes to the eye's edge, and the albuminous moisture runs out, and the eye descends and enters quickly.
And then tighten until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of falconers.
This illness, called alconati, is nothing but a fluid which has collected in the eye, resembling descending water, but it is not that.
However, the method of treatment is to have the patient sit on a prepared and straight seat, then take their head in your hands at two points and move it until you see the fluid coming out of the lower parts, and then it is dried and does not descend, and the light is blocked, and the patient sees things as they appear.
And if it does not descend further, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the fluid is not prepared with what we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands, and then take some fine, thin material, and use that to apply a small amount to the conjunctiva area near the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea (on the exposed area), until the fluid is expelled.
Therefore, when it is expelled, then you instill into the eye warm water, in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then treat with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of water descending from the eye.
We have already described the types of water in the division and which ones should be pierced with a lancet, explaining, then, that one [type] follows the health.
Then, you should place the patient between your hands on a bed, and bind their eye tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of their eye, if the eye in which the water is located is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right eye.
And then with your right hand, take an almond, if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
And then place the tip of the almond close to the eyelid, along the thickness of the ray in the white part of the eye, near the smaller tear duct, and then press the almond with force and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the almond has now reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the distance of the almond from the [eye] opening be the same as the space between the pupil and the end of the blackness, and that is the eyelid; for you will see through that pupil with the sight of the eye, through the clarity of the cornea.
And then place the almond into the place where the fluid is, and then pull it down one time, then another.  Therefore, if the fluid descends immediately, then you see the patient looking at what is before them, and the almond in their eye.
And then rest for a little while; therefore, if the fluid has descended, then you pause it again before the almond is thrown out.
And then, when it is dried up and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and gently roll it with your hand. Then dissolve a small amount of clear rock salt in the fluid and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then apply a cloth or a bandage or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white to the eye, and close it.
But we and those of our time place it [presumably the cloth or bandage] over the crushed [eye] area with egg white.
And if you cannot push your almond into the eye because of its hardness – for some people have very hard eyes – then you must take a substance called 'alberid', which is of this form:
And then pierce the conjunctiva with it, and do not press your hand into the hole, and you will only make a subtle passage for the almond.
And then place the almond in as we have said.
And complete your work by preparing a suitable sleeping area for the patient, where they sleep on their back in a dark room, and all movement is prohibited.
And place the patient in a position such that their nature is relaxed, and their head is not turned to either the right or left side at all.
And the bandage should be left in place according to the patient's condition until the third day.
And then, remove that in the darkened room and remove the covering of their eyes, show them some things, and then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And you should not do this at the time of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Having this, you should do this so that the water can easily pass through the great and vehement guarding.
However, if a boil/abscess occurs, then it is necessary to remove the dressing before the seventh day, and to treat it with what will cause the boil/abscess to subside.
Then you cover the patient's eyes and place a bandage over their face, which will regulate the movement of their eyes beneath it for several days, keeping them in a darkened room.
And gradually, the patient should be taken out of that room and gradually returned to their normal activities.
And know that in a difficult case, the student is not excused from examining the patient sometimes, and then you should be bold in your operations.
And it has come to our knowledge from some of those who are at Artach, and some said that a fire was made in the Ayrac, a large hole through which water was drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has accomplished this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the types of medicines, so that those who do not know them may understand them:
