Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
When various growths of flesh have arisen in the nose, some of which are caused by something resembling a very heavy scorpion or a large polyp; and this illness is called polyp because it resembles that depicted fish. And some of it is cancerous, ulcerous, dark-colored flesh; and some of it is lion-like flesh, not dark-colored.
Therefore, for that flesh which is not fraudulent nor cancerous, you should make the patient sit between your hands and expose them to the sun, and open the patient's nostrils, and then prick the flesh with your lancet. Then extract it to the exterior, and then dissect what you have grasped of it with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know all the flesh has been removed; for if any part remains from which the excision is possible, then you slice that with one of the thin instruments with ease until nothing is left of it. However, if it is accompanied by blood or a hot abscess, then you apply what is necessary; or if it is a malignant abscess, then you cauterize it until the blood is separated and all the cavities are removed.
And then, after the incision, place vinegar and water or wine in the nose. But if the nose is open and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is already healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is still corrupted flesh upon the excess bones of the nose to which the incision has not reached.
Therefore, then it is necessary to insert a piece of lead (or a similar dense material), and make many small incisions, placing the space between each incision at the width of a finger or less.  Then, you should make the patient comfortable, and insert one end of the lead into the nose with a probe or another appropriate instrument.
And after doing this, stimulate the patient's breathing until the moisture reaches the nasal passages and exits through their throat, as you often see children do in school, and it is an easy thing to accomplish.
And then place the two ends of the lead, one end emerging from the nose and the other remaining inside, and then apply a scraping/excision of flesh to the incisions in the lead, and continue until you know the flesh is removed around the lead's incisions.
And then remove the lead, and place a poultice, similar to bloodletting, near the nose, and do this for three days or more, until the ointment has corroded all remaining flesh.
And finally, place a lead cannula in the nose for several days until healed.
But if you need a more conclusive cure, use this one.
And this is the form of the instrument for nasal purging, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or brass, which should be similar to a small, open lamp, like a little shell, and the opening similar as well.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a pipe, and the cannula of the purgation instrument should be flat, open; at the back of which should be a handle, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water that you want of juices and subtle substances.
Chapter 25.
Of the ulcers that appear in the extremities of the nose.
Often an ulcer arises at the extremities of the nose, and it grows larger and worsens over several days until it is very noticeable and difficult to treat.
And therefore, you should cut it [the ulcer] at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. And then place either a cautery or a burning medicine on the affected area.
However, if its incision is delayed until it has grown large, then consider if it is hardened, hard, dark-colored, and with little sensation.  If so, do not touch it with iron, for it is a deep-seated abscess; for often, whoever incises this abscess brings great harm to the person who had it.
And if the abscess is easily touched and not dark-colored, and sometimes incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the area with what dries and constricts it until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when the continuity is broken by plague or similar ailments.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, you achieve little in that operation except in certain individuals.
Therefore, when you encounter a cause of that sort in someone, consider if it is a fresh plague with its own blood, and if so, bring together the two sides of the wound with sutures, and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the broken skin are already separated, and each is made sound, then you must scrape each part of their visible skin until blood appears. And then bring together the two edges with sutures, tighten them, and sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed herbs over them.  Place a diafinicon plaster, or some other consolidating plaster, on the powder. Leave this bound for two or three days, then remove it, replace the plaster, and leave it until the pus is separated.  Then treat with ointment until healed.
But the method of suturing is that you bring the broken skin together either with needles, as we described in suturing the belly, or join the break with adhesive, as we have shown you.
Chapter 27.
Of the removal of those things that appear between the two edges/lips in the internal parts.
Often, men experience hard abscesses resembling millet seeds within the folds of their lips; some are smaller, some larger.
Therefore, you must turn back the lips and excise each hard spot completely, and then fill the area with scraped linen until the bleeding has stopped.  Then wash the area with sharp wine and treat it with something astringent until healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, there is flesh adhering to the gums, which the ancients called "albulus."  In this case, you must separate it with a needle or forceps, cut it off at its base, and allow the pus or blood to drain.
And then apply scraped linen or other astringent or drying powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it again and cauterize it, for the cautery will prevent it from returning.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with a metal instrument.
When on the surface of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums, rough, hard, and sometimes blackened or yellowish scales form, causing gum inflammation and resulting in the teeth becoming loose.
Therefore, you should place the patient between your hands, resting their head on your lap, and extract the teeth where these scales and similar dark, gritty substances appear until nothing remains, thus removing the blackness, yellowing, and other impurities.
If you have removed what was there at the beginning of the procedure, it is good.  Otherwise, repeat the procedure the next day, and again on the second and third day until you achieve the desired outcome.
And know that teeth require many different tools and shapes, depending on how you prepare for your operation. And therefore, the scraper used to scrape the inside of the tooth is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth, in other ways, and these various scrapers need to be prepared by you, which you will see soon.
Chapter 30.
Of the removal of teeth.
You should treat the tooth's pain with every effort and take your time with every extraction, because there is no way to compensate for the damage done to it when it's removed, for it is a delicate substance that requires careful consideration during the extraction process.
However, when the patient insists on having a tooth removed, you must be absolutely certain which tooth is the painful one, because many times the pain deceives the patient, suggesting it's in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and the pain doesn't stop until the diseased and painful tooth is removed.  We have seen this ourselves through the experiences of barbers (or surgeons).
Therefore, how are you to be certain which tooth is the painful one, and that you know it well?  Then you should make incisions around the tooth with a substance having some medicinal properties, until the tooth is separated from the gum on all sides.  Then carefully remove it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, little by little until you detach it.
And then, if necessary, use strong large pliers for good leverage or firm grasping, and place the patient's head between your knees to hold it firmly so it doesn't move.  Then extract the tooth in a straight manner, so that you don't break it, and do not cause the patient excessive pain.
And if it is not easily extracted, then take one of those instruments, and place it under it from all sides with ease, and apply force to remove it, just as was done initially.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you must cover the hole with cloth and press it with a thin, sharp instrument so that it does not fracture when you apply the pliers.
And you must carefully make incisions around the entire gum, and be very careful not to break it and leave a portion of it behind, returning to the patient with more malice than the original pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity regarding tooth extraction, without administering what we have described; for often they bring great illnesses upon people which are more difficult to heal than the initial problem, and the roots of the teeth remain, some or all, or they pull out the tooth with some jaw bone, as we have often witnessed.
And after the extraction, the mouth should be rinsed with hot wine or vinegar with salt.
And if there is bleeding, as often happens, then apply a small amount of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if that doesn't stop it, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers, with which the tooth is initially extracted, should be long at the tip, with a short, thick handle, so that they do not bend when using them.
And they should be like that, with a thick handle so that when you tighten them, they don't bend, with short tips.
And they should be made of Indian iron, with straight tips, and on the tips of these, there are teeth that interlock and tighten with strong constriction, and sometimes their tips are shaped like the beak of a stork (or heron), which is strong and tight.
Chapter 31.
Of the removal of teeth and the extraction of bones from fractured mandibles.
When you need to remove a broken tooth, which is still causing problems, you should place a compress with some wool on the area for a day or two until the area is softened; then, use forceps or pliers whose tips are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork or heron, the shape of forceps:
The tips of them should be made sharp, like a file or a whetstone.
And if you are unable to extract the tooth with these forceps or pliers, you must then cut away the surrounding tissue and expose the root and insert a small lever, whose shape is like this:
And it should have a short, thick end, a little bit, and not be too thick so as not to break.
So, if the root is thus extracted, it is good; and if not, use these other instruments, of which these are the shapes: the first instrument is triangular in shape, with some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument should be triangular, with a more slender tip.
And having used these instruments, proceed now with this other instrument, having two ramps, whose shape is approximately this: and with other instruments, which I will describe for tooth extraction; for the extraction is aided by an instrument shaped like a large hook, and this is its form:
of triangular, bent or short shape, which should have some thickness, and not be too narrow so as not to break.
And further understand that the instruments for teeth are similar to other instruments.
But the skillful and subtle artisan in the art, finds for himself, according to what the operation and the various illnesses signify to him; for there are illnesses for which the ancients did not describe or prescribe specific instruments, due to their various forms.
But if the jawbone or other bones of the mouth are fractured, or decayed, then apply pressure to the fractured part, placing it in its proper position, using the appropriate instruments and forceps we have described for extracting teeth roots, and assist it with the method of which this is the form:
And apply gentle, gradual pressure, and hold it in place with the mouth to prevent movement or escape until the bone is fully set; and then restore the area with appropriate medicines.
But if there is a small, painful swelling in the bone, then remove that swelling and its blackness, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth prominent above the others.
When teeth are prominent and do not follow their natural course, and the form is unsightly, especially in women, and particularly in venerable young women, then you must consider: if the tooth is close to another tooth, and separation or repositioning is impossible, then remove that tooth; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then cut it with an instrument of this shape: resembling a pig's snout, made of Indian iron with a very sharp end.
And the incision you make should be gradual over several days due to the tooth's hardness and so that it doesn't damage other teeth.
And if a prominent tooth is exposed for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And it should be entirely made of Indian iron and easily shaped; and it should have many small points, just like the file with which needles are made, with which the tooth should be filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then, finally, smooth it and root it with some razors.
And if a tooth has a fractured part that cuts the tongue, then you must file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and filled in, so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver fillings.
When teeth are fractured or displaced by impact or trauma, and the patient cannot hold the missing piece, and you have treated it with astringent medicines, and your treatment has been ineffective, then, because it does not fall out, it is better to use a gold filling rather than silver, for silver becomes green and deteriorates in a few days, while gold never changes.
And the filling should be so fine and thick according to the amount needed to fit between the teeth; and the method of application is to take the filling and place twice its length between two healthy teeth, and then continue shaping it with two curves of the filling between the fractured teeth, or one or more, until the filling reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the fitting process back to the original starting point, and hold it firmly but gently until it is no longer broken.
And your grip, with which you hold the filling against the tooth roots, must be firm so that it does not fall out.
And then trim the excess ends of the filling with forceps or scissors, and carefully adjust it in place, and shape it and hide it between the healthy tooth and the broken tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue. And then leave it firmly in place as it remains.
However, if it is loose or broken, firmly reattach it or them with another filling in such a way throughout the time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the procedure for restoring two healthy teeth and two broken ones, as seen in these bound teeth:
And when one or two teeth are returned to their sockets, and are reattached as we have described, and remain firmly in place.
And only skilled and knowledgeable artisans can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow bone is shaped like a tooth and placed in the gap where the tooth is missing, and fastened as described, and remains and serves as a replacement for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the constriction of the ligament that affects the tongue and hinders speech.
This ligament, which sometimes forms under the tongue, is either natural, present at birth, or accidental and caused by a wound that has healed.
And the operation on it involves opening the patient's mouth, holding their head; lifting their tongue, and then carefully cutting the nerve ligament, according to the width, until the tongue is freed from its constriction.
However, if there is any hardness or knotting there, resulting from a healed wound, then you should apply a cautery and cut through it according to its width, until you have severed the ligament and the knotting has been dissolved.
And be careful not to create a fissure in the flesh, and that you do not cut too deeply and cause bleeding.
And then wash the incision site with water, vinegar, or cold water; and then place a linen cloth under the tongue. This is a pad that the patient will hold under their tongue every night, so it doesn't heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, apply powdered vitriol to the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a suitable, smooth cautery. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the removal of a ranula (a cyst) under the tongue.
Sometimes, a boil resembling a small tumor develops under the tongue, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it swells significantly until it fills the mouth.
And the treatment for it is to open the patient's mouth, sitting facing the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it as a dark color or black, hard, to which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that which is a cancer.
And if it has turned white, which indicates moisture, then you place a lancet on it, and cut it open with a sharp blade, and separate it from every side; and if blood flows during your operation, then you apply crushed alum, that is, vitriol, so that the blood is stemmed.
And then return to your work until you have removed it completely, until it has been washed with sour wine and salt; and treat it with further treatment for this until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the removal of abscesses on both ears and those found in the throat, and other abscesses.
When an abscess develops in the throat, meaning glands that have swelled together, which have burst outward and are called "two ears," and when you treat them with what we have described in the division and they are not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and with little sensitivity; then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is reddish in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron for the sake of blood flow; rather, allow it to mature, and then either you will drain it, or it will break open on its own.
And if it is whitish, soft, and its root is subtle, then it is one that should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you should, before your operation, consider if the abscess has already rested, having complete rest, or if it has diminished with some lessening; and then make the sick person sit in front of the sun, and let their head be in your lap, and open their mouth, and let the attendant hold their tongue down with a very fine instrument, of which this is the shape:
And it should be made of silver or brass, and be as thin as a knife.
So, when you press the tongue with it and the abscess is shown to you, and you have applied your gaze to it, then take a lancet and place it in the opening, and pull it outward in parts as much as you can. After this, when you pull out some part of the tissues, then cut that with an instrument of this shape:
After this, when you pull out some part of the tissues, then cut that with an instrument of this shape: and it is similar to forceps, but the ends are curved, so that the opening of each is on the opposite side, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a spatula of this shape:  And it should be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one opening, cut the other according to this same method of incision, and after the incision the patient will recover from the cold.
However, if bleeding ensues, treat with water decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves, or similar astringent remedies, until the bleeding is reduced, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat or near the two ears, and cut those according to what I have said about the incision of two openings (incisions).
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her throat, which abscess was tending toward a dark colour, with little sensation, which had already closed off her throat, and the woman was breathing with a narrow airway, and had already been forbidden food and water, and she was close to death if it had lasted another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had formed two fistulas before it broke through two openings.
Therefore, I treated it fistulously and inserted a probe into one of them, and pulled it out, and a large portion of it was pulled out, and then I incised the opening of the other fistula, and then I opened her mouth and pressed down on her tongue, and then I inserted the probe into that abscess.
And then I excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was completely healed, and she immediately began drinking water, and then ate food, so I continued to incise that abscess many times for a long time, and another abscess followed in the same place, which I incised until it was finally resolved.
Therefore, I skillfully cauterized her throat, thus adding to her treatment; and then I walked away and did not know what God had done to her (or to me) in that instance.
Chapter 37.
Of the incision of a boil when it is abscessed.
When catarrh settles on the uvula, and it is abscessed and protracted, then it is called a perpendicular boil; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a bubo.
But when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and you do not consider your cure complete, and sometimes a boil has already been evacuated and is subtle, then it is necessary for you to incise that which is swollen and round; and if it is of that length, and is blood-red, or dark coloured, and is not tender to the touch, then you must test the incision there, for there is fear of the illness in that case.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, by its whiteness and length, have the patient sit up straight and press down on the tongue with an instrument that will reveal its shape, and then place the knife in a position above the boil, and pull down to incise it with one of the two instruments that are described in the incision of two boils.
And it is necessary to incise only that which is aligned with the natural border without excess, for if you incise more than that, you will harm the patient's speech.
And after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of two boils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, use a cautery without fear, and the method of cautery in this case is only with sharp water.  You should place the patient's head in your lap, then press down on the tongue with the instrument we have mentioned.  Next, take the water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it to the living tissue with a moderate amount, filling the cavity of the instrument, which has the following shape:
Let the tip of the cavity where the medicine is placed be like the cavity of a spoon for collecting herbs, and place the instrument with the medicine on the ulcer.  The patient lies on their side so that any saliva or mucus from the mouth flows away due to the medicine, so that nothing from it reaches the patient's throat and harms them.
Then hold the medicine with your hand and press it onto the uvula for half an hour until you see it blackened and settled by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take a cloth and wrap it around the tip of the herb, then dip the cloth in the medicine.  Insert the herb with the cloth into a tube/cannula until the cloth adheres to it. Repeat this as many times as needed to achieve the desired cauterization of the ulcer. Then leave it until it dries and falls off in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  Then, after the cauterization, clean the area around the ulcer with a cloth dipped in wine.  Remove any remaining medicine from around it and wash with cold water.  Treat the outside with an external application and the inside with healing remedies until it is healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incisions and less likely to cause fear.
Sometimes the ulcer is cured with something more delicate than cauterization or incisions, for it is cured by fumigation in this way: take mint, yarrow, oregano, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, marjoram and similar herbs, and combine all or some of these in a pot.  Submerge them in sour wine and boil. The pot should be securely sealed, and a hole should be in the middle of the pot, over which a hollow metal instrument should be placed, preferably silver or brass.
And the end of the instrument should be inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the ulcer through the cannula, until the ulcer is significantly dimmed.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry.  Be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of an abscess, for it would worsen the abscess.  You should not do this without a diagnosis of a mature abscess.
But if this instrument is unavailable, then take a reed and place an egg shell at its end, so that the patient's mouth is protected, for the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the removal of thorns and what adheres to the throat and other similar conditions.
Many times a thorn or fish bone or some other thing adheres to the throat.
Therefore, you should remove whatever is visible, upon which you direct your gaze,  after pressing the tongue with an instrument in front of a light, so that what is in the throat is revealed to you.
And that which is hidden and concealed in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the object lodged in the throat will be expelled by vomiting.  Or make the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or have the patient swallow a piece of dry sea sponge, bound tightly with a bandage, and thus, as it passes to the area where the thorns are, they will be expelled. Or take an eel and drink it with a bandage, and place it within and quickly extract it; or take a leek and quickly put it in and quickly draw it out.
But if it is not expelled by those methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this manner:
And it should be larger than a small ray, and at its end there should be a cavity which you can easily insert into their throat.
And have them raise their head, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so that you don't cause them pain, and with that you push the bone or thorn, or the physician pushes it with their hand.
But if the patient can push it themselves, better, because of their knowledge of the location of the affliction, and push it downwards, and pull their hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the quantity that is prepared for them until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the extraction of bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When the leech is treated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines, and it does not yield, then consider placing the patient's throat in the sun, after you have pressed the patient's tongue with an instrument which we have described to you.
Therefore, if you see the bloodsucker, extract it with a small hook or with delicate, skillful gestures.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron into the concavity of the cannula, and do this many times (until the bloodsucker is dead and removed from the throat), and keep the patient away from water all day.
Then take a vessel full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth to it, and rinse the mouth with it; and let him not swallow anything, and then pour more water from your hand, for the bloodsucker, as soon as it feels the water, will move.
But if it is not expelled by what we have described, then fumigate the patient with incense or with asafoetida using the instrument we described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall.
And there is another method of fumigation for it, which is that you take a bowl in which burning coals are placed and the bowl is covered with a cover in the middle of which there is a hole, and the tip of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then perform the fumigation above; and have the patient place his mouth on the tip of the cannula, and hold his mouth closed so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the bloodsucker, for the bloodsucker will immediately fall.
And if it does not fall, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and let him eat salty foods and onions, and do not let him drink water, and thus it is necessary that with this treatment it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a bloodsucker is treated from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps in some ways, except that it has some curvature in it. Which is placed on the throat, and its tip is similar to a stork's beak, which is smooth, on which it is sharpened with a file, so that when we grasp something, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many types, in many ways, according to their appearance, each one described in this book.
And they are diverse in their opening and incision in two ways, of which one is according to the type of abscess itself; and according to the moisture we find in them.
And the second way is according to the locations in the body where they occur, for an abscess in the head is different from an abscess in the buttocks; and an abscess in fleshy areas is different from one in a joint; and for each of them there is a specific judgment for treatment.
And moreover, some abscesses should not be opened until the pus has matured within them or until they are fully developed.
Some other abscesses should be lanced while they are still inflamed and not yet fully developed, like abscesses near joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its presence prolongs until it affects the area around it, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are easily corrupted, causing contraction of that limb. Or it's near a major limb, and if you delay the lancing until it's fully matured, it might damage the major limb. Or if it is near the anus, then lance the unripe one;  but do not lance a raw, incompletely digested one, unless it's near the anus, for fear that it might penetrate into the surrounding areas of the anus and cause a fistula, or create a condition that won't heal. But you must know the time to lance the abscess, and that is when it is mature.
And you know it is mature when there is a cessation of pain and fever, and a decrease in swelling and throbbing, and the clarity of the abscess head, and the other signs.
And therefore, you must make the incision in the area just below the abscess and from the pus that comes from draining the swelling.
And the incision should always be along the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in places away from tendons, nerves, arteries; and, finally, in all similar locations where there is no reflex; for in those that are reflexive, use an incision appropriate to that particular limb.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to let it be until the digestion of it is confirmed, according to the process we have described; for if you incise it before that, the course of the illness will be prolonged, and much filth will be released, and the abscess walls will become harder and deeper.
However, some abscesses must be incised across the width of the flesh or body, based on necessity, or in a way appropriate to the limb.
And you must make a small incision in small abscesses, and a large or wide incision, or multiple incisions, in large abscesses, according to the difference in size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses where the skin must be hollowed out and incised, when done this way and when it is in a state where it is already ready, similar to that which happens in case of large boils and carbuncles.
And some are incised with three-sided incisions; and some are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like abscesses of the joints.
And some of them are treated with round incisions and crescent-shaped incisions, and similar ones.
But those, where there is no such thing as deep abscesses, you should puncture them with a superficial, or simply flat, incision.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, pierce it and do not attempt to remove all the matter at once; having removed a portion, then close the abscess until the following day, and then remove a larger portion, and do this repeatedly according to a gradual process until it is fully drained.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person, for the animal spirit is often resolved with the expulsion of pus, and the patient often dies quickly, and you should operate with caution.
Observe this chapter with great caution, and regarding your incision for these abscesses, you must cleanse the sores and consider whether the abscess wound is small or a simple incision. Then apply ointments of lead or of old cotton.  If the abscess is large and the fissures of the incision are numerous, you must apply medicaments to each opening until they unite.
If a portion of the skin has been cut away from the abscess, or if you have created a hollow around it, then you must fill it with old linen or cloth strips, like when removing crusts or scabs, without moisture.  Hold this in place for three days. Afterward, treat it with appropriate ointments until healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your procedure, then apply cold water and vinegar mixed with a linen cloth, and place this over the bleeding area repeatedly.
However, if the bleeding persists, you must apply the regimen and cures and powders mentioned in many places throughout this book and its divisions.
But if it is wintertime, and the abscess is in a location with many nerves, then you must infuse powders in warm wine and oil, and apply them to the affected area.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you must apply powders mixed with water and oil or with wine and oil, and keep this cool until the third day, as we have said.  Then you must drain the abscess and cleanse it, and administer the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the understanding of the treatment of abscesses in general, but according to the method of differentiation I have already discussed, each type of abscess has its own treatment method detailed in specific chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses which appear on the neck of the head.
Small abscesses form on the neck of the head, and these are of various types of nodes containing tissues that are characteristic of them; and their various natures are numerous.
And some contain pus, and these contain moisture similar to lime. And some contain moisture similar to dust, and similar to them; and some are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, as extracting them does not cause contrary injury.
And the surgical procedure for cutting these is that you probe them with an instrument shaped somewhat like this, which is called a "probe," until you know what they contain.
If what it contains is moisture, then you divide it with an incision along its length by a superficial incision in this way:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Then, once the moisture has been evacuated, you scrape the skin that contained the moisture, and remove anything remaining, for it may return if any remains.
Then, soak some cotton in Egyptian unguent, if available, or in water and salt otherwise; and fill the wound with this and leave it until the next day, for this will corrode any remaining parts of the skin.
And then apply more Egyptian unguent to that cotton a second and third time, if necessary, until you are confident that no part of the skin remains; then treat the wound with unguent until healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you should incise it in this manner: with an incision shaped like a cross, and place hooks between the two sides of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your strength in removing the membrane that contains it.
But if your effort encounters resistance, then do as I told you initially.
And similarly, do the same for a stony abscess in terms of incision and treatment as we have told you; and incision on a stony abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these abscesses that are on the head of some men, lack moisture altogether; the reason is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if someone were struck with it, it would hurt.
However, for other accidental abscesses on the head, this is not the case, as for abscesses that appear near the heads of children and the roots of their ears, but the incision for all of these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those from the lowest part of them, so that the body is relieved of the blood/pus in the lower parts.
And then treat them with whatever is appropriate for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Of the incision of scrofulas that occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses appear in the neck and armpits and groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.

Chapter 1 of the abscess that arises from a nerve twisting.

Chapter 52.
Of the treatment of a protrusion of the navel.

And then treat it with astringent wine until it is healed.
Chapter 57.
Concerning the cleansing of children and the treatment of complications arising from circumcision.
Circumcision is not a simple break in the skin like other wounds; however, since it is a procedure we perform and one that is specifically for children, it is necessary to devise the best and easiest method of treatment to promote healing.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not describe circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws.  This is only because, through experience, we have found that most craftsmen and barbers use circumcision and cleansing with a small knife and forceps; and they use a bandage with wool and incision with the fingernail.
And I have already tested all these methods, and I find no better cleansing than with scissors, and a bandage with wool, than cleansing with the fingernail.  For often the foreskin peels back further because the foreskin has two layers, and the inner layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision, causing further pain and discomfort. And with the cleansing done by a method using a type of scraping tool, and done digitally, which shoemakers use, the incision on the tip of the male member is not reliably made, because it easily slips into the opening of the member.
But in the operation using a fingernail, it easily slips and damages your operation. And the foreskin of the child is naturally short, and often born in such a way that it needs no cleansing, and I have seen that.
But the cleansing done with scissors and a bandage of wool, its goodness, experience has shown me, because the scissors are for a proportional incision, because the sharpness above is as sharp as that below;  and therefore, when you press your hand there, you have a proportionate double sharpness, and it cuts in a way that is comparable in one motion.  And a compression of the wool is applied in a way that encloses the foreskin of the penis from every side, and with it, there's no possibility of any error at all.
And the first method of the operation is that you should position the child, especially if it's one that understands a little, so that you only bind the wool around its penis and leave it there until the next day.
And then make it joyful in whatever way is possible for it, based on what its intelligence receives. And then position it between your hands, carefully, and let nothing be visible; and conceal the scissors in your hand, or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other instruments.
And then place your hand on its penis, and blow into the foreskin, and pull it back so that the head of the penis is exposed, and then clean it from any adhering dirt.
The shape of the scissors for cleaning/cauterization, this is the shape:
One end should be blunt, and the other sharp, so that the shank of the nail doesn't come out, and the length of the two blades should be as long as two handles together.
But the mistake in cleaning/cauterization is when you forcefully pull the foreskin completely, or part of it, away from the incision.
Therefore, you must stretch that with your finger beforehand, before opening the abscess, and cut it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your finger, then pull it out with forceps and cut it.
However, if three days have passed, and that which is swollen beneath the penis remains abscessed, then you should let it be until the abscess has subsided and gently open it, and cut it according to what is prepared for you.
And beware of the head of the penis, for that will not help him.
And then heal it with that which consolidates the wound from the powders which we have recounted in the treatise on powders.
And if it is cut from the cord excessively, and you treat the upper parts, that will not help him.
And further treat it with what we have said until it is healed.

And then slowly pull out the cloth with the wool, little by little, for the urine will soak the wool.

Chapter 60.
Of the extraction of stones.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones formed in the kidneys and stones formed in the bladder, and the signs of each.

And the stone formed in the bladder is properly that which is located within the bladder.
And I am narrating this with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore, I say that stones formed in the bladder mostly affect children; and among the signs of this are that the urine passing from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs their penis, and often groans, and is often bent over; and with this, the anus is often constricted in these cases.
And the cure is easier for children up to fourteen years of age; and the cure is difficult for the elderly. But in young people, it is more manageable because it is among them. (Or:  But in young people, it is more manageable because it is contained within them.)
And in the case where the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to arrive at a cure for this, it is necessary at the beginning to cleanse the patient with a clyster which removes all the feces in their bowels; for that blockage sometimes hinders the expulsion of the stone from the body.
And then the patient should be placed with their feet raised and gently massaged from below so that the stone descends to the bladder area or, in some cases, jumps from its position.
And then make this incision (or procedure) between your hands, and the patient's hands should be positioned under their thighs, so that the entire bladder is positioned to slope downwards.
And then remove that (the stone) and all the rest (of the material) from there.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the space, then you immediately cut around it.
And if it doesn't completely descend through your manipulation, then you should lubricate your left index finger (if the patient is a child) or middle finger (if the patient is a fully grown person) with oil and insert it into their anus. And push the stone until it is below your finger. And move it slowly until it reaches the neck of the bladder.
And then press on it with your finger, and expel it to the outside of the area where you extracted it.
And instruct the assistant to press on the bladder with their hand; and instruct another assistant to use their right hand to pull the genitals upwards, and with their other hand to hold the penis (or scrotum - depending on the context of *codena*) which is below the genitals, at the point of the incision.
And then, insert the instrument properly, the shape of which is as follows: And cut through the flesh between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be made on that stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let a transverse incision be made, so that the external incision is wide and the internal one narrow, according to the amount of protrusion of the stone that's possible, and not larger, for if you strongly press your finger in the anus, the stone will be pressed towards the incision, and it will be ejected without difficulty.
And know that there is some stone in it, with angles and edges, which makes its removal difficult because of this.
And that which is of this stone is like a gland, round, and easily removable.
Therefore, if it is one with angles and edges, it is wedged in the fissure.

But if you cannot remove it, then you enlarge the opening a little, and if you see blood flowing, you cut it while pressing it with ground vitriol.
But if the stones are more than one, then initially you push the hand towards the mouth of the bladder, and then cut upon it; and then push a little further; and do likewise if there are more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is foolish for you to cut it with a large incision, for you cause the patient one of two things: either death or a continual urine leakage, because the area is not completely healed.
But administer expulsion of it until it is ejected, or apply force to its fracture with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
So, when you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with aloe juice and dragon's blood, and press it down. And place over it a cloth steeped in oil and wine, or in rubbed oil and cool water, so that a hot abscess may be relieved.
And then lay the patient on their back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day.
And when you have removed that, embrocate the area with water and oil, and then treat it with diafinicon ointment and diabazilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound a hot, burning, and corrosive abscess develops, and it resembles the blood being congealed in the bladder, obstructing urination (and this is known by the blood appearing in the urine), then insert your finger into the wound and remove that blood, for if it remains, it will cause corruption of the bladder and subsequent putrefaction.
And then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with whatever other suitable remedies are appropriate for its treatment until it is healed.

of triangular tip, sharp, fixed in a shaft.

And then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the external side of the penis, for that is broken and is forced out with the urine, and the patient is healed.

Chapter 61.
Of the extraction of stones from women.

But if you are deprived of such a woman, then seek a skillful and subtle male physician, or present a matron well-versed in women's ailments, or a woman who has expertise in this art.

Then make an incision at the junction of the vagina near the thigh root, wherever the stone is lodged, and hold the stone in that area, ensuring the finger doesn't release the stone from the grasped position.

So you know how many types there are, so that you can specify what you want.

Therefore, when the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the area has healed, then you should resume your procedure until the stone is expelled.
Chapter 62.
Of surgery on a strangulated hernia.
An acute hernia is nothing other than a collection of moisture in the white sac, which is beneath the groin area containing the testicles, and it is called the sac.
And sometimes it is in the proper skin of the scrotum, which nature prepared in the region of the testicle, until it is seen that there is another testicle; and it is the groin region between the white sac that we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a pouch.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness that occurs in the testicles, because this material is released into them, and sometimes it occurs because of a blow inflicted on the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending toward yellow or bloody, or red, or black and foul-smelling, or watery and white, and this is what mostly happens.
And the signs by which it is known where this collection of water is are these: for if it is in the white sac that we have mentioned, then the swelling is round, sloping slightly lengthwise, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle appears, because the moisture surrounds it from all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper skin of the scrotum, then the swelling is round with a part resembling the testicle, and for this reason one believes it to be another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the fold of the testicles and the white sac, then it will feel like a lump below.
And if you wish to know the color of the moisture, then you should test the color of the moisture in the swelling with a slender probe whose shape is known beforehand; and according to what comes out on the probe, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to treating it with iron, it is necessary to instruct the patient to be bled, if possible, and to see if their body is full.
And afterward, let him lie on his back on something slightly elevated, and place soft cloths beneath him.
And then, sit on the patient's left side, and instruct the attendant to sit on the right side, extending their staff to one of two places: the crease of the testicles and the area near the navel of the abdomen.
And then, take a broad spatula and split the crease of the testicles lengthwise from the middle to near the pubic bone.
And let the incision be made along a straight line equidistant from the line that would cut the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white scrotum encompassing it.
And scrape that area, observe it, and be careful not to cut it; and the scraping you perform should only be done gently and subtly with the lightest possible scraping, according to the quantity that is feasible for you.
And then pierce the water-filled scrotum with a wide opening, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with forceps, and extend the scrotum outward; do not touch the crease of the testicles, and cut the scrotum in whatever way is possible for you. And cut it entirely or in pieces, paying particular attention to its thin edges, for if you do not carefully examine the incision, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
However, if the testicle protrudes from its pouch during your procedure, then after completing the incision in the scrotum, return it inside, and then stitch the two edges of the scrotum's pouch with sutures, and then treat it like other wounds until it heals.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another illness, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the supporting structure, remove the testicle, and then treat it as we have described.
But if the fluid is collected in two separate areas, for example, know that there are two hernias; therefore, cut that area as you did the other hernia; and if it seems to you to be one operation, then perform it as one.
And then you should clean away the blood, and in between the incisions place wool soaked in oil or oil mixed with something else; then place another layer of wool soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the genitals and the belly area; and place over it folded cloths and powders, and bind them together with a bandage in this way:
And then take two cloths and fill them with wool, prepared in this manner; and have their ends bound with cotton or damp wool, and attach them with a bandage to the bracers of the breeches, and one end should reach from the buttocks to the groin, and the other end reach to the thigh area, and two ends on the lower thighs, and two other ends on the knees, and all the bandages should be fastened at the bracers of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with a cautery instead of cutting with an iron.  This means that you should take a sharp, knife-like cautery iron and cut through the skin of the genitals with it, and heat it as we have described until the white cyst containing the fluid is exposed.  Then you should take a cautery iron in this shape: And it resembles a Greek letter.
And then pierce through that cyst, and heat it until all the moisture is expelled.
And then pull the cyst open with hooks, and scrape it with hooks, and scrape it with a heated, sharp cautery; and cut it as best you can until it is removed; and be careful that the fire doesn't burn it.
And know that this operation is safer and less prone to bleeding, and better than an operation with cutting.
However, if you encounter an abscess during the incision or cauterization, or bleeding, then you must treat whatever difficulty arises with a treatment appropriate to it, as previously described.
But if the patient is weak, and cannot tolerate the second operation because of its difficulty, then administer a puncture as I recommend; and this is when you are certain, from what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is acute.
Then you must have the patient sit on a raised stool, then puncture the abscess in the lowest part of the body with a sharp instrument, and make the puncture along the length of the body.
And let the opening be wide enough so that all the water drains out.
And then place a cloth over the area, bandage it, and leave it, so that the remaining water drains out. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is resistance to the outflow of water at that time, you puncture it: and this is done only because the white membrane in the wound's opening causes resistance and prevents the water from escaping.  Then you must place a poultice or instrument, similar to those used to remove water from dropsy (as described earlier), on the wound, or gently compress the wound.
And know that the water returns, and is collected again approximately every six months or thereabouts; and when it is collected, it should be punctured in the same manner, for the patient will thus pass their days.
Chapter 63.
Of the incision on a fleshy hernia and its cure.
Know that in the incision of this hernia, there is a fear of death in the most serious cases; therefore, I have observed the release of it and its being saved by this method.
And I remember the operation on this and the types of abscesses.

And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And a hardened abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no sensation in it, is very serious.
And sometimes it is caused by the knotting of arteries, and by the swelling of those, as is already perceived in the memory of it, or by swelling of the veins.
But that which is caused by the swelling of arteries is recognized by the dispersal of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers; and it is not appropriate for it to be touched.
However, that which is caused by the swelling of a vein is not dispersed by any means due to compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that on this type [of abscess] a section can be made, and on the other flesh; and that you should incise the skin of the genitals, and then spread the sack outwards, and extract that from the white sack, and free the ligaments of the vessels, and tie them, and cut the ligaments further once you have freed it completely from all parts of the sack.
Therefore, if the sack is already with those hardened tissues, then it is necessary that you extract the sack and cut it.
And if there is adhesion to any part of the sack, or between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and cut it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the location of the adhesion, which is behind, then it is necessary that you cut all that which is from it, and extract the sack just as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool steeped in olive oil and wine; and then treat that with the other treatment until it is healed.

Of the treatment of an inguinal hernia.

Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture that develops in the extended diaphragm over the abdomen, by some of the intestines of the abdominal cavity being pushed through the rupture.
And this rupture is either of the diaphragm or a rupture in it, and these two types occur due to many causes: either a blow, or a fall, or a cry, or the lifting of some heavy object, or similar events.
And the sign of this, when it is due to a stretching of the diaphragm, is that it occurs gradually over a long period, and not suddenly, and it is a swelling that gradually moves towards the navel, because the diaphragm is pressing the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a rupture of the diaphragm, is that initially it causes sudden, intense pain; and there is a diverse swelling appearing under the nearest ribcage, and this is due to the intestine being pushed out, and the body of the intestine is on the outside of the diaphragm.
And sometimes the intestine is pushed out with a part of the membrane/tissue, and this is called a strangulated intestinal hernia.
And sometimes it is caused by wind, and sometimes a tear develops in the intestine, and it is trapped there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes wicked pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of the species of this illness with iron there is fear, for which it is necessary to avoid using that method in this case.
And the way to perform the operation is to instruct the patient to push the intestine back into its cavity with their hand if it is possible to return it, and then place it over their abdomen between your hands, and elevate their legs.
And then extend the sheath, which is the sheath of the testicles, upwards, and cut the sheath of the testicles entirely lengthwise; and then insert two blades of the scalpel into the cut according to your need to open the incision with them.
And if the incision, according to the amount that is possible for you, allows the testicle to be removed.
And then scrape away the tissue that is beneath the testicle sheath, until the hard white tissue of each part is exposed.
Then you insert your finger into the area surrounding the testicle, the area between the white tissue that is beneath the testicle's sheath, and the surrounding tissue, and separate any adhesion that is on the back side of the testicle.
Then hold the testicle sheath with your hand outward, and with this, pull the white tissue upward with your left hand, and lift the testicle with the tissue toward the incision; and command the assistant to pull the testicle upwards, and you detach any adhesion on the back with a suitable solution.
And check with your fingers that there is no cause for the intestine to be wrapped within the hard white tissue. But if you find any such cause, then you push it back into the abdomen.
Then cut into the area below the sheath of the testicles, so that blood and pus flow out, as we have described previously in what we have said.
Then apply wool soaked in oil to the wound; and apply the bandage as described (and sometimes the white tissue is cauterized, which we mentioned near the incision to prevent blood flow, because it frequently happens).  Leave the bandage until it falls off by itself.
However, if the swelling is delayed, then warm water should be applied, and left on it until it falls off, as we have said.
And then treat the wound with other methods for treating wounds until it is healed.

Of windy hernia.

Of the disruption that occurs in the joints.
