And cauterization is better in this location, for it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
Of softening the hardness of the testicles.
Often the hardness of the testicles in some men is softened because the tissues surrounding them are softened, and it is trustworthy to observe this.
Therefore, it is necessary for whoever is tasked with the cure to have the patient lie down in the appropriate position, and gently separate all the hardened tissue from the natural tissue; and then to bring the two edges together with stitches.
And if you wish, stitch the excess of softened tissue at the beginning with three or four firm stitches.
And then make incisions between the stitches; and then treat with remedies for other wounds until it is healed and the pus comes out.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration, in our law, is forbidden, but it is permissible for me to mention it in this book because of two reasons. The first is for the knowledge of the physician when questioned about it, so that he may know the treatment for those who suffer it. The second reason is that we often need to castrate other animals for our own benefit, such as oxen, goats, pigs, and similar beasts.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by slitting or cutting.
But in that which is done by crushing, the method is to immerse the beast in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed, and hang down. And then grasp them with your hand until they are broken and no longer appear below the touch.
But in castration done by slitting and cutting, you must hold the beast and press the scrotum with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut into each testicle with a cut until both testicles are separated.
And then cut them further, after you have each peeled them. And do not leave on those of the scrotum any cause, but only the subtle scrotum which is over the veins.
And this method of castration, which is done by attrition, is better, for strong attrition leaves no cause in the testicles for the beast to desire mating.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men comes in two ways: one is when there appears in the scrotum area, between the testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, with hair on it.
And sometimes, from that area, urine flows from the scrotum.
But in women, it is a single type: and it is that the vulva of the woman on the pubis appears as if two testicles of a man, somewhat small, are on the outside, in the same way as a man's foreskin and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: for two types in men and one type in women. And it is necessary that you cut the joined flesh until the pressure is relieved, and then treat it with the cure for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, concerning urine in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of swelling and protruding flesh due to female causes.
A slight swelling is connected to natural causes until it is tightened and its care is made easy; and sometimes it becomes so large in some women that it spreads as in men and protrudes until the act of sexual intercourse.
Then you should hold the excess swelling with your hand or forceps and cut it.
And do not overdo the cutting, especially in the area of the root, so that you do not cause a flow of blood.
And then treat it with the treatment for sores until it is healed.
But flesh that is born in the womb of the mother until it fills it, and that has strongly emerged from the parts externally like a tail (and for this reason some earlier ones would call this “tailed disease”); then it is necessary that we cut it as we cut swelling and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Of the treatment of elephantiasis.
Elephantiasis is when the vagina of a woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to an illness that has preceded it, and this is caused either by poorly aligned flesh or by subtle or thick adhesions.
And it is located either in the mother's groin, or in her ribs, or in her highest back, or in her lowest back, and you prohibit cooking, pregnancy, and childbirth, and you strongly prohibit menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by observing the midwife, if the obstruction is apparent, promptly; but if it is not apparent, then you search for it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin adhesion, and is close to the two lips, then you should cook and break it apart.
And you should place powders on the two lips, and then place your index fingers from both hands on top.
And the woman should lie on her back, her thighs extended, and then forcefully extend the two lips until that thin adhesion is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it on the area, and let her rest with a man during the day so that the incised area does not heal.
But if the adhesion is thick and dense, then it is necessary that you cut it with a sharp spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to a clot, then lift that with forceps, and cut it.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing harm, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and gum arabic, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep that in place for several days, and apply a poultice of flaxseed.
And then it should be treated with another healing treatment until it is healed.
And sometimes accidents happen to the surrounding flesh, and in those cases, it is necessary to make incisions according to this method. But if it is due to a brain abscess, the brain abscess in the head should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and of the cutting and red swelling that occurs in the female genitals.
Hemorrhoids are a collection of many veins that continually leak blood; and when these hemorrhoids are old and are causing pain.
And I have already described in the division the types of those [hemorrhoids] and their signs, and will now describe which ones receive treatment and which do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and their pain, when located deep within the body and not apparent to the touch, cannot be treated with iron.
But those which are on the surface of the body, where the touch can feel them, are those that can be cured.
And you must place the woman in a cool place, and then carefully cut them away with scissors or a rough cloth, and cut off those parts with the roots.
And then powder over them the flow of blood from one of the incisive powders without biting, like acacia and similar ones.
And then soak their feet for an hour, and then place that in cool water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make that soak in a decoction of sumat, and of the bark of pomegranates and quince, and of similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine, and make a plaster with that over their back.
And if the blood is stopped, good; and if not, apply two leeches, and to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice, or arnica juice, or  mulberry juice mixed with diluted wine, and apply it to the affected area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a bone injury in the arm.
Many types of abscesses can occur in the arm, such as tumors, bone abscesses, ulcers, hardened swellings, spreading sores, fistulas, hemorrhoids, deep sores, and other abscesses. And I have already described these illnesses and their types, their symptoms, and their treatments in the division.
And it is necessary that I describe in this treatise the specific abscess that appears on the arm, when it is among the abscesses that collect pus, and if you want to know how the operation with iron is performed.
Therefore, you should consider whether the pain of the abscess at its onset is sharp, with percussion, phlegm, and fever, and if there is redness and sensitivity when you touch it, then do not try to pierce it. Treat it with what aids maturation until the severity of the condition subsides.
Then you should lay the woman on a cot with two supports; and have her lean on her two supports and place her feet near the lower part of her abdomen, and have her legs spread, and place her arms beneath her elbows, and bind each one with a suitable bandage.
Then the midwife, standing on the right side, administers the instrument with which the opening in the arm is made, the shape of which will be clear from the procedures to follow.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you should insert it into the arm of the woman with the shaft, and the part of the instrument that enters the arm should only be the shaft and the handle, and comfort the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the arm's shaft, then you must place powders on the surface of the arm's shaft so that the instrument is prevented from entering the arm's shaft.
And you should place the liquid that flows through the instrument's shaft on the upper side, and the assistant should hold the instrument and turn the liquid until the opening of the arm is made.
And when the discharge appears, and it is touched with the hand and is sticky and wet, then you must dry that area around his head with a linen cloth.
And when the pus has been evacuated, then you should place a piece of cloth soaked in rosemary oil or green oil, in which some astringent has been added, and put the cloth on the affected area on the arm.
And place outside the arm, and over the armpit, a clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be treated for approximately two days with an ointment suitable for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the arm and the wound are washed in such a way that it is cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lily or long-stalked thistle has been boiled, and then it returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep within the arm, then you should be prohibited from treating it with iron, but should heal it with what we have discussed in the division [or section].
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat living embryos when they develop in a non-natural way.
It is necessary for the midwife to recognize the signs of natural childbirth from the beginning.  This means that when you see the woman pushing from below and yearning to breathe air, and she is relieved by what is upon her during labor, and hastens the birth of the embryo, then know that this birth is in the natural form, with the birth of the embryo occurring above the head, and the afterbirth either hanging or suspended from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then the abdomen should be compressed so that the embryo descends easily.  For when it descends above its head, it releases the afterbirth, and is freed of excess matter with proper cleansing.
However, if the birth is different—not natural and not commendable—it is when the embryo emerges on its feet, and its body emerges before its head and feet, or its one hand or foot emerges, or its head emerges but a hand or foot or its belly, and strongly turned over its neck.  Such forms are considered unpraiseworthy.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be knowledgeable and subtle in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what ingenuity is in them so that it may be understood and be established upon them.
When the birth has emerged above its head by natural expulsion, and this is difficult for the woman and is occurring with the woman's strength already weakened, then apply this treatment, or what women use, and evaporate the substance from it with gathered fenugreek and moist oils.
And then the midwife should insert her fingers into the small space and fissure with it the afterbirth, or cut it with her nail until it releases any moisture within it. And press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be treated with a poultice of ptilium and a poultice of fenugreek with cumin.
And then command him, the attendant, to compress her and make her sneeze with warmth; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled soon.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at first, then it is necessary that you gently reduce them little by little. But if you do not succeed in the process, then place the woman on a stool and lift her feet up, and then lower the stool towards the ground; and the woman must be held so that she does not fall during the movement.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then cut them and extract the remaining parts of the fetus or tie the body to his hand with a cloth and extract it with care, for it will be expelled.
When the fetus is positioned on its feet, then you must push it upwards, and then turn the fetus gently, and then grasp it, and then take one of its feet and gently extend it.
But when it has emerged between the thighs, then you tell him to strain, and make him sneeze with the proper procedure, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have described, then you gently return the fetus until it resumes its natural position, for it will then be expelled with an easy exit.
And if it is impossible with all that we have described, then take marshmallow and dissolved gum, pound them well in a mortar, and then apply this mixture to the vulva, the lower part of the woman's abdomen. And then apply a poultice in lukewarm water, until the hypochondria (lower ribs/sides) are relieved.
And when the parts below are soothed, then make a fumigation with myrtle and let him inhale it. And when the fumigation has been applied for an hour, then place him on a stool, make him sneeze, hold his mouth, lift him up and gently press the lower part of his abdomen, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
The fetus emerges lengthwise, hanging by one of its hands. If it can be reduced by hand, then do so. But if you cannot reduce it by hand, then lift the woman and let her walk.
And if walking is not possible, then lay her on a bed, and forcefully compress her thighs, and subsequently administer remedies of which I have already spoken about, consisting of mucilages.
And if labor has begun, then have her sit on a stool, then study the reduction of the hand, and gently pull the fetus into its natural position, and make the woman sneeze, and command her to strain until it is expelled.
The fetus is positioned on the mother's pelvis, its hands outstretched, and its face is towards the mother's back. The midwife should grasp the baby's hand and gently and easily turn it.
And when it is properly positioned, then apply to its forehead what we have previously mentioned: oil and mucilages.
Then command her to gently move, and when you have done this, have her sit on a stool and command her to lean to her right side, and then command her to strain; and make her sneeze with condiments. The prepared fetus is positioned on its side.
When the mother is freed from the ordeal in this way, consider this, and then you sever the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended over the face and neck, then you sever it from the navel, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus into the parts within until you place it as is appropriate, and tell the woman to compress herself several times, and the fetus will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
Delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are born frequently, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and difficult to achieve; but these are things outside nature, and not living things.
And count those, whether they have multiplied, a second time, and separate them by the umbilical cord of each one, and sometimes they are expelled as easily as a single fetus by a natural expulsion, and sometimes this occurs in a manner not worthy of praise, as we have reported.
And if some of them are difficult during delivery and reach the point we've discussed about unnatural figures, then treat them as we have described.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is ingenious in every way, in accordance with the preparation of the situation for a beneficial outcome.
A recollection of what forms in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even ten are formed in the mother's womb.  It has been verified by me that one woman miscarried six, and another ten, all fully formed.  This cause was determined by the wise physicians' examination.
And all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife, for it is very easy to recognize that small likeness of it, and therefore one must act accordingly.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus using what we have discussed in the division of medicine, and it has not emerged naturally, and you are forced to use manual extraction, then you must consider if the woman is of healthy constitution and not suffering from any of the conditions that might cause concern about her loss.
Then the woman must be positioned on her back on a bed, and her head should be inclined downwards, and her thighs elevated.
And the attendant should hold the woman in place on both sides, or bind her to the bed, so that her body does not move during the extraction of the fetus.
And then, moisten the mother's mouth with moist oils and with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where she can hook her fingers into the fetus.
And suitable places for this: then consider if the fetus is ready to descend on its head, and then you hook your fingers into its neck, or its head, or its mouth, or its palate, or below its chin, or into its fork, or into the appropriate places on the sides, or below the ribs.
But if the descent is on its feet, then in the perineum, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the fingers with the right hand, and place the curve of the hand in that place that is between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and place the fingers in any of those places that we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty space.
And then place and hook another finger, or a third if necessary, in order to achieve a drawing motion, and not just a pulling motion according to its own direction.
And it should be moved in every direction, so that its extraction is eased, just as a tooth is eased during its removal.
And it is necessary in the area between them that the stretching be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary for the midwife to anoint some of her fingers with oil and introduce these instruments and rotate that which is retained.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the fingers should be moved to a higher location; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
However, if one hand has emerged before the other, and it is not possible to return it by means of the constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until the whole is expelled, and cut it either at the wrist or at the elbow.
And thus it is necessary to do the same for the other hand and the two feet.
But if the fetus's head is large, or constriction occurs during delivery, or water is collected around the head, then it is necessary to insert instruments between the bones of the skull and break the head open and remove the water, or to cut that open with an instrument called an "alundat", the form of which will be found with the shapes of other instruments in the chapter immediately following this one.
And similarly, do this if the fetus has a large head naturally: and then it is necessary to cut it with an algematu, or to take it with the alunda as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is protruding and is constricted towards the fork, then it should be cut open until the moisture within the feet is drained.
But if it is not complicated, nor impeded by delivery, then you cut the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscessed or hydrops, then you must perforate it until the moisture within it is drained.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then the pull of it is gentle and the direction of it towards the mother's mouth is gentle.
But if it is constricted towards the belly and the feet, then you must remove it with a cloth that you have wrapped around your hand, and cut the belly and feet until what is within them is expelled.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head remains, then the midwife should insert her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she should insert her hand into the space around the air, and search for the head with it, and pull it with the fingers towards the mother's mouth, and then insert a finger, or two fingers, and pull it with them.
And if the mother's mouth is obstructed by a hot abscess that afflicts her, then it should not be treated with harshness; instead, a medicinal application of fatty, moist substances should be administered first. And administer poultices; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fet (presumably a tumor or similar growth) is on its side, then if possible, it should be cut out; and what we have said about a living fet (i.e., a fetus) should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and removed.
And it is necessary that you leave no cause within the second-skin (presumably a membrane or covering).
And when you have completed all your operation, then administer what we have said about the treatment of boils, which occur from the mother (i.e., a similar affliction as that which the mother had).
And if a flow of blood occurs, then immerse in astringent waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose belly a stillborn child was formed, and shortly afterward pregnant again, and a stillborn child was produced again. A long time ago, an abscess occurred near his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and medicated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied to him some plaster of strong attraction, and one bone came out of him, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was very surprised, as there are no bones in the belly.
And then I thought they were bones from the stillborn child.
Then I treated the wound, and removed many bones from him, the woman being in her usual state of health, and she lived in this way for a long time, discharging a small amount of pus.
And I did not consider this strange, but rather that in this lies knowledge and assistance to what the skillful physician administers with his hands for healing.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of vertigo with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of constriction in which the books are bound, to which binding are two twists at the two ends of two cords. However, this twist should be more subtle than a constriction, and should be made either of a reed or of a piece of wood.
And the width of each piece of wood is two fingers, and the thickness of each is one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood there are two joints of the same wood, which are already fastened, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mother's mouth, so that it may be opened with those when you turn the two prescribed twists.
Another form similar to that, more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of reed wood or wood, resembling forceps. However, at its end, there are two additions, just as before, and the length of those additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with those, then you have the woman lie on the bed with her feet hanging down, opening what is between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument, as if using shears, according to the amount you want to open the mouth of the mother, until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients described:

A method of grasping/hooking, having a spine/point.
A method of grasping/hooking with two spines/points.

All those instruments, and the more the types are multiplied and prepared by the artisans, the more easily they are to use and the more esteemed they are by men due to their quantity.

Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after delivery, then you should instruct the patient to hold their breath, and then make her/him sneeze with some stimulus, and press your hand on the mouth and nostrils, and if by this it is expelled, well and good.
And if not, then take a bowl and pierce a hole in the cover, and place in it herbs that open the mouth of the mother, such as balm, rue, anise, chamomile, Arabic cicadas, cinnamon, and St. John's Wort. All these herbs, or some of them, should be immersed in water in the bowl, and place that over the fire.
And then place a reed tube in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the steam reaches the cavity of the mother.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it has not been expelled, then you order the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or in marigold mixture, and then introduce it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it.  And when you have found it, take it and pull it out slowly.  But if it is attached to the wall of the mother's womb, then insert her hand as we have described, and when she has found the afterbirth, remove it little by little as directed so that the mother does not suffer from excessive pulling. Before that, it is necessary that it be moved from side to side with ease.
And if the mother's vagina is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and with herbal poultice application.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then be careful not to exert further pulling.  However, it is necessary that what has come out of her vagina, lies on the woman's thigh, and then treat it with ointment and a four-ingredient medicine.  For that will cause it to decompose in a few days, dissolve, and be expelled.
However, when it decomposes, a bad odor rises from the head and stomach, and this afflicts the patient; and then fumigations and suitable remedies must be administered.
However, some of the ancients have already tested fumigations with dried narcissus and figs.
Form of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and the afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or earthenware.
And the narrow end should be placed in the container, and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed over the flames of the fire.
And let whoever is in charge ensure that the fumigation is consumed and replace it with another.
Of the treatment of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which are already small and tight. Then the midwife should pierce that tightness with her finger; and if not, pierce it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the sphincter.
And then place some wool soaked in wine and oil over it; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear it is obstructed, then place a tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wants to digest.
And sometimes blockage of the anus occurs due to the consolidation of a plague or abscess; then it is necessary for that consolidation to be incised, and then treated with what we have said about the cure and the lead.
Of the cure of the fistulas that develop afterward.
The fistulas that form in the lower area, with pus, swollen and thick, which develop near the anus externally or within the space of one side.
And it is a fistula or multiple ones.
And when that swelling is longstanding and comes from watery white moisture or subtle putrefaction, and sometimes these fistulas are penetrating.
And those that are penetrating are known by what comes out of them through discharge and windiness; and sometimes the patient experiences discharge, and strong worms come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas extend into the bladder and into the opening of the penis; and some of these penetrating ones extend to the joint of the thigh and into the bone marrow of the thigh.
And how a penetrating fistula into the anus is recognized, compared to a non-penetrating one, is that you insert your index finger into the anus, and gently probe the fistula with a thin instrument of iron or bronze when there is no twisting/tortuosity.
But if there is twisting/tortuosity in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you don't feel anything at all, nor does any discharge, worm, or windiness come from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then your efforts are towards a cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the opening of the penis, then the indication is to restrict urination there, and to apply medicine so that the area is healed.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone, and causing pain in the surrounding area, then the sign of it is that the probe can penetrate here, if there is no twisting into the bone, and pain in the surrounding area; and that it will likely be permanent; and that no medication will work, nor is it healed with ointments.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the cure of these is the work and vanity of foolish doctors, on which you should not rely.
But in the case of a non-penetrating and not chronic fistula, healing with iron is expected, as I have told you, and what I have already tested.
And it is necessary for the patient to lie on their back, raise their legs upwards, and bend the thighs towards their abdomen. Then, gently insert a lead probe, or an iron one if there's a twist in the fistula, until you know the exact location where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then it is necessary for you to insert your index finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe already penetrating through your finger, or if you feel anything besides flesh or tissue between your finger and the opening, then you know for sure that it is penetrating; and therefore, do not work on it, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
However, some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And the treatment by which recovery is hoped for is to take a small, thin cautery, as described before, and introduce it into the fistula until it reaches near the anus. Then repeat this two or three times until you know that all the flesh around the fistula, which resembles a bird's feather quill, has been cauterized.
Then treat it with some bandages until all the cauterized flesh has healed, and then treat it with medicines that promote flesh growth and healing. If it is healed, then it is good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate to your finger which is in the anus, and between it and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and the fistula runs along the surface of the anus, then cauterize the anus from the beginning of the fistula. You should cauterize along with the probe, pushing it into the fistula until the cauterization reaches the point where the probe's tip lies, and by the probe it is released and removed.
Then cleanse those fistula-ridden tissues which are clustered together like a bird's quill, and leave no cause of them remaining.
But if a flow of blood occurs between you and your procedure while you cauterize those tissues, then the best and quickest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire has two good effects: it cauterizes those joined tissues, and it cuts off the blood, drawing out the moisture.
Then treat it with bandages or strips of cloth until the wound begins to heal, and then treat the sore with ointments that promote the growth of tough flesh and healing.
And those remedies which involve astringency and excision; when it is thus restored, it is healed and you need not fear a recurrence.
But if the fistula has reached the anus and is extending from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
And thus, if you feel a barrier of sinew or flesh between you and it, and it is close to the penetration, then there is no surgery on it, but instead, treatment via trust and hope.
The treatment for this is done in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or cut it until you reach its cavity, so that it is possible for you to insert medications and facilitate healing; and do not use too much cutting, so as not to injure the surrounding tissues, and thereby cause the patient harm or death unintentionally; and then treat it as we have said, for a swift cure will be made; or lengthen that membrane and cleanse the pus which you have cut until near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or another instrument with a sharp tip, and then cleanse those fistula-like tissues in the incision where you can, with cauterization; and then endeavor to repair the entire wound using incision, and if the opening above is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the anal region, the patient will be more vulnerable to further illness. And sometimes the fistula is treated in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the folds of the anus around the surface of the body with the string and along the margin of the anus, then use a probe with a pointed end, which is like a needle to prepare the tissues, in this manner:
and insert into that fold wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert the probe with a thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
However, if it penetrates the margin of the anus near the inside, then you pull the thread from that hole as if you inserted your finger into the anus; and pull out the end of the thread and tie the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when you further cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues are cut between the two ends of the thread and fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and sound.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever manner is prepared for you, but do not make it too deep, for it is not your task to do so, lest you injure the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the pointed spatula with which the fistula is opened:  the curve of which is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as often happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds, for either they are between the buttocks like lumps of charcoal, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of those there are small and large, and that blood runs from them constantly and is one, and are many, and are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the times of little humidity, from which flows yellowish water or a little blood with a continuous substance, and it is according to the color of the substance.
And the cure for those that are inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and squeeze until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grasp and suspend them with forceps or with your nail, and then cut them near their roots.
But if the forceps are not holding them due to their moisture and softening, then take them with a rough cloth or cotton or wool fleece, and extract them with your fingers, and then cut them and sprinkle some hot powders onto their cuts, so that they are there in place of cauterization; or cauterize them as it is described in the cauterization chapter. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not open, then prick the patient with a pricker that has a small bite, and wash it with whatever is in the anus, so that it opens slightly when the patient squeezes.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and it is that you grasp them with your nail, or grasp them with forceps, and cut them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And whoever you have cut, you should apply stitches to them in this manner: take a thread wrapped around it, thread that through a needle, then pull the hemorrhoids apart, and push the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread to the part below the needle, and do it latitudinally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a tight stitch, and then tie the thread and take out the needle.
Do this to all of them, and leave one of them unsewn, so that the excess blood flows from it.
And then place a cloth soaked in warmed oil or ointment over the anus; and place it over the area, and tell the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls out; and when it has fallen out, treat them with ointments or other remedies until they are healed.
And if they are old and no longer respond to that medicine, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or your fingernail until they are moist and the layer of pus and scabs on top, which prevents healing, is removed; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then you scrape them with a more forceful scraping than the first, until much blood comes and they are scraped clean; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a boil, or inflammation, is key, and it is a red, round swelling the color of the body, similar to a flower bud, and it appears throughout the body, especially in the parts below the feet and on the fingers, and it often causes pain in the region affected by the inflammation.
Then it is necessary to cut open what is in the area surrounding the boil, holding it with forceps or tweezers, and remove it from its root; and then treat that area.
And if you wish, cauterize it as described earlier in the chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round elevation on the body's surface, and it often appears on the hands of children.
And the treatment for it is to scrape or cut it, or to press it with a file or a needle until it bursts. And if you wish, cauterize this, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
However, a pimple is a small, rough or fistulated, large elevation on the body's surface, often occurring on the penis. When the cold touches it, it is felt like an ant bite, and it spreads around the body.
And the treatment for this is to take a hollow tube, like a hawk's or vulture's feather, that is strong, and place it over the pimple until the tube encircles it.  Then, rotate your hand with the tube until you cut what is inside the pimple's boundary and press it against the surrounding flesh to remove the pimple from the root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of bronze or iron according to this method:
The part above the tube should be thin, closed (meaning not perforated), wrapped so that the application of the tube and its rotation are facilitated.
And if you wish, cauterize the pimple as previously described in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cauterization.
Of the types of instruments used for giving treatment.
Sometimes treatment is done with silver or lead or cast or hammered bronze; and sometimes it's done with those small and large instruments, depending on the recipients.  That which is given to small children should be small; and that given to those with aching or sore bodies should have small treatments.
The form of large and small treatments:
The head of the cannula above it, where the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a clear demarcation where the bag is tied, like this.
And the lower end of it, which is inserted into the body, should be completely closed (that is, not open) very carefully, almost thin; and on one side of it there should be two openings, and on the other side one opening; and the size of the opening should be according to the thickness of the rays, and the largest one slightly smaller.
And the bag, in which the bladder medicines are placed, should be made of animal skin or sheepskin, and its size should be one palm.
And it should be pierced with many small holes, just as it is wrapped/rolled; and if the space between the holes is the thickness of a finger.
And then insert into those holes strong, pure (or strong, crude) oil, which should be ten or so oils; and attach the bag to that.  And when the bag is in place, tightly bind the head of the tube with a firm constriction between two marks, then tighten each hole with that [binding].  The important thing about using this binding is that you fill the bag with the medicine, making sure it is heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not too hot nor too cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back, raise their legs, and place a cloth or sheet underneath them; then, anoint the end of the cautery iron with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or similar; and then gently insert it into the anus.  Then the attendant takes the bag and squeezes it with both hands and firmly until all the material in the tube is expelled into the bowels; then remove the tube and the patient lowers their legs to the ground.
And if the medicine causes such an effect, hold it in place as long as possible; and if they sleep with it all night, it is better for healing.
However, if you use this method for ailments of loose bowels and expulsion of it (i.e., diarrhea), then you should repeat the cauterization until healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or festering sores in the anus, then the tube should be very thin and delicate, like the tubes used to cauterize children.
Of the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of plague treatment what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus the treatment of those with hand and with medicines according to the fulfillment (or, the completion/appropriateness).
Therefore, I say that plagues are diversified according to how the plagues are made and according to the place where the plagues occur.
And the causes why plagues are often made are like the throwing of a stone, or the incision of a sword or knife, or a blow with a spear, or a stick, or an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
But the plagues that are on parts of the body are like a plague that falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the liver, and similar to those of the members (or limbs).
And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues, so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the remembrance of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from the throwing of a stone or similar, and the skull has only been fractured, and it is a serious wound, and you fear the onset of a bad boil on the patient, then you should quickly bleed the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and draw from him blood according to his strength, if no other reason forbids the bloodletting, especially if the blood that has come from the wound is little.
And let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from strong food and drinks.
But if any reason prohibits bloodletting, then the diet should be lessened, and he should avoid meat and strong drink.
And apply to the wound, if a bad boil has occurred there, a poultice made from roasted oil or wine in which something astringent has been steeped.
But if you are sure of a bad boil, then apply to the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and if the air hasn't changed the powder, which I will shortly recommend.
And if the air has already changed that, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
However, if the wound is large or from a deep cut or similar, and its edges are not closed with stitches, then close them with sutures, as I will recommend later on in the suturing section.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendon, and there is no retention but a small suspension, then you should cut that, and then treat the wound with medicines, restoring the flesh soon in the place of the damaged area, and order the patient to eat foods rich in substance: heads, and shaped like the meat, until it is repaired.
And if you find putrefaction in the damaged area and it doesn't adhere to the bone, then you cut away all the rotten material and discard it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood might flow, and it is not severed by medicine, then you search for the artery, and if you find it unsevered, then you cut it with a scalpel, or tie it; and if necessity requires that what we have said does not work, then you cauterize until the flow of blood is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; therefore, you should sprinkle this powder on them, for it will consolidate them before the air alters them.
And the method for the powder is this: take one part olive oil, two parts dragon's blood, and three parts unquenched causs.  Grind it all finely and sift it, then fill the wound with it, and apply a good bandage so that it adheres well to the wound, and coagulates with the blood.
And then bandage it tightly with dressings and leave it, and do not remove it until it is suppurated, for the medicine will not be eradicated from there until it is healed.
And sometimes this works alone, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood at hand.
And this same operation is done on large wounds when it is appropriate to place it on them and bind them tightly, and in fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially in summer, then this powder is not suitable for their application. Instead, treat them with what we have already described, or apply digestive ointments to them, or dusts of barley flour with water and honey until they dry; and then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if the wound is accompanied by a fractured bone, and it is small, then you extract it with skill.
And I will describe the treatment of skull fractures in what follows.
Of wounds of the neck.
However, if the wound occurs on the neck, there is no difference in treatment compared to a simple wound of the head.
However, if the wound affects the neck's nerve or artery, if it's a nerve, no healing is possible unless the treatment avoids strong astringents, like alum and vitriol and similar things, as they damage the nerves and counteract it. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, for the substance of the nerves is cold and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is treated with gentle medicines, such as washing with fresh water, sometimes infused in oiled or un-oiled, and poultices when washed with fresh water and other minor stones following this method, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then apply stitches, or close its edges, and then treat it with powders until it heals.
But if the wound is deep or reaches the lower bone marrow, then pierce it at the lowest point.
But if the wound has already cut an artery and blood is flowing, then separate or cut that artery, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or all of them have been cut, and the veins of the neck are preserved, then you should bring the edges of the wound together with sutures along the spine of the neck and do not touch that area.  First align it, and return it to its natural position, then secure it with appropriate bandages, and leave it for a few days; then treat it with ointments containing astringents and stimulants until it is healed.
But if the wound has already been made in the bone, then you should clean it.  If there are bone fragments there, remove all you can; and those you can't remove, leave them until the wound becomes clean and the discharge from the wound is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle the powder recommended to you over it. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what the recollection will provide in its appropriate chapter.
If the wound is from a lance or knife, and you assess the depth of it, then consider if the wound is producing air when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is likely fatal.
And if there is no great depth, and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you put on the powder in the first hour, and do not press it so that the blood is not retained in the depth of the wound and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but apply an attractive ointment, and renew it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available, then you place a piece of old cloth over the wound opening to absorb the humours; and have the patient sleep on the wound, so that the collected fluids drain out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound, and the patient shows no spasms or tremors in the heart, nor constriction of the airways, and the other bodily conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with herbs and other cures until it is healed.
But if healing is impossible, and the wound remains open indefinitely, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore, treat it according to the chapter dedicated to fistulas.
And if the wound is caused by a cut from a knife or sword, and it is superficial on the foot or leg, then treat it with sutures if it is large, or with powders if it is small.
But if the wound has already penetrated the bone, and if pieces of it are protruding, then you should clean the wound and try to remove those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound can heal, for removing them will be less painful.
And for other wounds occurring on other limbs, the method of treatment is the same as what we have described.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The ailment that affects the abdomen is either severe, mild, or moderate.
A smaller rupture is more difficult to address in another way, and the restoration to its proper place is even more challenging if you delay the repositioning of the damaged parts promptly, for it opens and swells, making its reintroduction difficult.
Therefore, the best rupture is a moderate one, for in that case the repositioning of the intestines is not difficult, unlike the two types previously mentioned.
And understand that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two of these methods are common and applicable to the suturing of other parts of the body as well.
And two other specific methods are applicable to other parts of the body, although they are particularly appropriate in suturing the abdomen.
And I will explain each one to you with explanation and clarification.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has protruded through it, and it is difficult to return it to its place, this can be due to either the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, you should warm the area by soaking a sponge or damp cloth in warm water alone, or in water with squinant, cyperus, and spikenard, and apply this to the protruding intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes, doing this with a styptic substance is better than using only water in resolving the inflammation.
And when you see the inflammation is resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which marsh mallow and mallows have been boiled, for this alleviates the discomfort with little effort.
But if the discomfort is lessened by this treatment, then carefully cut the rupture slightly with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the initial treatment is better, when it is appropriate rather than a surgical incision, and there should not be an occupation to cut (the tissue) for unnecessary and harmful purposes.
Of the instrument, consider its shape:
One part of it is sharp, the other part blunt, and the end is slender, but not as slender as a spatula; rather, it should be slightly blunt.
And therefore, if the rupture is extensive and the intestine is out, then its return should be according to its natural form, into its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
However, if the rupture is wide and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you must have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you must raise their head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then always keep your attention and consideration on this, so that the part where the rupture is located is higher than the other.
And thus you should apply this in large and in medium ruptures.
And in small ruptures, place what you have prepared, and have the assistant hold the rupture completely with their hand and adjust the edges, and then ascertain if there is any reason for another stitch or suture nearby.
And this is the common method of suture: take a needle, or several needles depending on the size of the rupture; then place one at the edge of the rupture from the ring finger, and thread the needle, or others, so that you do not place the thread on the two edges of the cord with two edges of the ligature under the cord's inner portions, until you have made it penetrate from that part; and you have now joined two edges of the cord and two edges of the ligature, making four knots.
And then tighten with double thread around the needle, and sometimes from both sides together, and then join the two edges and the rupture by proper joining; and then leave the thickness of a finger and place another needle in it, and then tie that with thread in the same way as you did with the first needle; and do not stop doing this if necessary with the needles until you have completed the entire closure of the rupture.
And let the needles be medium in thickness and fineness, for very thin needles easily pierce the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the cord, and therefore they should be medium in fineness and thickness.
And the needle should not be placed too close to both ends of the cord, so that the flesh is not pierced easily and the rupture opens up before its consolidation.
And do not extend the sutures so that the rupture is prevented from healing.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not disturb the patient's sleep; and you will place on the suture, powders spread around on all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the rupture is already healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, for in their suture, one or two needles, or thereabouts, are sufficient.
However, the second suture is common: and it is that you bind with a suture the four extremities, of the cord and the two extremities of the strap, at one time, with a needle that is adequately wrapped, both in fineness and thickness.
And then, when with the needle you have pierced these four extremities, withdraw the needle from the same point from which you began, so that the thread retracts from the most superior part to the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth suture with which cloths are tightened.
And place between each suture the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture satisfies the men (or: this suture is known/acceptable to men).
And with such a suture, I closed a wound that had befallen a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the wound was more than a palm, and the intestines had protruded about two palms from the middle part of the intestine and the rupture was in the middle of the member; and then I returned the part of the intestine that had been outside the wound after 24 hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of 15 days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he was healed; and after that he lived for many years, and is alive and well.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and by a miracle it was, for I did not treat him with an ointment, for it was in a place where no ointment or any medicine could be found, but I placed old cloth over the wound twice a day and washed it assiduously with honey water until it was healed.
But I will tell you two kinds of sutures, proper according to the narration of Paraula.
And it is that you begin with a suture of the cord (or thread), and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And so, as the needle penetrates the cord (or thread) and the same wound along its length and width, you leave the tip of the thread where you inserted the needle; and push the needle from the inside to the outside at the other end of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again into the same wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this wound, and insert the needle from the inside to the outside at the other end of the wound.
And make penetration with that penetration of yours, with which you make that penetration into the thread at the end of the wound, which is on its side, until all of it penetrates.
And now, starting from this same side, sew it with the end of the thread on the other side, and pull out the needle from the thread it has pierced, and then return that to that thread and sew the end of the thread that is on the other side to this end of the wound; and pull out that from the thread which is on that side.
Do this once and again, and do this once more until you have sewn everything that is outside according to this example.
And the essence of this method of suture is that you sew with a skin stitch, the way you sew skin, so that you sew the thread once from the end of the thread, and then leave it once again until you have completed it.
However, the second type of proper suture, which is called "Gualia" — a term of his own and recounted by him— is that you sew it in a similar fashion to how some physicians sew it, in that they bring each part to its corresponding part, as nature dictates; and they apply the end of one to the end of the wound, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
You should insert the needle into the end of the wound close to the outside part, and make it penetrate the inside part at that point, leaving the two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside at the two ends of the thread.
And then turn each one, and make each one penetrate from the inside to the outside at the two ends of the wound on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common, superficial suture, and is the suture of which I previously spoke.
This is the method of Gualia.
And some might say, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles.  This is so that you insert a thread into each of them, beginning with the needle from your side, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who know how to close the skin equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle part of the belly, then the suture there is more difficult than in other parts of the belly.
But the judgment of the treatment of plague is the same as for other plagues.
Which means that when you catch the plague fresh, with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestine to its place and sew it up and make it whole, then apply the flesh-healing powder to it.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then apply some ointments that are at your disposal until they form a hard stone and the pus dries and it is consolidated and cleaned; and then treat it as you would treat other plagues until it is healed.
But if the necessary medicines aren't available, then immediately apply something like old cloth or tow, and change it twice a day, as you've been taught, until it is healed, for you don't need other treatments in many cases when plagues are simple.
But if you fear the plague might spread to major limbs, then you should soak lion's wool in high-quality oil, or in processed oil, and place it around the affected areas, between the armpits and under the arms.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in their intestines, as often happens, then treat it with dark astringent wine, and especially if the corruption has reached the intestine and has made a penetrating exit into the cavity.
And know that a wound in the large intestine is easier to heal than one in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal, for the intestine called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a plague affecting it entirely; this is because of the great multitude of veins within it, the size of the area, the delicacy of its structure, and its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if what proceeds from the plague is tissue, and you obtain some fresh tissue, reduce it according to the method you use for reducing the intestines as well.
But if it has progressed to that stage and has already turned green or black, then you should apply a bandage to the darkened area, in order to bring blood flow to the area; for in the tissue there are veins and arteries.
And then, cut what is remaining of the ligature, and hang the two ends of the bandage from below the wound, so that the pull on the wound, towards the joining of the tissue and the discharge of the affected part, is lessened.
A reminder of wounds when they occur in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine, and it is small, it is possible in some cases for it to be repaired.  For I have seen one who was wounded in their belly by a lance thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and had become a fistula, and from it escaped gas and wind.
Then I considered the cure for him, though it was not focused on healing, but I attempted to improve it so that it might be healed and the area be consolidated.
And after I saw the area healed, I told the patient that an illness might still affect him from the accident in his abdomen; but nothing bad resulted from it, and he remained in good condition, eating and drinking, and later took a bath and engaged in his activities.
And some experienced persons have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine, and it is small, then it should be treated in this manner:  You should take nettles with large heads; then bring the two edges of the wound together and place one of those nettles, with its mouth open, over the edges of the wound.  Then, when it bites those edges and closes its mouth, cut off the head, because it will adhere and not dissolve.
Then place another nettle beside the first, and do not stop doing this with nettles, one beside another, according to the size of the entire wound.
And then you compress it and close the wound; and those heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm comes to the patient.
And it is possible that the intestine be bound with a fine thread made from the intestine of an animal, attached to it, once it has been threaded onto a needle; you should take the end of this intestinal thread, and it should be very lean, and bind the end of it to the thread of the wound, wrapped; then, insert the thread through a needle and secure the wound with it, and return the thread to the wound's rupture.
And this method of suturing with nettles is only a way of hope and trust (or reliance).
However, if the wound is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the delicate intestines, for then it is not really a cure, but only a temporary fix, and it will not fully heal.
Of the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it ages and becomes chronic, and is ulcerated, and does not heal and discharges pus continuously and is not incised, then, in any limb, it is universally called a fistula, and we call it an ulcer.
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, fibrous growth, with no pain and a cavity similar to the cavity of a bird's nest; and thus it is called a 'riddled ulcer', that is, a 'cavity'.
And in some people it is moist, discharging pus; and easily the moisture is removed in a short time. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes thin, and sometimes large, and sometimes small.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of those and their cure with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you aware of the treatment of fistula, which is done surgically with the hands.
And know that fistula occurs in all parts of the body; for there are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spine, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to one of the spinal vertebrae, or to any of the joints formed by composition, such as the hand and foot; and those are the ones that affect parts close to the main limb and similar ones.
Therefore, those fistulas of this type are among the illnesses with difficult healing, and do not receive a cure, and any attempt to treat them with that method is work and ignorance.
And if any fistula's depth does not reach the locations mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to understand it, then take a probe or iron, and if the fistula runs in a straight line, you will find it with it.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is eased and directed towards the winding path.
And if the fistula has many horrible things, it is not possible for you to indicate them with a probe.
Then irrigate the openings with a solution, for the moisture with which you irrigate it will flow toward the other openings and run from them.
And then inquire carefully, by whatever means you can, to know if there are bones or nerves there, and if the cavity of the fistula is distant or near, or if it is a fistula with many openings and this is all covered by your strength, with your questioning, by which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain from your pressure with your hand on the area, and similar to the indications and accidental causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all those causes with true judgment, then proceed to the cure according to the knowledge: and it is that you then consider whether the fistula is a superficial fistula near or in a healthy area, distant from a joint, or a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula as previously described by my narration, and remove what is within that filtration and corrupted flesh, and that which is born from the opening of the flesh, or of pus and similar things, and cure it until it is healed.
However, if the fistula is of great depth, and is accordingly, then it must be incised at the base as far as possible, and then clear it of all corrupted flesh; and then administer some bandages with sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have opened.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the cavity of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have described.
However, if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then you probe and cauterize it as described, if you do not find any vein or nerve or main limb obstructing it, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is any corruption or blackness on it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then heal it with something that makes flesh grow until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and still bleeds as it did before, then understand that you have not fully eradicated the corruption; and then you uncover it again carefully, thoroughly scrape and clean it with the best tools you have, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, well and good; but if not, then understand that the corruption is in a deep, distant part of the body, which cannot be reached, and then your only option is to leave it be.
If the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then you pull it out with fine forceps suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then carefully remove them all, and leave no trace of them with your tools.
But if any issue with the humors is causing trouble, and their incision poses no fear or error, then cut those ligaments or flesh, if present, and free the bones.
But if there is no preparation to remove the bones immediately, then place something over the wound that can protect the wound and leave it until it is surrounded by flesh and ligaments, and ensure the wound does not become consolidated and the incision you made during the operation is contained.
If you are concerned, apply cotton soaked in warmed oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian unguents, or in other green ointments, for when those ligaments soften, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and pulling out of those parts is eased.
But if it's a large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone, or similar, and only the surface is corrupted, then scrape that surface with a tool just behind it until the blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
However, if the corruption has affected a large portion of the bone, and has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to cut through that bone completely to the point where the corruption has reached, and then heal that until it is consolidated.
And I will relate to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and help in your own operation.
There was a young man, approximately 30 years old, who experienced pain in his thigh.  The affliction spread from internal parts of the body to the thigh, causing a large abscess, and it wasn't due to an external cause.
And then the condition was prolonged due to the doctors' error, until the abscess opened, and many materials were discharged.  His healing was delayed until his leg became fistulous, and many openings were formed, all of which discharged pus and bodily fluids.
Then, a multitude of doctors treated him for about two years, and none of them had the proper knowledge of the art, until I was called in.
And I saw his leg, and the materials flowing from those openings, a great deal of them; and the man's body was already completely drained, and his color was yellowish.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone.
And then I examined all the openings, and found them reaching their ends in all parts of the leg.
Then I carefully incised one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone, and found it corrupted, already earthy and decayed, and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted, having penetrated to the marrow.
Then I closed what had been exposed of the discovered bone.
And I judged that it was not in the bone, but that corruption which I had scraped off and closed, and I had already removed it.
And then I applied restorative medicine to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I again opened and exposed the bone, further than the first exposure, and found continuing corruption with the bone.
And I removed what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was forced to perform its treatment and restoration, but it was not restored.
And then I exposed it again and did not cease to cut the bone little by little, specifically, forcing its restoration, but it was not restored until I cut around a palm's width of it, and removed the marrow along with it.  Then I restored that with medicines, and it was slightly healed and healthy.
And further operations and cutting of it are not necessary except for the weakness of it, and because of its low tolerance and fear of death, for it was happening to it at all times due to an excessive evacuation causing a bad fainting spell.
And thus healed entirely, and there is nothing in place of bone but hard flesh; and its arrangement within its body is now good, and its strength has returned; and it has been restored to its proper functions, and it did not cause him any difficulty in walking or harm in any way.
However, if the bone is prominent in a dislocated place, then you should cut it in this manner: you should take a ligature and tighten it on the extremity of that prominent bone, and order someone to pull it outward; and place a thicker wool ligature on the first ligature; and then tie this over the flesh beneath the bone, and pull its extremity so as to draw the flesh downward, and you expose the flesh over the area you want to cut, so the cut doesn't damage the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or board under the bone properly, for when you do this, the cut is not prohibited from cutting through the corrupted flesh.
And the incision should be made over the corrupted area little by little, for if the corruption is in the bone's cavity and does not appear on its surface to the touch, another incision must be made.
And if the bone is corrupted but not protruding, with one part continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle or part of it, then you expose all the flesh around; and then place wood underneath, and then cut that first part where the corruption is until the incision continues to the other part.
And make the incision following the location of the corruption, a little bit, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not in a rational place, but in a problematic one.
And if the corruption is in the palm of the hand or sole of the foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and cut that, and cleanse that according to every possible disposition, and with each expedient rectify it if it does not cause you to cut a vein or nerve.
And know that the incisions and saws used to cut these bones are very specific according to the position of the bones, their preparation, their thickness, their subtlety, size, and smallness, and their hardness and rarity; therefore, you must prepare suitable instruments for each type of operation.
