Of the cutting edges and the sawing of bones.
The extremities [of the disease] can be caused either intrinsically or extrinsically, or if you have treated that corruption with remedies, and the treatment is not effective, or if the corruption extends to the limb which is not affected by any cause, then you must cut into that limb where the corruption has spread, so that the patient escapes death or great harm to the limb.
And the sign of that which afflicts [the patient] is that the limb becomes black as if fire has already burned it, or the blackness spreads until that hardness enters that limb, seizing it and affecting the whole body; and then make a cut into it.
And likewise, if the cause of the corruption is the impact of some poisonous things, such as a sea scorpion, or viper, or weasel, and similar creatures.
And if the corruption or the injury is in the extremity of the finger, then you cut the finger, and do not allow the corruption to spread into the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you cut it at the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread so that it reaches the forearm.
And if instead, it affects the arm, then you cut the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond the elbow, then in that case is the death of the patient; and to administer another cause for its cure is a matter of little use.
And similarly, do with the foot. When corruption affects the toe of the foot, then you cut it up to one of the bones.
And if it takes hold of the sole of the foot, then you cut off the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you cut the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy but to let him go, and to leave the patient to death.
And the way to cut or sever the limb is that you tighten the ligament in the part below where you want to cut, and tighten another ligament over the area; and let the attendant extend a ligament down, and let the attendant extend another ligament up; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligaments with a wide spatula until all the flesh is exposed; and then let it be cut or severed.
And it is necessary that a cloth be placed on all sides so that it does not touch the area itself, and that the patient experiences a well-adjusted and cautious abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood when working, then cauterize the wounded area carefully, or place some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man on his foot, the same accident that I and you have described, which is that blackening occurred on his foot, with a burn similar to a fire's combustion.
And that corruption first appeared on his toe, until it spread to the entire foot.
And then that man prepared himself, since he saw the treatment affecting the limb with the severity of the pain and burn he experienced, and he incised that foot down to the joint, and the wound healed.
For a long time, that same corruption occurred on the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I was forced to address that excess using remedies I applied to his hand, effectively cleansing his body; but the excess wasn't dispelled, and it began to spread to the other fingers until it affected the whole hand.
And he urged me to cut into his hand, but I didn't want to do that, hoping for the expulsion of that excess, and fearing the death of the patient if I incised his hand, for the patient's strength was at its lowest ebb.
And after he left me, he returned to his homeland, and later word reached me that the man prepared himself and cut his entire hand, and it healed.
And I have related this story only to provide help in understanding this disease, and as a way to indicate the signs that accompany it and how it should be treated.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicine.
When an abscess occurs on any fleshy part of the body, and the space of the abscess becomes large enough to collect pus, and it is then broken and perforated, and pus is discharged, leaving a hollow space like a vessel, and the skin over it is thin like a delicate cloth, and it hasn't progressed to deeper corruption, and it hasn't affected the bone, nerves or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
However, if the space becomes so large that the corruption spreads to other parts of the body, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment of it is to cut away the entire skin, like it has been turned inside out, especially if it is thin and like cloth, and make sure it does not adhere to the affected area due to its corruption.
But if you suspect the skin is adhering and that the corruption hasn't progressed deeper, and there's a visible opening in the skin, then treat it with an enema.
And if you consider it to be a sinus, and if it's large, and the discharge has a foul odor, then administer an enema with an Egyptian medicine, which is made of olive oil, sour wine, and vinegar in equal parts.  These are mixed in a vessel, and heated over a fire until the medicine becomes reddish and takes on the consistency of honey.
Then take what is necessary of it and dissolve it with water and honey; and use this mixture to administer an enema into the sinus, and constrict the opening, and leave the medicine there for two hours.
Then remove it with a spatula; and do this for as many days as necessary until the sinus is cleansed and the bad odor has gone away.
And sometimes, if this medicine isn't available to you, it's enemated with water from ashes; and you should take the ashes of willow, place them in water, then filter it and use that water for an enema until you are confident that the sinus is closed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then administer an enema with water and honey, mixed with finely ground vinegar.  Or, use a mixture of wine and honey, because honey has the property of cleansing and washing, and wine helps to adhere the sinus, especially when the wine contains an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are sure the sinus is healed, then give an enema with something that will help grow flesh in that area.
And you should take diafine ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and astringent wine; or you should administer an enema with other medicines and ointments that have the properties that I have already written in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the enema tube cannot be inserted, then widen it little by little with a small iron implement, and apply heated cloth wrapped in Egyptian or basil ointment until it widens.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the edges together with stitches, leaving enough space for the enema tube to fit without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is elevated to the upper parts, then dry it in the lowest part of it so that the pus flows downwards, because when the pus is contained within the depth of the sinus, flesh will be generated there.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus down to the lowest bone as desired, then you must prepare the limb with a preparation through which the pus flows easily, as you are equipped, and that there is no cause for the pus to be retained there.
But the one over the sinus opening, which remains at all times to allow the matter contained within the pus to escape. And so that you may know if the pus flowing from the limb is much or little, or digested or undigested.
And by this you consider the location of the sinus, if the patient feels pain there or not, and if it is an abscess.
And as your treatment proceeds in this manner, and you see that the matter discharged over the sinus opening has adhered, or shows signs of adhesion, then on the second or third day, you loosen the scab, and when you loosen the scab, change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle virus is spreading, then do not despair of the adhesion, for the healing is taking its course; for some people, flesh regeneration is slow on their wounds, and for others the opposite is true.
And if undigested pus has been discharged from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus has not adhered; and if the sinus adhesion is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you should adjust the drying effect of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and virtue, they should be dried, just like a diachronic ointment, when excess moisture has been removed.
The final remedy, with which it is cured, is to take diachronic ointment, dissolve it in warmed oil, and add some old, strong wine to its substance.  Then mix these together and use the mixture.
And a similar procedure is followed when there is no pus: use diachronic ointment, honey when it has thickened, and apply it; or take myrrh, aloe, and incense, grind and powder them, and sprinkle over the honey while it is over the fire. Then spread this mixture on a cloth and apply it to the sinus. Or, use honey that has thickened, powder it, spread it on a cloth, and apply it to the sinus.
Sometimes, long-stemmed aristolochia and the roots of the celestial lily (and it is celestial lily), along with the flour of erran and centaury, are administered. Use these individually or combined, grinding and sifting them, and sprinkling the powder over the honey, as previously described, and use this mixture.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone underneath, or in other hard parts of the body or ligaments.  Then cure it by fistular treatment, as previously described.
Concerning the treatment of whitlow (or felon), bruised nails, incisions in the finger, and further sections (or divisions).
Whitlow is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of a finger or toe, though it commonly appears on other fingers.  If prolonged and its cure is slow, it might be an abscess of a severe abscess, or become corrupted, and discharge pus, gradually corrupting the nail bed, often completely destroying the nail, and spreading its corruption to the bone until it emits a foul odor. The fingertip may become dark, and its color discolored.
And therefore, when you have performed the procedures we have described, and your treatment does not prove effective, you should cut away all remaining excess from beneath the nail with a knife, and then cauterize the wound, for a cautery is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone and nail are healthy, and the nail bed cavities are already pressing against the surrounding flesh, and causing it to swell and ulcerate, then you must place a thin wedge under the corner of the nail that is pressing on the flesh.  Push up the affected flesh and cut away the flesh with ease, and apply to the remaining flesh, medicaments that burn and corrode until all of it is removed. Afterwards, treat the area with ointments until healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then you must cut into and remove the bone, for a bone affected by corruption is never properly healed.
And if the corruption and decay extend into the finger, then you should cut it open at one of the joints, according to the anticipated recovery, and then treat it until healed.
However, if the nail suffers blows or friction, and intense pain results, then you must first bleed the patient, and then carefully scrape the nail with a sharp instrument, using a transverse cut from top to bottom. Be careful not to cut into the flesh beneath the nail, as you will cause the patient severe pain and create conditions for the formation of excessive, inflamed tissue in the area. Then, treat the affected area with whatever relieves the pain.
But a swollen finger, which is often found on the hands of some men, is easily affected, both in the flesh, and in the bones, or even in the nail.
And some of these swellings are born at the joint, or their origin is in certain finger joints. However, those that originate in the joints of the finger are not as bad, and those that arise in the joints are easily aggravated.
And for those that are fleshy growths, the incision is gentle, and you should cut it away from its base with a broad blade.
But for those that originate at the base of the joint, the cure is difficult; therefore, avoid cutting it.
But for those that arise on the fingers near the joints, you must first cut the flesh around it with a circular incision down to the bone, then use a suitable saw to sever it, and then treat it until healed.
But if the fingers are bound together abnormally, either by birth, or by the effects of an injury, plague, or burning, or similar causes, then the abnormal joining must be separated until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in heated oil to prevent them from quickly fusing, and separate them, or place a thin lead sheet between them until they are properly healed.
And similarly, if fingers are abnormally joined to the palm, you must sever that abnormal joining in a way that is suited to the member's shape.
Regarding incisions of veins, these are varicose veins.
Veins are varicose veins, and they are thick, twisted veins filled with melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and more commonly in the legs, especially those of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
However, the treatment of these [varicose veins] with iron is according to two methods: one is that the vein be incised and the black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be raised and entirely extracted.
The incision is performed as follows: first, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, muddy blood from the leg is resolved.  Then, tightly bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh down to the area just below the knee with a bandage.  Then, incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision.  Then, raise the black blood from the bottom of the leg upwards and downwards, until the desired quantity of blood has been drawn, which will lessen the patient's ailment.
And then bandage it, and command the patient to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are filled up, and this is noted by the patient.
But the raising of them [the veins] is done in this way: you, who are attending to the patient's leg, if it is covered in hair, first place it in a bath and soak the leg in hot water until it reddens and the veins become prominent; or, if a bath is not available, use vigorous exercise until the limb is heated up.
Then dry the skin over the vein by making an incision along the vein, either behind the knee, or further down near the ankle.
And then open the skin with lancets and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears, and it is to be observed that the redness will become a black or dark colour.
And then, as it emerges, you see that white string (or cord) from the skin.
And then you place a reed under it [the vein] until it is raised and pulled out of the skin, and suspend it with a lancet or similar sharp instrument.
And then make another incision near the first incision, after three days, and scrape the skin over the vein until it appears.
And then raise it [the vein] and cut it in the second incision, towards the heel, and then pull it out and elevate it until it comes out through the second incision, and then pull it out from the incision above.
Do this until you pull it [the vein] out of the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until it is all out; and then cut it.
And if you are unable to pull and elevate it, then insert a needle with a strong double hook and tie it and pull it out, or insert a reed under it and turn your hand around in all directions until it is thrown out. And be careful not to cut it; for if it is broken, it will be difficult for you to elevate it much, and it will have caused harm to the patient.
And when you have completely removed it, then place on the affected area wool soaked in wine and rubbed oil and unrubbed oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having twists to which it is wrapped in parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then you must cut on that part of the twist of it and in the places where it is wrapped, and then suspend it with hooks until you lift it all out.
And be careful in your incision not to cut the vein and not to injure it, for it will be difficult for you to lift it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision by which the vein is cut:
It is not sharp like hooks or with a pointed end so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of great flexibility, like a lion's, for if it were slender, it would cut the vein through its thinness; it does have superfluous thickness, as we said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in warm lands, like in the land of the Arabs, and in the hot and arid lands of India where little vegetation grows.
And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from beneath the skin, like snakes, worms, and insects that breed between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this condition is a fierce inflammation in the leg where the vein is; and then the area becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of the blister, appearing like a plant's root.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap a lead weight, equivalent to one drachma to two drachmas, around it, and bandage that and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
And as many times as you find any cause of it on the outer parts, wrap that in lead and bandage it. And if the condition is prolonged, then you should cut off a part of it and bandage the remaining part; and do not cut it off at the root until it is completely out, because if you cut it off at the root, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing an abscess, swelling, and a bad ulceration in the area.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from behind it and no cause of it remains in the body.
And from this vein, some men have had it extend to five palms and ten fingers, and it has come to my attention that one has extended twenty palms.
And if it is incised during its treatment, then insert a rod into the cavity and pierce it with a long opening, through the body, until everything that is within the material is evacuated, and allow the area to fester for several days with medicines; and then cure it by the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or on the foot, and it has many openings, and a branch comes out from each opening.
Therefore, cure it thus as we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Of the incision regarding worms generated beneath the skin, and it is called the bovine disease.
This illness is called in some lands near us the bovine disease because it often affects cattle, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh, and it travels throughout the body going up and down, and it is felt moving from limb to limb until it breaks through the skin and comes out.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, serpents, and earthworms, and ascarids are produced in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it travels through the body and rises to the head, and reaches the eye, it often causes a severe inflammation there, entering and destroying the eye.
And therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, it is done only by following its movement and appearance as felt, and then you must tightly bind and constrict what is above it and what is below it, and then carefully dry the area and extract it.
And if it is hidden in the flesh and you cannot find it, then apply a cautery with fire to the area until it dies, and the greatest fear is that it will corrupt the area, as we have said.
And if you see that creature reaching the head near the eye, then apply strong pressure to the forehead, dry the area carefully, and extract it.  The patient must use medicines to cleanse the body of the bad, putrid humours, and avoid foods that cause putrefaction.
Of the incision upon the illness.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes upon certain limbs, and then it moves from one limb to another.
And I have already seen that, as I have told you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm. After an hour, I saw this swelling move like a worm climbing upward, but more swiftly than a worm could, like living silver running from place to place.
And then the pain went to that place and settled in the muscle, and then stayed there for an hour.
And then it moved to the other body part until it reached the other arm.
And then it returned to me, moving around her entire body as I watched; and I was very surprised by its movement from one limb to another. And I had previously seen this sort of thing with my own eyes, though I have seen many instances where pain is moved from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes quite like this in the woman.
And I did not consider it anything else, but that it was because the woman was old, with dried-up body and exposed veins, and in this appearance, that wind-like sensation was exchanged.
And it is necessary that this sort of  "return-healing" not appear in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And when you desire the cure for them, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eyes as we have said, therefore you press gently and lightly upon it until that constrained wind-like sensation is gone, and cauterize the location.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you cure it with an extraction of the body's humours and with things that expel windiness, and alleviate them with stinking pills and pills of serapi and similar remedies.
Of extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types and according to the parts of the body in which they lodge, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, that is, solid; and some have three angles, and some with tongues; and some with barbs.
And the signs of the brain when an arrow lodges in it, and pierces the bone, and exits through the skull which is over the brain, are a sudden violent headache, and dizziness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasms and confusion of thought and vomiting of bile, and often the ability to speak is lost and the voice is destroyed, and white moisture resembling dust comes out from the area of the wound, and the area looks like the flesh is becoming watery.
Therefore, if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from treatment and extraction of the arrow, unless it is on the outside.
But the signs of an arrow lodging in the heart, especially if it is near the left breast, are that it feels as if it were lodged in something hard, and not empty; and the movement of the arrow is often similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and the body chills, the extremities become cold, and the person faints; and therefore, know that death is imminent without a doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are the spitting of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck are inflamed, and the patient's complexion has changed, and they breathe deeply, and crave the intake of cool air.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane that is in the foot, the pain is then near the small ribs, and there is great difficulty with vehement pain and sighing, and all the muscles of the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then frequently undigested food will come forth from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the belly, and is embedded there, there will be an opening in the wound, or the lining, or the intestines, which are ruptured, and there is no hope for treatment, if the arrow is not removed.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine is discharged externally, and some cause emanates from the wound to the outside, and severe pain is upon the patient, then know that it is fatal.
However, the other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, and arms, and the vertebrae of the back, and the collarbone, hip, leg, and similar limbs, are saved according to the greatest arrangements, when the arrow does not strike a vein or artery and it is not a poisoned arrow.
And I tell you this, what I have seen of these arrows, so that you may signify this upon your treatment.
Which cause is this: that in a man, an arrow struck the tear duct of his eye at the root of his nose, and I removed it from the other side, under the ear, and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which struck him in the fold of his eye, beneath the lower eyelid; and the arrow was already hidden, and only a small end was retrieved, connected to its shaft, and it was a large, pointed arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion-like iron head, which had no feathers.
And the Jew was healed, and no bad accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man, which had lodged in his throat; it was an Arabic arrow, and it was one with feathers; and then I incised the area around it, and it was already embedded in his throat; and then I skillfully dealt with it, and then extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man, which had lodged in his abdomen, and I thought he would die from it; and then, after thirty days had passed and no change had occurred in his condition, I incised the arrow and worked on it, and he was healed, and no bad accident happened to him.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow lodged in his back, and the wound healed over it, but after roughly seven years, the arrow came out near the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow lodged in her abdomen, and the wound healed within her, and there was no change in her condition, and the woman suffered no harm in any of her natural bodily functions.
And I saw another man in whose face an arrow lodged; the wound healed, and the arrow remained embedded, causing little pain, and many similar cases like this.
And I extracted the arrow from the King's nobleman that had lodged in the middle of his nose, and had already deviated slightly to the right side, and the entire arrow was concealed.
And I was called to treat him shortly after the arrow struck, within three days.
And then I found the arrow wound very tight; then I probed it with a fine probe, and I felt nothing; but he felt a prick beneath his right ear, and I inferred that this prick was caused by the arrow's point.
And I applied a plaster to the area, one with attractive and digestive virtues, trusting that the area would fester, so that the arrow wound would appear, so that it would dry over it.
And no significant injury occurred to the area where the arrow had struck; and I continued with the plaster on it for many days, and no further injury occurred; and the wound consolidated meanwhile, and the patient remained in pain from the extraction for many days, until one day he felt the arrow within his nose and told me about it.
Then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it opened up, and with the probe I examined it, and found the fine point of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I adjusted the application of the sharp medicine to the wound until the arrowhead became clearly visible to me, and I spent time with this for a period of up to four months.
And then, because the wound had become enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert the forceps into it, and remove it, and it did not yield, then I did not cease to refine and devise strategies on it, until one day I began with mechanical devices to remove it with carefully crafted forceps, in the manner described later in the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I healed the wound.
Some physicians thought that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound was healed, and the patient recovered completely, and suffered no further harm.
I recount to you the method of extracting certain arrows, so that you may consider this a rule and signification of what I have not said, because the parts of this art and the definitions of it are not grasped by narrative, nor do they fit the book.
Skilled craftsmen compare little with much; and that which is present with that which is absent; and find new operations and new instruments in the removal of foreign bodies from the art when they occur.
Therefore, I say that arrows are not expelled from the limbs in which they are lodged except in two ways: either by drawing them out from the place in which they entered; or by applying force to the opposite part.
That which is expelled from the place where it entered, or is an arrow lodged in fleshy tissue, is pulled and expelled.
If you do not follow the method at the time of the wound, then it is necessary to leave it for a few days until the surrounding flesh may heal, and the extraction of the arrow and its expulsion is made easier.
And similarly, if it is lodged in bone and you do not follow the method, then leave it for a few days and repeatedly apply pulling and movement each day, and it will be expelled.

Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the practice of phlebotomy is customary to do so XXX, of which are in chapter XVI, are two pulsating veins near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins in the two apparent temples; and two veins in the two tear ducts, called *aspicientz*; and the vein situated in the middle of the forehead; and the vein at the tip of the nose; and two veins at the neck; and two veins on the upper lip; and two veins on the lower lip; and  — these are the veins called *algelic* —; and two veins below the tongue. But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of those, one is the cephalic vein, and it is beside the wild side; and the people call it "the vein of the head"; and it is smooth, and is a middle vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilica, and another branch of the cephalic; and the people call that "the vein of the whole body"; and the basilica is the vein that is located on the domestic side, and it is called "citillar", and the common people call that "the vein of the belly"; and the vein of the arm, and it is located above the fossa, and it is the one in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is the one that appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and *salvatella*, and it is the vein that is between the aforementioned auricular and the smallest finger, and there are two branches there.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of them is located below the curve of the knee on the wild side; and the second is the *sophena*, and its place is below the leg on the domestic side; and the sciatic vein, and its place is below the leg on the wild side; and in the leg are other veins similar to those.
The benefit of the veins near the ears is for headaches, migraines, toothaches, and old sores of the head.
And the method of bleeding those veins, as I recommend to you, is that you should place the patient's head on a raised surface, and rub the patient's head at the occipital region, in the location of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then gently restrain the patient's neck with a bandage until both veins appear, and the places of their presence are near the ears in two depressions on the head; and locate them with your finger; and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, then you should proceed with the lancing.
Then take the lancet, which is the one that is sharp, and then insert it under the vein at the right angle until the phlebotomy reaches the bone. And then elevate your hand with the vein and the angle upward and cut the vein with a sharp incision. And the length of the incision should be about the width of two fingers; and let the blood flow out as much as you want.
And then compress with cloths and leave it until it is healed.
And already, at the beginning of the book, the incision of those and their cauterization have been described.

And therefore, make the incision on the largest vein, either below the leg towards the ankle, or near its junction, for it is better and more beneficial.
And if you make the incision on the surface of the foot, then you must be careful of the nerves that are underneath on the surface of the foot, and position your incision with the tool you're using to cut across it, as if you were intending to make an incision.
And let the bloodletting be done skillfully.
But if the flow of blood is prohibited, then you should immerse the patient's foot in hot water and allow the blood to drain until it is evacuated.
And if the blood-letter errs in the first attempt, then repeat the bloodletting a little further along, for the spot is beneficial; and do not fear harming the patient, but be careful of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly do so for the sole of the other foot as well.
And for the sciatic vein, the place, as we have said, is beneath the heel, below the bone of the outside part.
And the benefit of the bloodletting for him is for hip pain when it is caused by hot blood.
And the procedure for his bloodletting is that the patient should enter a bath, and tightly wrap the thigh from the side of the hip, extending above the heel, with a thin long bandage for four days; for it appears only through that.
And therefore, when it appears, then you should perform phlebotomy on that, according to each technique possible to you and following the transverse method, which is better; or cut that by incision, or cut it by section, because the site for him/her is saline.
And it is very hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it does not appear to the touch, then open a branch of it, and it is the one that appears on the back of the foot, near the mentioned auricular and annular areas.
And beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow out in the quantity you want.
And then loosen the constriction and put a compress on the site of the phlebotomy and bind the area tightly, for it is quickly healed.
Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping is of horns, or of clay, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with incision and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping that is without incision is in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And to the cupping which is administered with incision and extraction of blood, there are fourteen places on the body, of which the one on which cupping is placed is at the nape of the neck, and it is the back part of the head; and cupping in the middle of the two sides of the neck together; and cupping of the chin below the jaw, below the mouth; and cupping on both shoulder blades; and cupping of the hypochondrium, and it is above the end of the rib cage; and cupping on both sides of the forearms; and cupping on both sides of the shins; and cupping on both thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood from the large veins, but from small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore does not drain the vital force through them, as is the case with bloodletting; neither should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by fullness until the entire body is emptied.
But if necessity provokes cupping for illness or for a chronic condition, administer it at any time at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at any time.
And therefore, when in any of those cases, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we command that cupping be done after two or three hours have passed.
The benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and what is pressing on the eyes; however, this should be done after the whole body has been emptied.
And this cupping is in the location of a phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted to use it on someone with a cold, or on someone with a catarrh, for it would do great harm to them.
And therefore, it should not be used on the elderly, and on those whose illnesses are cold.
But in those who are continuously ill, oblivion is induced; and therefore you must instruct the cupper to place their hand slightly beneath the cupping jar at the lower parts, to prevent the generation of oblivion.
But cupping with alcohol is used in place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and basilica veins:  and by doing so, it helps with asthma, and constriction of the windpipe and the tearing of the windpipe, and with coughs and congestion.
And the alcohol cupping jar should be raised slightly, for if it is placed too low, it creates weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* (likely a specific type of cupping or substance used in it) is beneficial for accidental pains in the head, and for ophthalmia, and migraine, and feverishness, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And you must instruct the cupper not to press their hand with a cutting action, so as not to cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin is helpful for ulcers in the mouth, and for corruption of the gums, and for similar maladies in the mouth, and it takes the place of phlebotomy of the veins that are in the two lips.
But cupping done over the two shoulder blades is beneficial for *caphatan* (a likely medical condition), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping of the two parts inside the armpits is beneficial as a substitute for the phlebotomy of three veins, namely, the basilica, the cephalic and the basilic vein, because it draws blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh, and these smaller veins draw from the larger veins until the drawing is done in the three major veins.
And tell the cupper not to press too hard in cutting, because the area is lacking flesh, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping that is applied to the buttocks, which is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus and for ulcers beneath them.
And it is necessary to instruct the cupper to use a large, strong cupping glass, because the area requires a forceful suction; and flimsy, easily pierced cupping glasses are inappropriate and should not be used, nor should they be slashed/cut with a knife.
However, cupping of the legs is less effective, as it draws blood from the whole body, and it relieves old pains in the kidneys, the liver, and the bladder; and it induces menstruation, and is useful for boils and abscesses; and it can replace bloodletting from two cephalic veins, and then from two arm veins.
However, it greatly weakens the entire body and can cause fainting in many men.
And the benefit from cupping at the armpits is comparable to the benefit of cupping both legs.
And the quality of applying the cupping is to place the cup initially empty and create a moderate suction, and do not prolong the application of the cups, but rather apply them lightly and keep them in place briefly, so that the humors are drawn to the location by a gentle influx. And do not cease repeating this and continuing until you see the area reddening and swelling, and the redness of the blood appearing.
And then you should make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and then consider the disposition of the body's condition.
For that person with tender, easily bruised flesh, you should only make one incision, lest the area become ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to deepen the incision slightly, and to moderate the suction gently.
And if the blood is thick, then you should make two incisions, for on the first occasion you open the path for the blood and its watery part to be drawn out. But on the second, by scrutinizing the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then a third incision should be made to complete the process.
And finally, when we want to extract little blood gradually, a single incision is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make many incisions; and if we know that the blood is thick, then we should make deep incisions.
And the depth of the incision should be just the thickness of the skin.
And if the skin is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, the places where the cupping glasses are to be applied should be anointed with soothing, opening, and resolving ointments. If it is summer, use oil of rosemary, or oil of violet, or oil of mild almonds, or oil of chamomile seed.  If it is winter, use narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or bloodroot oil, and similar substances.
And if the superfluity is thick and cold, then the oil should be of amaranth, or balm, or rosemary, or dill, and similar oils.
But if the cupping is on thin skin with wide pores, then the use of oil should be avoided, and instead the places for the cupping glasses should be washed with lukewarm water or cold water of solanum, or water of chamomile, or water of mint, or similar substances.
And for one whose blood is of moist consistency, wash the cupping sites with sour wine, or water of myrtle and sumac, and similar substances.
And for those with thick superfluities, wash their cupping sites with old wine, or water of tansy (which is majorana), or a decoction of dill, or chamomile, and similar preparations.
And cupping should be avoided before a bath; if administered, it should be close to the bath by one or two hours, and one should rest after the cupping.
What is required to be administered before cupping or bloodletting?
Consider this at the beginning: if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp, inflammatory blood, they should take things that cool it, such as pickled cucumbers, endive in sour wine, lettuce, plantain, and juleps, and similar remedies.
And avoid foods like lentils and game birds, and dishes seasoned with acids, and similar foods.
And for someone whose constitution is cold, they should drink licorice syrup, or syrup of cannabis seeds, sumac, and moderately sweet wine; and they should be advised to eat and drink sparingly, avoiding lentil dishes, hemp, pigeons, and small birds, and doves.
And on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the intake of drinks should be greater than the intake of food.
And some men should be given theriac alpharac, or musk-based remedies, or aloes, before cupping or bloodletting, or shortly afterward, so that the major limbs are strengthened and the blood is refined; and they should not consume these remedies while hot.
However, cupping without scarification should be applied to the liver, spleen, gout, the navel, the areas of the knees, and the groin.  These are areas where scarification would not be tolerated.
And these methods are not sought for the removal of blood from a limb, but rather for drawing blood from another part of the body, just as we apply a cupping glass to the breast in cases of nosebleeds; we use this to dispel the cold and viscous wind trapped within the limb, as we would with cupping on the abdomen and navel.  This thins the limb, warms it, and lessens the pain by resolving the wind.
And it is applied to the sinuses when they are congested or filled with phlegm, for by virtue of the strong suction, it quickly opens the congestion and removes the phlegm from its location.
And similarly, it works when placed on the liver and spleen to draw out the wind trapped within them.
And these cupping glasses are applied empty, with suction only; and sometimes they are applied with fire.
And sometimes they are applied filled with lukewarm water, in cases of spleen ailments.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and larger; it is either just hot water, or water in which herbs suitable for the purpose have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass applied with fire:  Let the opening be about two fingers wide, as we have shaped it; and the amount of the fire underneath should be half a palm; and on the patient's side, in the area that is in the middle of the circle,  the hole should be small, as small as an awl could fit through it.
It should be made of smooth, red or citrus wood, with thick, smooth, and even edges so that it does not damage the limb where it is applied.
And there should be a transverse rod of wood or iron in the middle, where a candle is placed with the fire.
And this cupping glass should be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one should be adjusted according to the illness and the age of the person using it.
The cupping glasses for children and the weak are different from those for young adults and those with strong bodies.
And the method of applying this cupping glass with fire to the limb is to heat the surface with wood fire, gently, in front of a small candle made of wax, and place that on the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is ignited around the area below, so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the hole we have mentioned, until when we have the cupping glasses and we do not want to see the finger and the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be dissolved.
And then ignite the surface according to its manner, and repeat if needed.
But a cupping glass administered for the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod or hole, and is not administered unless it is filled with water, and is placed on the limb simply.
And this is its form: and the larger this cupping glass is, the better, because it can receive a greater quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs in which the application of cupping is not possible, due to their small size, such as the foot and the gums and similar parts; or to a limb deprived of flesh, such as the finger and the nose and similar parts.
And the quality to use of those, if you have found leeches in the still waters of muddy places.
And then let them be left in fresh water during the day and night until they are hungry, and that nothing remains in their bellies; and then let the body be emptied at the beginning by phlebotomy and with cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it turns red; and then place them on it.
And when they are full and bloated.
And if suction of the area with a cupping vessel is possible, it is better for healing.
And if not, then wash the area with sour wine and then with plenty of water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the body's blood flow is prolonged during the leeching process, and it is oozing, then you soak a cloth in cool water and place it over the area until the oozing subsides.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle crushed vitriol, or other similar styptic substances, on the area until the bleeding stops; or place roasted bean halves over the area and leave them until the beans adhere to the spot, and the bleeding will stop.
And if necessary, repeat the leeching, if possible, changing the leeches.
And if the leeches are prohibited for use, then the area should be rubbed with fresh blood or a needle should be inserted into the area until blood flows; and then, poultices should be applied, because these stick when they sense blood.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little crushed alum, or salt, or ash, on them, and they will fall off quickly.
Thus begins the treatise of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of bones that happen by accident.
Oh, son, know that foolish and ignorant physicians, and those who have never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them, scoff at this chapter.
And for this reason, this method of the learned in our land is hidden; and I have not forgotten this at all, nor have I adopted this that I have learned, except by the length of my reading of the books of the Ancients and my study on their understanding, until I have extracted the knowledge from them.
And I have devoted myself to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge and upon which my experience has been applied; since I have abbreviated this for you and freed it from lengthy explanation, and I have shortened it in the end, and shown it in the end of its explanation, and I have formed in it many forms of the instruments that are administered to it, as has been done by adding to the explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
Summaries and collections concerning the nature of bone fractures, and the necessary preliminary steps, before we begin recounting fractured limbs and their various types. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with summaries of the subject matter, and the principles, which practitioners are initially required to understand and abide by, in order to maintain the truth of these matters.  This is for you and those who are ready to learn or teach this noble art.
And therefore I say that when someone suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or injury, then it is necessary for you to begin with bloodletting or other remedies, or both together, unless some cause forbids it, such as weakness of constitution, or if the person affected is a child, or an elderly person in decline, or if the time is one of intense heat, or intense cold.
And afterwards, let the patient's diet consist of cool herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits;  and forbid fatty meats and foods that fill the body, and any food that overloads the veins with blood, until you are sure the inflammation is resolving, and unless you fear an outpouring of matter to the limb.
And then restore the patient to their original regimen, with which they were accustomed.
And so, once the broken bone begins to mend, then it is necessary that the patient eat nutritious foods that are substantial, rich, and heavy, containing viscosity, such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, rice, porridge, and the leg, belly, and other parts of cows, eggs, fresh fish, and thick wines and similar substances; with this regimen, the fracture coagulates more easily and effectively.
And know that broken bones in the elderly and those in poor health, in their first stages of ailment, cannot be rejoined and consolidated according to their natural state because of the dryness of their limbs.
However, nature assembles and brings together over the broken bone from every direction a certain glue-like substance, which is thick and adheres to and tightly binds it until it is completely joined and bound together, until it reaches the point of strength and firmness, just as a fire at the beginning until no cause prevents it from continuing its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the patient consumes foods that are heavy, viscous, and substantial, as we have mentioned.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly all other limbs differ among themselves.
And I come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
There is diverse kinds of fractures in themselves. And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for there is an arched fracture, another that occurs in star-like patterns; and sometimes a fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and these are fractures of the bones with splinters and pieces, proceeding and non-proceeding, just as it is done in a pen when it is prepared to write, and there is a fracture with a wound and breakage on the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for every type there is a specific treatment or remedy in restoring it, according to the reminder of it in its place.
Various signs of a fractured bone. And by this a fractured bone is known: there is twisting of it, and a noticeable prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and this is of it when you press it with your hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the area, nor is there a marked change to your touch when you touch the bone with pressure and the patient does not experience significant pain, then it is not a fracture.
However, it is possible that there is twisting or a small fissure, so you should not immediately handle it with your hand and compression.  Instead, place on it some medicines, whose action will remind the body of the appropriate moment for the injury to heal; and then bind it with a gentle bandage.
And know that when the area is bent but not broken in two or otherwise seriously damaged, and the whole part of its structure is retained, then it is necessary to begin the process of straightening it immediately before a harmful abscess develops.
And if a harmful abscess develops, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides; and then treat it according to any method you can devise, using gentle and clever techniques.
And know that the repair and mending of the injury is easier than that of a bone that is seriously damaged.
And then bind it tightly until its healing is complete.
However, if the bone is seriously damaged, then extension of the broken limb (whether hand or foot) is necessary, using two people to hold it, or one person's hand if the limb is small, or using two ropes or one rope and a hand.
And be sure that your position when you put the limb back into its natural shape, while the parts of the broken limb are extended, forces you to return those additions with ingenuity and according to any way possible to their places.
And be careful, according to your strength, that you do not cause further pain or injury to the patient through your operation; and use your skill to properly join one bone with its companion according to their best shapes.
And it is necessary that at that time you diligently feel it with your hand, and if you find any other cause, correct it and fix it with all your skill.
Avoid excessive extension and compression, as some foolish doctors often cause abscesses or contraction in the limb through their practices, as we have seen in their frequent operations.
And the required degree of immobilization for the calmed limb is what I recommend; so that you understand that broken limbs vary in size and form.
And for those that are small, like the arm, finger, or similar, then the wrapping should be with fine, soft cloth.
However, for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, the wrapping should be firm and strong, for a firm bandage joins the large limb and tightly compresses it from all sides, so that no swelling enters.
And then begin, with your alignment, applying a suitable bandage to the fracture site with three or four wraps, depending on the limb's needs; and tighten your hand slightly on the bandage.
And then bring it to the highest part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did with the original tightening of the fracture site. And then gently lengthen the wrapping of the fracture site a little, and gradually loosen the tightening, until you have taken a good portion of the swelling out.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken site with a bandage, and then bring the wraps to the lowest part of the fracture; and let your procedure consist in tightening the bandage and loosening it, just as we said in the first bandage above.
And then place between the wraps a piece of linen or cloth, with which the curvature of the fracture is aligned, if there is a curvature there; and if not, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over this, and then firmly wrap these bandages, properly made, immediately if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the area, and if there is, place on it that which relieves the abscess and removes the inflammation; and leave that for a few days; and then you tighten the bandages over it.
And the splints should be made of half a cane or similar wood, and well-shaped skillfully; or of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly larger and wider than the other splints, and the splint's length should correspond to the limb's size; and then tighten the splints with another bandage, according to your initial tightening; and then tie it securely on top with thread, as we said about tightening, and it is important that your tightening is specific to the location of the fracture, and the further it extends from the fracture, the less the tightening should be.
And it is necessary that the threads be medium in thickness and thinness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some foolish people who used their thick ropes for thread, it is a great error, because the tightening loses its equal distribution.
Likewise, excessively thin threads are not suitable, for you will not be able to tighten properly with them, and it is not appropriate for there to be less than a finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is irritated by the ends of the splints near the tightening at those places, then place some soft linen or carded wool under those ends to prevent them from causing injury.
Sometimes a fracture occurs with a contusion, and there is a disruption in the continuity, and I will return to this with a detailed description in the following chapter.
And know that any broken limb is not tightly bound with splints on the first day; the reason for this is that when a limb is severely injured, one should not apply splints until approximately five or seven days, or more, depending on your assessment of the likelihood of a suppurative inflammation developing.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients restored fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
Method of a common plaster for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many constitutions, and especially for women and children, as it does not cause heat or cold: take powdered flour, and this is the medulla of the flour that falls from the movement of the millstone on the walls of the mill.
And mix it like this, but sift it with egg white, and make a paste of it that is not thick nor thin; and then apply it.
Another method of plaster for restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains:  a recipe using asafoetida, opoponax, acacia, elecampane, and wild millet root,  all equal parts [by weight].  Also, frankincense and myrrh, five parts each; and aloes, twenty parts [by weight]. All should be ground and sifted, and mixed with water or egg white if the patient's constitution is warm. Then apply this plaster, as it is a good compound and restores slightly fractured bones, and is beneficial for many people.
Another method of plaster, beneficial for fractures and sprains:  a recipe using wild millet root and white marshmallow root, equal parts [by weight].  Also, frankincense and myrrh, five parts each; acacia, six parts; and Armenian bole, twenty parts [by weight].
All are ground in a mortar with thorough grinding, and sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and then applied.
A method of plaster for joints and bones dislocated from their places, and for pain and fissures/fractures occurring there: take wool, soak it in sour wine and boiled oil, and place it over the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoration, but is useful for treating suppurative boils and expelling pain directly.
Another method of plaster to restore a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; grind them together and make a plaster from them.
A brief method of plaster for the restoration of a broken bone, and when you want the resolution of remaining suppuration: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, violet flowers, and the flour of comfrey; grind all into a fine powder, mix together, and then add wine to it if the affected limb is not inflamed.
However, if it is inflamed, then mix it with moistened coriander water or simply water and use it.
Another method of plaster, even stronger in resolving suppuration than the first, when dealing with a hard boil hindering the restoration of the limb: take the roots of marshmallow, flaxseed, balm, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile; all into a fine powder; mix them together and add either willow water, plain water, or wine, depending on the nature of the limb and the colour to promote healing.
However, Hippocrates, in his book, did not say that a plaster should be applied to a fractured limb to restore it, but rather a cerot (plaster) made from wax and oil, and nothing else; and recommended that it be of a medium consistency, neither too thick nor too thin.
However, in Gaul, it is customary to apply plasters to broken limbs during their restoration, and for cases where there's desiccation with little heat, using substances such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar to those already mentioned.
A method of plaster for wounds and pain: take equal parts of crushed figs, human or bird skin (or a similar substitute), marshmallow, and salt; grind them, sieve the mixture, and form a plaster.
However, the length of time the bandage should remain on, and when to remove it, is dependent on whether there is pain, swelling or if the bone is fractured and out of place.  In these cases, you should not remove it for several days. And if, at the site, there is intense pain, swelling, or inflammation, then you should take it off immediately and remove the plaster.
And take linen cloth and a wet sea sponge, and immerse it in lukewarm water, and with this wash the area until the swelling subsides and the pain is eased; then allow the limb to rest for an hour.  Then place on it wool soaked in vinegar and oil or olive oil, and bandage it for a night, so you are sure to resolve any festering, and that the inflammation of the limb subsides and the pain is relieved.
Then repeat this subtle compression and small plastering, and do not apply it with your first strong pressure, but gradually to achieve healing.
And if the abscess, hardness, pain, inflammation, and all the other symptoms are completely gone, and you no longer need plaster and compression, then you repeat this process as you did at the beginning.
And if no such causes as we have mentioned affect the limb, then do not remove it for three to four, five, or seven days; and sometimes it is left for twenty days, according to how the limb heals, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the flesh around it coagulates.
Then, increase the compression more than your initial compression, and increase the patient's food intake, in accordance with the patient's recovery.
But if you find the fracture site already drying out and weakening more than necessary, know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, moisten the area with lukewarm water every time you remove it, and do this for three days.  Slightly loosen the compression, and through this operation, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what those foolish bone-setters do in a fractured limb a second time, when it has not been properly set the first time, and they set it according to a crooked method, is an error in their procedure, and a great fear.  If they were right, the ancients would have enlarged upon it in their books and would have worked in this way.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, which makes it more reasonable that it should not be done.
Second Chapter: Of fractures when they occur to the head.
The types of fractures are numerous, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow with a blunt instrument, and the blow crushes all the bone down to the skull, as is done in a tree trunk.  Thus, this type is called a comminuted fracture; or the blow cuts a portion of the bone and cuts the flesh, but does not penetrate to the back of the bone; and this type of fracture is called an incomplete fracture.
And the severity of these two types of fractures is great or small, and a fracture that is a crushing or a grinding; and its cause is a blow with a blunt object or falling on a stone, and similar causes.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is either great or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the subtle smallness of a hair, and thus this type is called a hairline fracture.
And a fracture is made either by falling or by a blow from a stone or similar objects, which causes a portion of the bone to splinter inward, and is made in the concave part, just as happens to the ribs when they are struck, and is fractured from the point of entry inward.
This happens most often in the bones of moist bodies, such as the heads of children.
And in all these types of fractures, the fragments, or pieces, are like separated stars. And I will come with a remedy for all of that, putting it back in place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing them and by investigating them with the probes and by examining the decaying flesh that is over them.
A fracture is recognized because it is revealed on the bone, cleaned, and probed over it, for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider first the patient's symptoms, and if you observe, through the symptoms, a clear indication of danger, such as vomiting of bile, convulsions, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, and swelling and stiffness of the affected areas, and similar symptoms in more severe cases, without a doubt, it is not going to be cured.
And if you observe non-dangerous symptoms, you expect recovery, then you begin the treatment.
The reason for this is that if the fractured person comes to you at the beginning of the illness, and this is during the winter months, then you should study the condition of the bone before the 14th day, according to all circumstances.
And if it is during the summer months, then you must try to set the bone before the 7th day, lest the area under the bone be corrupted by inflammation, and the symptoms we have mentioned arise.
And if the fracture has already reached the inflamed tissue covering the brain, and is with shattered and crushed parts, then you must cut away the broken and crushed parts, as I will instruct you.
And you must shave the head of the injured patient, and expose the bone according to the custom and method possible to you, according to the nature of the injury, and be gentle with the patient.
And if you encounter a bone protrusion, blood flow, or a hot abscess, then you should counteract it with what is necessary, and you should fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rubbed oil, and let the injury rest until the abscess subsides and the blood flow stops; then begin work on the circulation of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two surgical methods.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow-bladed scalpel, and this is the way to do it:  And then you place another scalpel, a little wider than the first; and this is how to do it:
And then place a scalpel even wider than the second one.
And it is necessary for you to have several different scalpels, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, sharpened at the tips of their edges, and made of tempered or hardened steel.
And use them with ease in striking the scalpel, so that it does not strike the head and break it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to bore around it before you apply the scalpels with the drills, which are called non-deepening drills.
And they are not called that (non-deepening) because they do not reach the edge of the bone that surrounds it, because the drill has a rounded tip or edge, which is below the sharp head-like edge or small circle, which prevents it from sinking and going through the thickness of the bone.
And it is advisable that you take a large number of these drills, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have on hand, for every skull, a drill whose tip is sharp, and the length and shortness are according to the thickness of that particular skull.
And this is the method for three types of drills: The way to bore around a broken bone is to place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; then change the drill to another spot and put in a drill of the same size of the bone's thickness (or close to it) between the holes.
Then, with the scalpels, cut what is between each hole in the bone, making it as thin as possible, as we have said, until you have removed the bone, either with your hand, or with some other tool such as tweezers or fine forceps.
And you must carefully ensure that the scalpel does not touch any part of the skull.
And then, once you have bored around the bone and it is separated from the skull if they are joined, and you have extracted it, you must rasp and smooth the rough surface of the bone with another tool similar to a scalpel, but it must be finer and more delicate than the other scalpels.
And if any small pieces of bone remain, then easily remove them with tools at your disposal; then treat the wound with liniments and ointments as we have described.
Another method of operation is gentle, free of fear, which Gualia described and praised greatly.  This is his statement:  It is necessary for you at the beginning to start exposing the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and most apparent, until when you have uncovered that part and have placed below it the tip of this gentle and joyful scalpel, of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the pointed part has two edges in front extending in length, as you see; so that the lenticular part is attached to the skull with the pointed cutting edge against the bone.
Then strike the scalpel on one edge with a small mallet until you have cut through the entire bone easily, as it turns, and you can be sure of the outcome without any accidents that you might fear. And even if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of their craft, certainly if he were asleep...
Then, if any remains attached to the bone, in certain places of the bone,  cut it off with the lenticular tip of the scalpel, and deliver it easily, for it is released without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the flesh, and that which is cut off or removed from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine irregularities remain there, then it is necessary to smooth out those roughnesses and eliminate those irregularities with fine razors, for which you have prepared various sizes, so that you can apply the most suitable and straightest ones according to the part of the broken bone and its shape.
And you must use the widest razors initially in your procedure to smooth the bone, then gradually use the finer ones, and do not stop doing so until you have used the thinnest and finest of them all.
However, for each of the other smaller, capillary fractures and subtle breaks, you must administer treatment according to what you find to be necessary for their repair.
And this is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And what we have written here pertains to large fractures.
And if the pannicle (skin) is pulled tight when you expose the bone, then you must take a cloth according to the size of the wound, and soak it in warmed oil, and place it over the wound opening; then take another cloth, folded once or twice, and soak it in wine and warmed oil, and place this over the first cloth, applying it as gently as possible so as not to compress the skin; and then apply a wide bandage, and do not tighten it beyond the point that it simply holds the wound closed.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two, until you are certain of a heat inflammation; then remove it, and then administer remedies for dryness, such as flax roots, powdered herbs, and ashes, or similar substances.
And you should make these remedies into a powder that is sprinkled on the wound, in their dried state; and universally, apply any medicine that has the property of cleansing, but not of stinging or causing pain.
And be careful, according to your skill, that the wound is clean during its treatment, and is not covered in any ointments or other impurities; nor do you allow virulence to accumulate there, for when it is concentrated on the brain's membranes, it will corrupt and weaken them, and thus cause the patient great, harmful harm.
And you will cause harm to the skin of the head, especially when the bone is exposed and the treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface;  and therefore, if you see the patient exhibiting the symptoms we have described, know that it is dangerous without a doubt.
And if the blackness is not from the wound itself but from the medicine applied, and if the medicine is causing that blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rubbed olive oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and apply it to the scalp; and then treat it with appropriate healing methods until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in two parts simultaneously, but rather one of those parts sometimes, because there are two bones there; for the lower part is cartilaginous and is not broken, and harm is caused only by bruising, twisting, and crookedness.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nose, and use your index finger to push the fracture inward, and your thumb outward, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful not to cause further pain to the patient with your actions.
And if the fracture is in the most superior part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it must be adjusted with the end of a rod, which should be of small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then do likewise.
And try to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then approximately the seventh or tenth day you should rest the suppurating wound.
And approximately insert a piece of linen cloth into the end of the nose, if the fracture is in one part, or place two pieces, if the fracture is in two parts, and the thickness of the piece should be so great as to fill the nasal passage.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the pieces should be soaked in wine, and be changed every day, but I do not see the need for this.  Instead, I recommend that the piece be soaked in a beaten egg mixture with ground spice; and leave it in place until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a piece of cloth, a bird's feather is placed in the nose, wrapped in linen, if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is unable to breathe normally.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, use the pieces of cloth.
And if another time a boil appears on the nose, plaster it with a cerate or with cotton soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot boil does not appear, then you should plaster the exterior with chickpea flour and ground herbs mixed with beaten egg; and then place a linen bandage over it, and do not bind the nose with anything else.
But if the nasal bones are fractured, either a small fracture or comminuted, then you must cut away the pieces and remove them with appropriate instruments; and then close the wound, healing it with what promotes flesh and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if a boil occurs *inside* the nose, then you should treat it with pieces of cloth and administer lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, observe if the fracture is only outward, and not broken in two but bent inward, then its recognition is straightforward.
And thus, if the fracture is on the right side, you should introduce your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then introduce the aforementioned index finger of your right hand; and with that, push the protrusion of the fracture inward easily to the outer edges, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; with this, gently straighten it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to administer extension of both parts according to their alignment until its re-alignment is possible.
And if you encounter a displacement of teeth with a rupture, then bind those that you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; and then place a compress on the fractured jaw, and then place a doubled cloth upon it, and place a large, properly made bandage, or a piece of linen the length of the jaw over it; and then bind that above it according to your skill in bandaging and the appropriate application, until it is not dislodged.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him food that is easily digestible.
And if you estimate that the shape has been altered in any way, then you should remove and loosen it on the third day; and then correct what has been altered in it and plaster it with ground chalk, with egg white, or with sifted flour, after you have removed the compress, and place a soft wool plaster over it.
And then, when that plaster is securely fixed in place, and the bone's position is not altered, leave it, do not remove it until the fracture is healed and tight; for often this fracture is tightened within three weeks.
And if, another time, a harmful abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its treatment until it is healed, and the abscess is resolved.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then consider if it is a detached piece of bone or splinters; then you must carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for removing such splinters from the wound.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with leeches, according to the extent of your need.
