And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you should close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place one of the appropriate plasters over it and allow it to heal until it is sound.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Often the collarbone is fractured near the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it happens with splinters, and it is difficult to restore it; or the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds and steadies the fractured collarbone, and the other extends the arm towards that area.
And then gently mold the fracture with your fingers until it is in the correct shape, neither protruding nor sunken.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your needs; and extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you have aligned the fracture correctly.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone's ends outward, because it has been pushed inward, then the patient should be laid supine on their neck, and you should place a cushion of moderate size on the muscle of their neck.
And the assistant should press on the muscle below, until the collarbone bone is lifted out of the depression, and then you should straighten the fracture and mold it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and they are sharp, then it is necessary to break them and easily remove those splinters.
And if it is lodged within the bone, then you should make an incision there with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the collarbone the instrument that serves for the saw; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is its shape:
And it is similar to a scoop, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as much as possible for the operation; let it have two ends, as often, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound which you have incised when you have removed the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure of a hot abscess, then you should close the two edges of the wound with stitches.
And if the opening is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's cut.
And if a hot abscess arises, then soak a cloth in sour wine, and rubbed oil and wine, and place it over it.
And be careful that the splint does not move from the broken bone; therefore, you must see the patient daily. When the ligaments have softened, or you see the splint has moved, then you must readjust and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's bedding over their neck, and place a small cushion under their armpit near their bedding at night, so that their shoulder is raised away from the injury, thus elevating the fractured collarbone from the muscle, or keeping the patient's arm bound to their neck.
Do not loosen the bandage unless there is swelling or an abscess in the area within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you see the area needing it, and adjust the splint and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone is restored and has healed.
And it is strengthened and comfortable within twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade is fractured at the lateral side and it is not broken into pieces but just the edges.
And therefore, when the location of the fracture is broken - and it is broken in the middle of the bone - and it is not known except by touch, then, depending on the nature of the fracture, prepare the area and reduce it to its natural shape using every possible method.
Then place on the area powder of pounded chalk with egg white and a little lint; then place a double layer of cloth; and then place a thin, flat piece of wood (or possibly a sliver of table) over it, roughly the size of the shoulder blade or a bit larger.
But if beneath the wood there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that hollow with lint, and then raise the board to an even level.
And then press firmly from above with a long, appropriate constriction, and with firmness on the board, until it is no longer displaced from its position.
And check the ligament every day; and every time it is softened, bandage it again, and reposition the board if it has shifted from its place.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And therefore, when this number of days is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and be sure that the connection is to the bones, from which the breaking is not feared, nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the site of the heavy bone and place the splint beneath it, then you cut on it, remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the furcula, regarding the treatment of a boil or abscess if one arises there.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle, and that is bad; but its extremities are worse, because they are crushed and fragmented.
And the complications when it is broken in the middle are that it declines to the inner parts, causing great pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, severe weakness, and vomiting blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is apparent, not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of it is to lay the patient on their back, and place a cushion between their shoulders; and then press on the muscles, and adjust the bones with the hands on both sides, and be subtle in the operation and in the application according to any way possible and suitable, until the bone returns to its proper form.
And then apply a plaster and bandage and place a thin sheet of willow or rush or similar materials on top, wrapped in cloth; and be subtle in binding it over the broken bone so it is not moved; and tie it around the back in a circular fashion sometimes, and tighten it properly; and then check the bandage at all times, and when it has softened, tighten it further.
And if necessity provokes loosening due to corrosion, swelling, pain, or abscess that occurs in the area, then you should loosen it, remove the plaster, and correct whatever happened by applying a healing method; and then reapply the plaster, if necessary, and adjust the tightness until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of bones when they are broken.
Know that fractures of the ribs only occur in thick parts, meaning the middle; and on the extremities/ends of their front portions, damage occurs only because they are cartilaginous. And knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling through inquiry into it using words.
And the restoration should be such that the fracture is aligned with words (i.e., by using the appropriate method), until it is properly set, then apply a plaster and bind the broken bone with a splint, if needed.
And if the rib fracture is descending towards the internal parts, then the patient experiences intense pain and stabbing, similar to the pain experienced by someone with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood result; and this is a difficult condition to cure.
And the ancients were ingenious in dealing with this (condition) with many devices.
And from their observations, they said: It is necessary to withhold food from the patient that causes inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outward.
However, we should avoid this (withholding food) lest it be a trivial cause of generating a severe abscess, if it does not occur; and if it has already occurred, then it is helpful.
And some of them would say that it should be placed over a cupping glass and then suctioned forcefully; and it seems a reasonable approach, but nonetheless, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw the superfluities to the site due to the weakness (of the patient).
And some of them would say: the area should be covered with wool soaked in warm oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, and the dressing should be tightly bound around the curvature; and then the patient should be treated with a pleurisy-like treatment of diet and medicine.
And if a severe injury causes the patient extreme suffering, and the bone pierces the skin with a painful puncture, we are concerned for the patient; then, we must cut open the area, expose the fractured rib, and then place an instrument there to support the fractured section, a method of which has been preceded, and then cut the bone easily and remove it.
And then, if the wound is large, we should bring the two edges of the wound together with stitches, and treat it with poultices until it is healed.
And if an abscess arises inside, then you should mix powders in rubbed oil and place it on the affected area, and the patient should be treated with whatever is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Sometimes there are fractures in the bones of the neck, and sometimes they cause attrition, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if you repeatedly press their hands, they are now softened, numb, dead, and cannot be moved, stretched, or squeezed, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, they feel no pain, then know that they are not healed and in that condition, it is fatal.
And if you move them and feel constriction with your fingers and pricking, then know that the bone's integrity is preserved and that the patient can be cured with treatment.
And if you see something similar in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then if you see that the feet are also numb, as was observed in the hands, and when lying on their back, they experience involuntary windiness or urination, and sometimes they are lying on their back, wanting to urinate but unable, then know that it is fatal, and thus do not work on their cure.
But if no such symptoms appear in them, the condition is better and less severe.
And the treatment for this is to apply a soothing poultice, either of heated oil or crushed eggs, to the affected vertebra.  Apply it several times a day until the inflamed area subsides.
And then apply a comforting and stimulating plaster, and bind it tightly.  Instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and not to sleep on the affected area until healed.
And if you observe bone splintering or any other separated fragment, then it is necessary to expose the wound and remove the bone.  Then, bring the edges of the wound together, with sutures if necessary, and treat with a healing ointment until healed.
But if the tailbone is fractured, and the coccyx is affected, then you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the rectum, and align the fractured bone with the other hand as best as possible through repositioning; and then apply a plaster and bandage, if necessary, and tightly bind it.
And if you find fractured splinters there, then remove them, and treat the wound with the previous method until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the restoration of the width/broadness (or perhaps "the breadth").
The bones of the hips are often fractured, and if they break, the fracture is typically only a separation of their ends, and they are jagged along their length and move apart;  the patient experiences pain and throbbing at the site, and the leg becomes numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at its end, then you align it according to your equipment for alignment until it is brought back to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is along its length or has already moved apart, then you have the patient lie on their stomach until you are ready to repair that fracture. And then, once you have aligned it, apply a plaster, and then place a splint of wood or bone over it, and bind it tightly, so you do not fear further fracturing or dislodging the splint.
And align the concave part with whatever fills it until it takes the binding according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a festering abscess occurs, then you cease the extension and repair until the festering abscess is soothed, and apply what will soothe it as previously described; and then return to the repair, and bind it as is proper.
And if the bones splinter or are comminuted, then you must set them.
Chapter 11.
Of the repair of a fractured limb.
Adjutori" is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder joint.
And if it is broken, then its repair is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness in this manner:
And tie two ligatures at both ends, and then suspend it from a raised position.
And place the patient on a bed; and then place the patient's broken arm on the wood until the point of the arm is in the middle of the area where the curve of the wood is attached.
And then suspend something heavy over it, or the attendant stretches it downwards; and then the physician holds the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
The other method is that the patient is placed on their back, and the hand of their neck is suspended with a ligature; and then command two attendants that one of them holds what is above the fracture with their hands; and the other holds what is below it; and each of them stretches it in their direction.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then you tighten a ligature over the fracture and another ligature over that, and each of the two attendants stretches in their respective directions.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the extremity of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the ligature be placed on the point, and the other below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the ligature be placed over that spot and over that elbow.
Apply it easily and without effort, and then, once it is bound as necessary and applied with good application, tighten it if you do not want a festering boil.
But if a festering boil occurs, then leave the constriction until the seventh day, and place on it wool soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil until the boil subsides, and then tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a plaster over the fracture, then a bandage of fresh cloth over the plaster; then adjust the arm onto this support, and place their open hand over their muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the support on the arm so that the arm is in its proper place, if nothing about the broken bone prevents this.
And then use splints.
And you should place on the fracture that splint which is widest and strongest of the other splints; and place the width of a finger between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the break, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the constriction which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And make sure your constriction over the fracture area is tighter. And the longer the fracture, the smaller the constriction should be.
And therefore, adjust the positioning of the splints and the constriction as we have described earlier, when you are returning the limb to its proper position, then you do so.
And if you fear a suppurating abscess, then you leave the constriction and splints in place for seven days, as we have said.
And now consider the ligament in all its parts for three days, so that no swelling or inflammation occurs in the area; or, the member should be deprived of food to avoid the excess of the constriction; and thus correct all of this as we have said.
But if you are confident that no such cause will arise, then do not loosen the bandage until approximately three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands on their stomach.
And place a full, wool-stuffed pad beneath the injured area, and constantly during the night or day, ensure that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted or its ligaments weakened; therefore, remedy all of this with your skill.
And provide the patient with food according to the previous instructions, so that the food is light at first until the healing begins to strengthen; and then you should increase the amount of food.
Because it is customary for the injured area and leg to be bandaged for 40 days; and then they should be unbandaged, and a bath should be administered, and medicated with ointments that restore it.
And if the fracture is severely inflamed, then you do not loosen the ligaments and splints until after a few days or until two months have passed.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones; one of which is small, and is the one that sits beside the said elbow, and the other is large and is the one that is situated below the smaller bone, in the lower part.
And frequently the larger forearm bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, and frequently both are broken together.
And when the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm bone is broken below the fracture, its restoration is difficult, and its healing is difficult.
And even more difficult than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is appropriate for the physician, during its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease until it is healed.
But if the larger forearm bone is broken, then it requires a more forceful extension.
And if both forearm bones are broken together, then you must apply the pressure much more strongly.
And you must place the figure of the hand during the restoration and extension of it, laid out on a cushion, with the wrist of the hand positioned above the other fingers, the index finger being lower than the other fingers, and the patient resting on a smoothed-out board/surface.
And there should be a cushion in front of him, at the same height or level, so that the patient does not experience discomfort.
Then the assistant extends the lower part of the arm with their hand or a bandage, and another assistant extends the upper part similarly.
And then the physician manipulates the bone until it returns to the best possible position.
And if the fracture of the bone is complex, then you must exert your strength to return every piece to its proper place.
And if there appears a separated fragment that pierces the skin and you are not hopeful of restoring it, then you must operate on it and remove it by the method previously described.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter on this, and therefore take care of this healing.
And if a pus-filled abscess arises at the beginning of your treatment, then you should line a cloth with a plaster made of rosemary oil and white wax, with moderate thickness and subtlety, and firmly bind the cloth over it until the abscess subsides; then remove the plaster and apply a prepared plaster of ground powder mixed with egg white, and then apply splints; and let the splint placed on that fracture be thick and strong.
And know that the names of the splints for the arm are varied in the different arrangements, meaning a fracture in one or two segments at the same time.
And then apply your bandage to the site of the fracture more firmly and vigorously, and the more the bandage is drawn on the upper or lower parts of the fracture, the more softly you should apply the bandage, according to what is previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which they are bound should be of moderate thickness between thin and thick, as we have previously described.
And consider the limb and its ligaments for several days. And if any cause arises requiring correction, such as swelling affecting the limb, then you must bathe the limb in warm water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the bandage is already softened and the bone is already repositioned and similar to the others, then rectify everything with your skill.
And further consider if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, for then you must soften it slightly and leave it for several days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient experiences no such causes as we have described, then it is not necessary to loosen it for approximately 20 days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's hand should be suspended by its power of active movement, and let them sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or a very easy one is restored in 28 days, and all this depends on the constitution and the strength of the body.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of fractures of the wrist and fingers.
Know that the wrist and the joints of the fingers are often fractured, and not only by accidents, but also frequently by excessive friction.
And when the hand is fractured or injured through friction, the patient should be kept still, and a bandage should be applied evenly to the area.  Then the patient's hand should be placed on a similar extended position.
Then the attendant should straighten the broken bones, and the physician should hold them together until they are firmly rejoined.
And then a poultice and bandage should be applied, lest an abscess arise;  then place a splint over it, proportionate to the affected area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make a kind of sling out of cloth, and tell the patient to hold it tight with their fractured palm;  then wrap it firmly with a long piece of cloth.
And let the splint be of leather covered with cloth, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and hold it tight as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer parts, then you must place a splint over it, and another splint underneath on the palm of the hand, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply a bandage, winding the hand back towards the fingers, with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the fingers, if it is the thumb, it should be wrapped as needed and then held by the palm; and if you wish, put a small, straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the little finger, then it should be wrapped and bound to the adjacent finger; or all of them should be bound together sequentially, and this is better; or place a small splint on it, as we said about the thumb.
And consider, in your treatment, and after that, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose it — with your operation — with what is necessary when any of those causes occur, according to how the narrative of it is repeated.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is declared to be broken according to whether it is displaced forward and most severely.
And its treatment is done by tightening a bandage over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient should be laid on their stomach and on their face.
And then the attendant should extend the entire bandage to its proper position, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then let the bandage be close to the knee in the direction of the hip joint, so that the extension is upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is close to the knee, then let the bandage be near the knee, and the extension be downwards.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both his hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is properly joined; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, otherwise the limb is at risk of developing an abscess. But if an abscess develops, then you should leave it for a few days until the hot abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this: it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, downwards, and let the ends of the splint extend from the most prominent parts of both, then wrap the excess of the bandage around the leg and thigh.
And then place splints on the thigh, over the fractured area, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the empty spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is immobilized.
And then begin with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture area with three or four wraps, and the farther you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, the bandage should become less strong, lighter, and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the splint downwards until they reach the ankle, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage does not shift from its position.
And then leave the bandage on it, so that no swelling, abscess, inflammation or similar ailments occur on the limb; and if any of these occur, then remove it with care and correct whatever happened immediately, as we have said many times.
But if there is a bone fragment (piece of bone) that is sticking out, then you must try to push it back in as much as possible; and if not, cover it and leave it there, and treat it with whatever is appropriate to its prior condition until it is healed.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is tightened with splints, as we have said in the case of the arm.
However, in restoring it in this way, the patient does not experience limping; and if it is restored alone, or with the other leg applied to it, then it is necessary that the one that has always limped, limps again.
And know that the thigh is bandaged for 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all of this is according to the diversity of the constitutions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of a fractured kneecap (or patella).
Know that a fracture of the kneecap often involves, but frequently causes, rubbing.
But if it is a fracture, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a wound, and concerning this everything has been felt.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts is brought together with splints until it is joined and united as much as possible by adjustment, ease, and construction.
Then apply a plaster and place it over that round area, if needed; and bind it tightly with a suitable bandage.
Then consider all the treatments we have described in other fractures, just as for an abscess and similar conditions, so that any accident is opposed with what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the thigh bone; and the other is slender, and is called the fibula.
And the treatment for any of the fracture types that occur to the two bones of the arm is the same as the restoration of the arm, and the procedure for it is one and the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is displaced in all directions.
And if the more slender bone is broken, the leg is displaced forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find it below, and it's from those causes that are not hidden from you.
And then administer extension, reduction, and ligation of the splints, according to that same procedure.
However, if the fracture of the leg is extensive, having many lacerations, the extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate it with your skill.
And if the leg requires an operation on the arm, and once you have fitted the splints and completed your entire operation, then you will take pieces of wood – particularly those placed beneath the structures that have been placed on the bed, and made easily from palm branches.  Choose those of small thickness, and avoid any that are large, and make their lengths correspond to the length of the leg, from the knee down to the lowest point.
And then wrap each piece of cloth in two layers, according to its length, and place one over one side of the leg and the other over the other side, extending from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then tie the two splints in three places – at both ends and in the middle.  With this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to either the right or left side, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden brace, corresponding to the length of the leg, is placed on it so that the leg retains this position.
And this is especially necessary to do in cases of fracture, and when the limb is properly set.
And from now on, examine the leg daily, and closely monitor it, observing for any abscess, inflammation or other similar problems.  If any such issue arises, treat it accordingly until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the heel bone and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the heel bone does not occur frequently, however fractures of the foot bones occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes are rare, and the only issue that occurs in the toes is friction during movement.
And if the bones of the foot sustain a fracture, and the bones are sometimes displaced from their alignment, then the patient must place their foot on the ground in a manner similar to walking.
And then place one foot on that which is raised by those bones, and then press and squeeze them until they return to their proper places.
And then apply a plaster and bandage on top, and place a small board with two padded ends under the sole of the foot.
And then firmly bind that with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other materials as needed.
And after three or four days have passed, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones either squeezed together, or broken, or dislocated, and the procedure in these cases is as you know.
But if any fingers are broken, then restore them, and fix them in the same way as I have explained to you in the restoration of hand fingers. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and a little wider than the finger; and then place the aforementioned board under the foot, and bind it with a descending bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two, three, or more, then you place a splint on each finger of its appropriate size, wrapped in linen cloth, and bind the board to the lower part of the foot, and place one end of the board above the patient's abdomen, so that it holds the foot firmly.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes that have been discussed concerning other fractures and accidents; and be prepared for any accident with what is necessary.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone, the hip bone, and the penis of the male.
When the thigh bone of a woman is fractured, then make a splint from a smoothed board, and then slightly incline it towards the back, and keep it held in place behind.
Then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it should be placed in the thigh just as the leg is; and then the woman should be calmed, and gradually raise their back, for everything is held back until the fractured thigh bone returns to its proper alignment.
Then place pillows under their back, and when they want to urinate, the wool comes out easily, and they urinate. Then return it according to the manner in which you put it in at the beginning, and then put it back on its original pillow.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for that is when it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and insert a reed cannula into the wound; and insert the entire bladder into the thigh, and then blow into the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated inside the thigh, for the fracture will return.
And then fill it with wool and leave it for a few days as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and setting what we have recounted for the hip bone.
And that which is right is not hidden in these close and minor fractures, especially for him who has some study and use and understanding of this book of mine; for that fracture signifies to you in the most direct ways the right procedure in restoring and binding it, therefore understand well.
However, if a man's penis is broken, take a bird's wing, and place it on the penis; then put a bandage of cloth over it, wrap it, and leave it for three days or so, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones when affected by plague.
Convenient it is for one who has sustained a fracture with plague, especially if the bone is large, such as the bone of the thigh or the arm, or similar, that you should immediately bleed and scarify that part if the conditions for bloodletting or scarification, as we mentioned above, apply.
And if the plague flows blood, then it is necessary to apply a cautery to the retention of it, so that you powder it with crushed stone, if no other cause is present.
And then begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this if an abscess is not developing.
However, if a severe abscess develops, then you should leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days of all, for you will cause bad accidents.
However, if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary to gently reduce it and hold it in place with your hands, using little force.
However, if force isn't suitable to reduce it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight digits, and its width is according to the size of the injury; and for this reason, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them, depending on the quantity needed for the treatment of any type of fracture.
And it should be round, and have little thickness so that it is not bent by the pressure on it during the operation.
And it should have a sharp tip, with edges at its ends;  and the top of it should be thick and taper from the middle towards the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem,"  which is a small spatula.
Therefore, it is necessary that the sharp end be placed elevated over the prominent bone end.
And you push it (the instrument) in one go until the bone returns, and it is bound with some bandage.
And therefore, strive to bind the fractured ends together; and if the broken bone end is thin and does not accept the instrument favorably, then it is necessary for you to cut the end of that bone until the instrument has the ability to do so.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have described, then cut it with whatever surgical instruments we have mentioned; or saw it with a saw in whatever manner is possible for you.
And then remove whatever roughness and small splinters remain in the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and therefore do what you deem best to greatly help the patient.
When your bone restoration is complete, then soak a cloth in black astringent wine, and specifically if it is summer, and do not place a plaster or other oily substance on the wound, so that it does not cause swelling and corruption.
And after administering the styptics at the time of your bone restoration, leave the wound uncovered, so that you cut the opening with scissors and apply dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish doctors have done this, and caused death or a corrosive or festering illness in their patients, that is, gangrene. And if your constriction is slight, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear any of the harmful accidents we have mentioned, and you find pain in the area, then you should not apply styptics, but instead make a dressing of hard cloths in place of styptics and bind it tightly.
And when, after a day or two, the wound begins to suppurate, then you remove the cloth you had placed over it with wine, and then apply some ointments, such as those we customarily use to treat wounds, like tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ones.
And you should loosen the bandage and examine the wound each day morning and evening until it is healed and closed, and the limb should be prepared in such a way that the pus can drain from it to the lower parts with ease.
If the wound passes many days and is not healed or the pus has not been drained from it, then know that there are difficulties here that require you to investigate the wound with a probe. And of those difficulties, those that are separable, remove and extract them; those that are not separable and cause pain and swelling in the limb, force you to cut them open as you are able.
And if the wound is afflicted with a swelling or inflammation, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then you should apply to it one of those remedies that are appropriate for its cure, as described in the preceding chapter.
And in this, where my instruction ends and your skill begins: when a large bone is broken, support the limb in the same way as the thighbone and the supporting parts, and similar to those of large limbs, and do not attempt to pull or remove it, for often death results from this; leave it until it sets, for it will often heal by itself in about 20 or 30 days; and then treat the wound if you see fit, and if it does not heal, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of inflammation that occurs in the vicinity of any fracture.
Often this inflammation occurs in the area surrounding the healing of a fracture, especially if it is near a joint, and disfigures the shape of the limb; and it easily prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the inflammation is recent, and apply medicines that have a styptic effect, such as alum, vinegar, myrrh, ceruss, and similar things; you can use some or all of them and mix them with a styptic wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and apply it to the inflammation on a bandage, and tightly bind it around.
And leave it tightly bound for several days; and then loosen it and reapply another, until the inflammation is gone; or apply a lead lamina to it gently, for lead has the property of relieving any swelling in the limbs.
However, if the inflammation has become hard and compacted, and its removal is necessary, then carefully cut it out in its most prominent area, and remove excess tissue and the inflamed part, or use some rasps until it is gone; and treat the area until it heals.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains delicate and frail, not according to its natural strength.
When a fractured bone is repaired, and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, this is caused by several things:  one is an excessive loosening of the ligaments and the binding done incorrectly; or by excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing the nourishment from reaching the limb; or by excessive poulticing; or by unnecessary movement at the wrong time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the treatment for this is to feed the patient and make their body soft until the blood is replenished; and administer baths; and bring joy and happiness to them.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water compresses, until nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly rejoined and the operation is not performed correctly.
When, after the limb has been rejoined, there is still a crookedness and prominence in the broken bone and callus formation, and this is causing a deformed limb, but nonetheless the limb is not prevented from its natural operation, then you should not believe the claim of anyone who says the limb is newly broken.
But already there have been many foolish physicians and surgeons who have done this in our land; and this procedure is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear of a fatal outcome.
However, if the lameness and callus are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which softening herbs are steeped, such as elder leaves and roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and a softening poultice should be made, such as a well-made diachylon; or a mucilage should be taken from elder root and mixed with the yolk of an egg or with cedar oil, and the limb should be poulticed with this; or fatty figs should be crushed with pigeon dung, and similar medicaments are known as "consolidants".
And sometimes it is dissolved by persistent gentle rubbing with moist hands, and movement of the limb should be administered in every direction at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become firm, turned to stone, and needs to be treated with iron, then the pus must be drained, and the continuity of the bone must be dissolved, and what is superfluous of the callus from the bone must be cut away with sharp instruments, and care must be taken with diligence and study.
Chapter 23.
Of discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the shifting of any joint from its proper place, which prevents movement, and it distorts the shape of the limb, causing great pain and harm to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident causes a dislocation, it is essential to attempt reduction immediately, and do not delay the reduction, for if it is delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and very stiff, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And therefore, it should not be moved or stretched during the inflammation, for it often causes spasms and persistent pain.
But when this occurs, you should proceed with the bloodletting of the patient, and then let it rest until the inflammation subsides; then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and the reduction should be attempted with ease.
And every limb should be treated in this way, so that the afflicted part returns to its proper position.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what precedes in fractures; from the most superior part of the body down to the lowermost part.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations of the lower jaw.
So the two jaws are dislocated, if not quickly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are displaced slightly from their places and somewhat softened; or they are completely and thoroughly dislocated, until they are softened at the joint, causing the patient to drool and unable to keep the mouth shut, and the patient's tongue is wrapped around its own mouth.
However, when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns on its own with little effort.
However, if the dislocation is complete and thorough, then a gentle reduction must be administered promptly, and it should not be delayed at all.
And it is necessary that the attendant hold the patient's head, and the doctor insert the fingertip of one hand into the site of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hand should be outside for support; and he should instruct the patient to stop moving their jaws and force them to go in opposite directions; and the doctor pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then apply steam with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both is easy and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place over them cloths soaked in cerot (a mixture of wax and oil); and it should then be bound with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be supported between two pillows so that it does not tilt to either the right or the left; and do not force them to chew anything; until the pain subsides and their jaws are healed, then let them eat whatever they wish.
And this should be administered easily, and do not exert yourself in opening the mouth for eating, drinking, and breathing, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws, when dislocated at any time and not returned to their place, then many times fevers and persistent sweating occur, and the patient's stomach is very easily upset, and there is a lot of vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and often the one who experiences this dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from the inside by its connection with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident through the feeling.
And the restoration is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then press the area with your palm with a strong pressure, for it will return.
And then place a plaster and powders over that area and press it down.
However, the extremity that is connected to the elbow and continues with it is not dislocated on any given day; therefore, it must be reduced and mended according to what we have said and according to what is in your care.
And then apply a plaster, and powders, and a splint, and command the patient to adhere to quietness and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done more slowly; and it is not dislocated to the rear by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and comes out from the lower parts toward the upper part, and more often in those with little flesh it is easily dislocated; but in those with a lot of flesh, it is forced out with difficulty.
And often, men are struck or injured by accident, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is considered to be dislocated because of this.
Therefore, you should ensure that you have full control of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is in the lower parts toward the top; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find a concavity at the head of the muscle and below the top, toward the point of contact of the head of the muscle, much like an egg, and the patient cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor move it freely in any way.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a clear point of contact, and it is not hidden.
And a gentle reduction of this dislocation is possible when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the physician raises the patient's hand towards the upper parts; and then places their fingers below the patient's armpit, and elevates the joint with strength back into its place, and the physician raises the patient's hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it down as it returns easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a long time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient must be bathed in warm water and be given an application that softens and loosens it, as if it were crushed roots of marsh-mallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and should be administered.
And then the patient should be placed on their back, and a layer of wool that is neither too loose nor too tight should be placed beneath their armpit, and the physician places their heel on the sphere, and elevates the muscle's head with force, and pulls the patient's hand so it is not displaced downwards, and another physician holds the patient's head so it is not displaced downwards, for this will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this way: have a man present who is taller than the patient, standing on the affected side, and he inserts his muscle under the patient's armpit and lifts the patient's armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another physician pulls the patient's hand from below their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then something else must be suspended to add weight, for this will cause the dislocation to return quickly.
And it is easily reduced in another way: a long wooden post is placed in the ground, its top rounded like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, and placed under the patient.  Then, over the top of the post, heavy blankets are placed; the patient stands along the length of the post, and their hand is stretched downwards on the opposite side, and the patient's body is stretched on the opposite side with force, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult by all the methods we have described, then use this treatment: take a wooden post that is about two arms long and four fingers wide, and its thickness should be moderate at the armpit area in this manner:
And then, over the rounded top, heavy blankets should be tied so that the post does not injure the patient.
Then, a support should be placed under the patient's armpit, and the arm or hand should be stretched along the post, down to the lower parts, and the post should be tied to the support and arm and hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a ladder-like support according to its width, and the hand stretched downwards, and the other side of the body suspended on the other side, so that the joint enters quickly/immediately.
And when its entry is completed in whatever way possible for you, then you should place under the armpit a wool pad of suitable thickness; and then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder with incense and egg white over all the muscle, spread evenly over it, and then bind tightly under the armpit with a suitable bandage; the bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the patient's hands should be tied to their neck, and left undisturbed, and the hands should not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be light, until the limb is strengthened, for this is more conducive to its recovery.
And then the food should be increased to a moderate amount, after seven or five days, and movement should be encouraged; and thus if it is healed and not softened, then it is already cured.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity affecting it or another cause, then a cautery with three openings should be administered, as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
And if all this is done, and after seven days the bandage and the joint are not healed, and the plaster and the binding are removed several times, and it is not healed and stiff and cannot raise that arm up, then know that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are now severed or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be placed back in its proper position.
For the dislocation of the foot and the nipple or the posterior parts, the reduction is done by pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns to its place.
And another binding should be applied to that area, and it should heal until it is cured.
And if after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths repeatedly until that heaviness is lessened and it returns to its original state.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so in the front to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is noticeable from pressure or from touch, according to what shape it has been dislocated. And when you adjust the dislocated elbow with your hand, this is clearly apparent to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch the muscle.
And you should act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes a hot abscess, for if it becomes a hot abscess, the reduction is difficult, and the limb is not completely healed; and especially when it is in the back parts, for it is the worst of all other types of dislocation, and causes greater pain, and often leads to death.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's arm is extended with both hands, and their arm is extended, and the physician's hands are on the elbow and beneath it, and he presses the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the heel of his palm until it returns to its place.
However, if the dislocation is on the front parts, then it returns by bending the arm with their hand, until he strikes the elbow with the heel of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer forceful extension and strong traction.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold that extension so that it is not pulled back from the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in all directions, since it is wrapped with a long folded cloth over their hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with their hand, anoint it with some oil so that these things aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with a forceful thrust until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary to place it on a compress in which there is styptic and binding with egg white, and to be bandaged tightly downwards, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and to be left for some days.
And then it should be loosened; and therefore, if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well tightened, then you repeat the poultice and the bandage, and leave it for some days until it is tightened; and then loosen it.
But if there is a worsening of the situation after the joint reduction, and the condition is moving, then apply moistening through bathing and gentle friction and compression, until it is relieved; or place on the joint soothing material, that is, something of a fatty nature; and then bandage it, and leave it bandaged on the limb day and night. And then remove that, and put the limb in a bath; and when it is soaked, then you rub the joint with gentle friction, and then repeat the soothing material, that is, something smooth and soft, once, and then a second time, and a third time. with the application of the bath, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place a heated, moist cow's hide over the affected area and press firmly on it; and do this several times, for this is moist and will restore it to its proper position.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of a dislocation of the shoulder, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
A dislocation of the shoulder sometimes occurs, and the reduction of its dislocation is relatively easy; the opposite is true of other joints, but nonetheless speed is essential in its reduction when it is dislocated, before the area becomes inflamed or a harmful abscess develops.
And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the patient's arm joint on a table, and the assistant extends the arm, and the physician places his hand, or his palm, over the area of the joint, and pushes until it returns to its proper place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already been turned towards the front of the hand, then the patient should place the back of their hand on a table during the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of the hand of the patient should be strategically on the table, so that the physician's hand can be applied to that area of the joint.
And so if it returns quickly, it is good; and if not, then firmly hold it while avoiding worsening the inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, it will not tolerate nor will you succeed in its reduction after several days have passed.
However, the joint remains according to its distortion; and if the patient reports no cause, but that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then know that the nerves are severed or damaged, and in that case, there is no remedy except to hold it with a cautery near the wound and firmly apply this, which may provide some benefit.
Therefore, once the injury has been reduced, apply the poultice that we have described, and let it be left and held for five days; and then it should be removed and left, and the patient's hand should be exercised.
But if the movement is hindered and some heaviness appears, then alleviate it with warm water and some friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various directions.
And when any of those fingers is dislocated towards the palm side or the underside of it, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation away with your fingers, until it returns to its place.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the side to which it was dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly aligned in its place.
And when it is night, bandage it according to that narrative, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bandage it at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And do the same if it is dislocated towards the underside of the hand, and bandage it to that side and do your first operation on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts of the body.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.
When an accident causes a complete dislocation of any of the vertebrae in the back or neck, and the displaced vertebrae remain out of place, then there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's bowel movements are involuntary and uncontrolled; and often some of his limbs, or both legs, or both arms, or one of them, are paralyzed; or a vertebra has been displaced, for it is often displaced, and frequently the removal of these parts is made in four directions.
Therefore, this [dislocation] has already moved to the posterior parts and is called "gelbozitat" (likely a medical term referring to a specific type of spinal dislocation or injury).
However, its treatment is to consider whether the "gelbozitat" [spinal dislocation] occurred in childhood, because in that case there is no cure, nor is there a complete recovery.
But in cases of injury caused by a fall, a blow, or similar events, the Ancients described numerous treatment methods, the most beneficial of which I will briefly summarize, omitting the unnecessary details to keep my explanation concise and focused on the specific points of my instruction.  I've presented and organized this information using various tools with which I will demonstrate and clarify it.
And only those dislocations occurring in the back, specifically as described by me, can be treated.
And it is necessary that the patient be laid on their face, propped up against a wall, and cushioned underneath to prevent the foot from being injured.
And then a straight piece of wood should be placed, driven into the ground at the edge of the support, around the head.  Another piece of wood should be positioned similarly at the opposite end of the support to hold the patient's feet.
And one attendant should hold the first piece of wood, making sure it doesn't dig into the ground; and another attendant should hold the other piece in the same manner.
Then, wrap a bandage around the patient's head and under the temples firmly; and extend the bandage end to the wood which is near the head, securing it there.  Then, tighten another bandage around the patient's hips, thighs, and lower legs; and finally, secure all the bandages to the other piece of wood near the patient's feet.
And then each attendant should extend the bandage to the wood, ensuring the two pieces of wood and bandages remain in their positions, however, do not bind them so tightly as we have described.
And the physician places their palms on the spine with strength until it returns to its proper place, or places a table underneath it, and then adjusts their feet on the table until it returns to its proper place.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a board whose width or length is approximately three arms' lengths, and excavate a place in the wall, near the patient, into which the end of the board will fit. And then place half of the board over the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is straightened and returns to its place.
And if you wish, turn the apparatus that is held by hand, and place two pieces of wood, each one arm's length long, in the ground behind the patient's head, and similarly two pieces of wood behind their feet; and the space between each piece of wood should be one palm's width.  A hole for the apparatus to fit should be made in each piece of wood, and each piece must be firmly planted in the ground so that it does not move.
And a round piece of wood, which the apparatus is on, should be rotated in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its [the round piece of wood's] end is a hole in which the apparatus is fitted, the length of which [the apparatus] is a palm plus whatever is rotated, and the other two pieces of wood have a similar hole.
And then tighten the bandages you have applied to the patient's feet in the hole near the head, and the bandages on their thighs in the apparatus near their feet.
And near each apparatus, let a helper turn the apparatus with their hand, while the physician applies the splint as we have described.
And then, once the splint has been applied and the area is secured, place a dry poultice with egg white, then linen; then place a thin wooden board, whose width is about three fingers and its length sufficient to cover the affected area of the joint, over the poultice, and on the splint's part.
Then bind it with the bandage we have mentioned, and let the patient consume fine nourishment until healed.
And if any lingering inflammation remains in the area after healing, then medicines that soften and soothe, administered with a long-term regimen as we have described, should be given; and a lead lamina should already have been administered to that area.
And sometimes inflammation occurs at the back of the spinal vertebrae, caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone that is now inflamed, so that no presumption should be made regarding it with this medicine, for it will easily cause death.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint should only be dislocated, and that what happens to other joints with minor displacement and concavity does not happen to them.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inward, or outward, or forward, or backward.
And it is mostly dislocated inward, and less so forward or backward.
A sign of an inward dislocation is that when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected leg, the affected leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their affected foot towards their groin.  The area where the groin should be is inflamed and painful from a hot abscess, because the head of the thighbone has moved there.
The sign of a dislocation outward is that the symptoms are the opposite of this inward dislocation.
The sign of a forward dislocation is that the patient extends their leg to its full extent, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if pressed forward it cannot be moved further in that direction; and their urine is retained and their groin is inflamed, and they walk on the ball of their foot.
The sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can it be bent forward to reach the groin, and the affected leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is swollen, and the head of the thighbone is in that area.
But the reduction of these types of dislocation is that you must consider, then, if the dislocation is old and chronic, that it has already become accustomed to that position, then your reduction will only go so far, and then it is not a complete cure; and therefore, no premature assumptions should be made in this case.
However, in the case of a recent dislocation, one of the four types, you should carefully attempt to reposition the joint, extending it inward and outward, and moving it to the right and left, for it will likely return easily and require no further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either by the patient's own hands, or with a bandage tied around the leg above the knee; and another assistant should hold the upper hand in such a way that their hands are below the patient's armpits; and then bind tightly with a bandage around the root of the thigh, and let a third assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be either from the inside of the groin to the area of the pubic bone, or from the back to the area of the buttocks.
And let the extension be performed in all these ways until the patient is lifted off the ground with their own body weight and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is a common procedure for all four [types of dislocation].
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then for every type it is necessary to proceed with the specific treatment.
In the proper reduction of a dislocation, when the dislocation is inward, the patient should be placed supine on their right side; then apply bandages over the root of the thigh, at the point between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin; and then extend the bandage over the groin area to the highest part of the pubic bone.
Then another strong assistant should take the patient's arms, and firmly grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outward with vigorous extension, for this will return it to its place.
And this type is easier to treat, with this limb being reduced more easily than other types.
But if you are excused and do not comply with this type of treatment, then it is necessary that the patient's two feet be bound together with strong, light bandages, on both ankles and both heels.
And the length of each bandage should be about four fingers long, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by about two fingers.
Then the patient should be suspended upside down from the top of a post, in the house; and the distance from the ground should be about two arms' lengths.
Then order a strong young man to grasp the upper thigh, and let another young man hold the patient, and the other young man, pressing with strength on the thigh, will push, so that the joint returns to its place quickly.
However, in proper reduction, when there is a dislocation to the outside, the patient must lie on a cushion, as we have previously described in the chapter on sprains; and the bandage must be tightened on the affected leg and on the patient's feet.
And then two posts should be placed, one at the patient's feet, and another at the head; and then posts fitted together in the middle of the hill on which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the posts are between the patient's thighs, to prevent pulling from the extension below.
And then each attendant should extend their portion, and the physician with their hand guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the forward parts, then the leg must be gently extended, and it is according to this same procedure on a hill; and the physician places the palm of their right hand on the affected limb, and then with the other hand, compresses that, and with that compression, positions that which is extended downwards to the front of the dislocation.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then the patient should not be extended downwards, but raised above the ground; instead, they must be placed on a hard surface, just as is necessary for traction if their shoulder is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about their lying on two pillows; and it is on their side and the ligaments are stretched, as we have said, not much.
And pressure should be applied with a bandage to the area where the joint is.
Therefore, once the reduction is completed as is necessary – and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those together and the patient grips their leg and expands it without excuse, then know that the limb has been returned as it should be – and then you bring the two thighs together and place a poultice and bind tightly so that the shoulder is not moved in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then remove the bandage and poultice, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of swelling, then you return and apply a plaster, and bind it according to your initial binding, and leave it for three days.
And then remove it, and have the patient walk slowly for some days, until they are stronger.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of a dislocated knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outward, and inward, and downward (or behind), but not forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you tell the patient to straighten their leg at the hip; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these ways is that you have the patient lie down straight, so that they extend their leg as much as possible; place your leg against theirs, and put their foot between your feet; then, firmly hold the patient's knee with your palms, and place your fingers around their knee with strength. Another assistant should extend the leg until the knee returns to its place.
And a sign of its return/reduction is that their leg will straighten at the hip easily, without strenuous effort.
Then, bandage that area and bind the leg to the hip with a bandage for three or four days, then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for a few days, until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is beyond your ability, then administer strong traction with the ligaments, as previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes displaced by a small movement, and sometimes it is dislocated according to its alignment, and the dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the heel swells on the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of the small displacement is a gentle return, and it is that it be extended easily with the hands and be held until it returns to its place.
However, in the treatment of it when it is dislocated according to its alignment, it is necessary to have the patient lie straight, and let a strong and able assistant stand behind the patient's heel in the middle of it.
Then, you, with your right hand, take the foot from the uppermost part, and with your left hand, take the lower part of the foot at the heel bone's location.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left twice, and then push with the left; and push the foot's heel with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the foot's heel against the leg a third time, and you are at the heel.
Therefore, if it returns in one or two attempts using this method, and if the foot is now aligned properly, it is good; and if not, then repeat the procedure, for it [the foot/condition] returns [to its original state].
However, if you are prevented from reducing it with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and firmly place a thick cloth on the ground, and position it between their thighs, and you have already draped a cloth over them so that they are not harmed.
And then, a servant of his holds his thighs, and another servant extends the leg with his hands or a bandage tied around the foot; and then each servant extends against the extension of their partner, and the cloth remains between the patient's thighs holding it, so that his body is not pulled to the lower parts against its extension.
And then the physician aligns the dislocation with his hands, and another servant holds the healthy thigh at the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And so, when the dislocation returns and you are certain of the successful reduction, then apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with bandages.
And you should be mindful of the nerves that are behind the heel, so that the bandage on it is not too tight and harms it severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is softened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from movement for 40 days. However, if he is forced to move before that period, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dislodged and damaged, and therefore may not receive a cure.
And if a painful abscess occurs, then you must apply what was previously discussed in our narrative, in another location for treatment and application, until it is resolved.
Chapter 34.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
It is necessary that the dislocated part be gently pulled back without effort; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
However, if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, then it is necessary to have the patient place their foot on a soft surface, like the ground or a table, and be positioned as if they were walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on whatever part of the joints is protruding, and then press that with your foot with gentle force until it returns, and you see that it is now positioned and no longer appears to be protruding.
Then place a board underneath the sole of the foot that covers the entire foot, with two supports, and tightly secure it with proper and firm straps for three days; and then loosen these and allow rest from walking for several days, until the injury is healed and the restoration is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations that are caused by a bruise, or a fracture, or by both together.
When any of those causes occur, and you exert yourself in the treatment and restoration, often death ensues.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for anyone to presume to treat such a patient, but only those knowledgeable in the art, having long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, neither rash nor presumptuous.  And let them use medicines in the beginning that only slightly reduce inflammation, and leave the patient to the divine judgment, except for one who has hope of healing from death, with an alleviation of the illness, and in whom some hope appears. Then strive as quickly as possible to perform the reduction at the onset of the cause, before it becomes a severe abscess.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that reduces inflammation and heals the bruise with whatever drying ointments are appropriate for it.
And if there is a dislocation with a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must set them, and do with them what we have said about simple ailments of those things that precede in those locations.
And, according to your ability, guard and avoid the dangers in the paths of fear, as my teachings precede you; for this is more beneficial to you from your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and better for your true body during your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
